The Argument from Divine Will and Its Refutation
Presents and refutes determinist argument from God's will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that God's will determines human actions and its two-fold refutation in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
God's will does not force human actions; possibilities remain open.

This passage presents a key theological argument used to deny human free will: that because God's will must either will an action or its opposite, the action is inevitable. The author then refutes this argument by challenging its assumptions. Let's examine the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Identify the claim that God's will must be for either occurrence or non-occurrence, and how this leads to determinism.
Notice the analogy with divine knowledge.
2
Understand the first refutation
The first refutation denies the exclusivity: God may not will either side, leaving room for human choice.
3
Understand the second refutation
The second refutation denies that what God wills from the servant must occur, as will be explained later in Ash'ari doctrine.
This distinguishes between will and compulsion.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how these refutations preserve human agency while affirming divine will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (وأما التمسك) (وأما التمسك بأن مراده تعالى إما الوقوع أو اللاوقوع، فرد بتجويز أن لا يريد أحدهما وأن يقع خلاف مراده). كما استدل على وجوب الفعل أو الترك بتعلق العلم، فكذا يتعلق الإرادة وتقريره أن فعل العبد ما أن يريد الله تعالى وقوعه فيجب أو لا وقوعه فيمتنع ، فلا يكون باختيار العبد ، ورد أولاً بمنع الحصر لجواز أن لا تتعلق إرادة الله تعالى بشيء من طرفي الفعل والترك . وثانياً: بمنع وجوب وقوع ما أراده الله تعالى من العبد على ما هو المذهب عندهم كما سيجيء .
He said: (And as for clinging to [the argument]) (And as for clinging to [the argument] that His intention, exalted be He, is either occurrence or non-occurrence, it is refuted by allowing that He may intend neither one and that what is contrary to His intention occurs.) Just as one argued for the necessity of the action or abandonment by the attachment of knowledge, so too does the will attach itself, and its formulation is that the action of the servant is [such that] if God intends its occurrence it becomes necessary, or if He does not intend its occurrence it becomes impossible, so it is not by the servant's choice. It is refuted first by denying the exclusivity because it is possible that God's will does not attach to either side of action or abandonment. And second: by denying the necessity of the occurrence of what God intended from the servant according to their doctrine, as will come later.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes an argument that seeks to prove that human actions are not voluntary because they are determined by God's will. The argument claims that God's will either wills the action (so it must occur) or wills its non-occurrence (so it is impossible), leaving no room for human choice. The refutation is twofold: First, it denies the assumption that God's will must attach to one of the two options; He may not will either, allowing human choice. Second, it denies the necessity that what God wills from the servant must occur, as this is not the agreed doctrine (likely referring to the Ash'ari position that God's will does not compel the action but creates it, yet human acquisition is involved). The passage ends by noting that this will be discussed further.

Key Terms
إرادة
تعلق الإرادة
الوقوع
اللاوقوع
فعل العبد
الحصر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's will always necessitates human action, leaving no room for free will.
Correction: God's will may not attach to either option, and even if it does, it does not eliminate human agency; the Ash'ari doctrine of kasb preserves human choice.

Checkpoint
What is the first response to the argument that God's will must be for either occurrence or non-occurrence?
A) It denies that God's will applies to actions.
B) It allows that God may not will either side.
C) It affirms that God's will always brings about its object.
D) It claims human will overrides God's will.

Key Takeaway
The argument from divine will is refuted by showing that God's will may not attach to either option, and even if it does, it does not eliminate human agency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your daily choices, do you genuinely believe that God's will leaves room for your own responsibility, or do you sometimes use divine will as an excuse to avoid accountability?
?
If God's will does not force your actions, then on the Day of Judgment you will be held fully accountable. Are you living today as if that is true, or as if your actions are predetermined?
?
The refutation shows that even if God wills something from you, your agency remains intact. When you have sinned, have you blamed God's will, or have you taken full ownership of your action and repented?

The Fourth Proof: Superiority of the Servant's Actions
Fourth proof with objection and refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth proof that the servant does not create his own actions, and how the objection regarding the superiority of power over faith is refuted.

Core Idea
If the servant's good deeds are better than God's creations, then his creating them would make him superior to God—an absurdity.

This passage presents a powerful theological argument: the servant's actions (faith, obedience) are more noble than many of God's creations (bodies, devils). If the servant were the creator of his actions, he would surpass God in excellence. An objection claims that the power to do faith is better than faith itself, but the author shows this leads to a worse conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof
Read the Arabic and translation of the main proof. Notice the comparison between the servant's good deeds and God's created things.
The proof relies on the obvious superiority of faith over created objects.
2
Examine the objection
The objection shifts the comparison to the power over faith. Consider why someone might think the power is better than the action.
The objector argues that power is necessary for faith and thus superior.
3
Analyze the refutation
The author uses a reductio ad absurdum: if the objection holds, then power over evil would be worse than disbelief, which is absurd. This exposes the flaw in the objection.
The refutation preserves the original proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن من فعل العبد الإيمان والطاعات وكثير من الحسنات ومن خلق الله تعالى الأجسام والأعراض والشياطين وكثير من المؤذيات، ولا شك أن الأول أحسن من الثاني وأشرف ، فلو كان العبد خالقاً لفعله لكان أحسن وأشرف من الله تعالى خلقاً وإصلاحاً وإرشاداً. فإن قيل : القدرة على الإيمان أحسن وأوضح وأصلح من الإيمان لتوقفه عليها بخلق الله تعالى . قلنا : فيلزم أن تكون القدرة على الشر والتمكن منه شراً من الكفر وأقبح منه.
And among them is that the servant's actions include faith, obedience, and many good deeds, while God's creation includes bodies, accidents, devils, and many harmful things; there is no doubt that the first is better and more noble than the second. So if the servant were the creator of his action, he would be better and more noble than God Almighty in creating, reforming, and guiding. If it is said: The power over faith is better, clearer, and more beneficial than faith because faith depends on it through God's creation. We say: Then it would follow that power over evil and ability to commit it would be worse than disbelief and more ugly.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof. The servant's actions (like faith, obedience, good deeds) are more excellent than many of God's created things (like bodies, accidents, devils, harmful things). If the servant were truly the creator of his actions, he would be more excellent than God in terms of creation and guidance—a clear absurdity. An objection is raised: the power to do faith is actually better than faith itself, because faith depends on that power. The author replies by a reductio: if that were true, then the power to do evil would be worse than disbelief itself, which is unacceptable. Hence the objection fails.

Key Terms
إيمان
طاعات
حسنات
خلق
أحسن
أشرف
قدرة
شر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection that power over faith is better than faith itself is a valid counter-argument.
Correction: The author shows that this reasoning leads to the absurd conclusion that power over evil is worse than disbelief, so the objection is self-defeating.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the objection that power over faith is better than faith itself?
A) By pointing out that the same would apply to power over evil, which is absurd.
B) By denying that faith is better.
C) By saying power is not real.
D) By stating that God does not give power for evil.

Key Takeaway
The fourth proof shows that if the servant created his actions, he would be superior to God, which is false; the objection is refuted by a reductio ad absurdum regarding power over evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a good deed, do you ever catch yourself feeling a sense of ownership or pride as if it originated from you? How does this proof shake that feeling, and what would it mean for you to truly attribute every good act to God alone?
?
The objection in this passage says the power to do good is better than the good deed itself. In your life, do you value the ability to do good more than the actual good you do? How does the refutation about power over evil expose a dangerous pride in your own capabilities?
?
If you truly believed that your actions are not your own creation, would you approach your next sin differently? Would you see it as a failure of gratitude rather than a simple mistake? What would you say to God tonight about the good and evil you committed today, knowing He is the ultimate source of all power?

The Sixth Proof: Gratitude for Faith Indicates Its Creation
Sixth proof: obligation to thank for faith shows it is God's creation; objection and refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the consensus on thanking God for faith itself proves that faith is created by God, and how the objection about blame is refuted.

Core Idea
Thanking God for faith proves He created it; His sovereignty exempts Him from blame.

The sixth proof draws on a simple but powerful observation: all Muslims agree that we must thank God for the blessing of faith itself. But gratitude is only due for something directly given. If faith came from the servant alone, thanking God for it would not be proper. This passage explores this argument and answers a key objection about blame for disbelief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: a claim (consensus), an implication (gratitude), an objection, and a response.
The passage contains a footnote marker (2) which is incidental.
2
Understand the ground of consensus
Identify why the unanimous obligation to thank God for faith is significant. The author argues that this implies faith is God's creation and gift, even if the servant's acquisition plays a role.
Gratitude is for the very essence of faith, not its prerequisites.
3
Analyze the objection
Consider the objection: if God merits praise for creating faith, He would also merit blame for creating disbelief. Why might this seem logical? Note that the objection assumes symmetry.
The objection is a common challenge to the Ash'ari position.
4
Examine the refutation
Study the author's response: God's 'conduct' is to be praised for good and blessings, but not blamed for evil because He is the absolute Owner. The concept of 'blame' does not apply to the Sovereign.
Reflect on how this connects to the attribute of divine sovereignty (al-mulk).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن(۲) الأمة مجمعون على صحة ذلك بل وجوب حمد الله وشكره على نعمة الإيمان نفسه، ذلك إلا إذا كان بخلقه وإعطائه وإن كان لكسب العبد مدخل فيه، فأما الشكر على مقدماته من الإقدار والتمكين والتوفيق والتعريف ونحو ذلك فشيء آخر فإن قيل : لو استحق بخلق الإيمان المدح لاستحق بخلق الكفر الذم . قلنا : ممنوع فإن من شأنه استحقاق المدح والشكر بخلق الحسنات، وإيصال النعم لا الذم بخلق القبائح وإرسال النقم، لأنه المالك فله الأمر كله، لا يقبح منه خلق القبيح
And among them is that the community is agreed upon the validity of that, rather the obligation of praising God and thanking Him for the blessing of faith itself; that is only if it is by His creation and giving, even if the servant's acquisition has an entry in it. As for thanking for its prerequisites—empowerment, enablement, success, definition, and the like—that is another matter. If it is said: if He merits praise for creating faith, He would merit blame for creating disbelief. We say: It is prevented, for His conduct is to merit praise and thanks for creating good deeds and conveying blessings, not blame for creating ugly deeds and sending calamities, because He is the Owner, so to Him belongs the entire command; it is not unbecoming of Him to create the ugly.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth proof. The community unanimously agrees that it is valid, rather obligatory, to thank God for the blessing of faith itself. This implies that faith is a direct gift from God, created by Him, even if the servant's acquisition (kasb) has some role. Gratitude for the prerequisites of faith (empowerment, enablement, etc.) is a separate matter. An objection arises: if God merits praise for creating faith, He would also merit blame for creating disbelief. The author answers by denying this, explaining that God's nature is such that He deserves praise for creating good and blessings, but not blame for creating evil and calamities, because He is the absolute Owner. He may do as He wills, and it is not improper for Him to create the ugly (qabih) because He is the sovereign. This argument upholds that faith is directly from God while defending God from any imputation of injustice.

Key Terms
حمد
شكر
نعمة الإيمان
خلق
كسب
مدح
ذم
قبيح
مالك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God creates all actions, then He must be blamed for causing disbelief.
Correction: God is the absolute Owner; the categories of blame and praise used for creatures do not apply to Him. His wisdom and justice are beyond question; He is not subject to human standards.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the objection that if God deserves praise for creating faith, He would deserve blame for creating disbelief?
A) By asserting that God is the Owner and does what He wills without being blamed.
B) By denying that God creates disbelief.
C) By saying that disbelief is not blameworthy.
D) By stating that the servant creates disbelief.

Key Takeaway
The unanimous obligation to thank God for faith demonstrates it is His creation, and His sovereignty absolves Him from blame for creating disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'alhamdulillah for Iman', do you truly feel that your faith is a direct gift from God at this moment, or do you subconsciously think it is your own achievement? What would change in your daily struggle if you fully believed every pulse of belief is His creation?
?
If God is not blameworthy for creating disbelief, how does that challenge your natural anger or judgment towards those who disbelieve? Does this truth make you more humble or more distant?
?
You thank God for health, wealth, and safety, but do you thank Him for your faith with the same intensity? If faith is the greatest blessing, why does your gratitude for it often feel mechanical or absent? What would it look like to thank Him for it right now, in this moment?

Third Proof: Verses Explicitly Using 'Khalq'
Presents the third proof using Quranic verses with 'khalq' and analyzes grammatical conditions for exclusivity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Quranic verses that explicitly mention 'creation' (khalq) serve as a proof for Allah's exclusive creatorship, and how their grammatical structure affects their evidentiary strength.

Core Idea
The word 'khalq' alone isn't enough; grammar determines if the verse proves exclusive creation.

This passage presents the third proof in a series, citing several Quranic verses that contain the word 'creation' (khalq). However, the author notes that their indication is not straightforward; it requires careful grammatical analysis, especially for the key verse 'Huwa Allah al-Khaliq'. This lesson will walk through the verses and the subtle grammatical points that determine whether they prove that only Allah creates.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins with a list of verses.
2
Identify the six verses and the subtlety note
Identify the six Quranic verses cited. Then note the author's comment that their indication has some probability and subtlety, which is why he treats them as a separate type of proof.
3
Focus on the grammatical analysis of 'Huwa Allah al-Khaliq'
The author explains that exclusive creatorship is only implied if 'al-Khaliq' is a predicate (khabar) and 'Huwa' is either a pronoun of the matter (ḍamīr al-sha'n) or an ambiguous pronoun (ḍamīr mubham). If 'al-Khaliq' is an adjective (ṣifa), the exclusivity is not proven because 'Allah' is a proper noun indicating a specific essence.
This grammatical nuance is the key to understanding the proof.
4
Reflect on the significance of grammar in theological proofs
Consider why the author dedicates attention to this grammatical point: the strength of a proof depends on precise linguistic analysis.
This connects to the broader method of kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدليل الثالث
قال ومنها قوله تعالى هو الله الخالق وأسروا قولكم أو اجهروا به إنه عليم بذات الصدور ألا يعلم من خلق هل من خالق غير الله والذين يدعون من دون الله لا يخلقون شيئاً ماذا خلق الذين من دونه
هذه الآيات صرح فيها بلفظ الخلق إلا أن في دلالتها على المطلوب نوع احتمال وخفاء، لهذا جعلها نوعاً آخر، فقوله هو الله الخالق إنما يفيد حصر الخالقية في الله إذا كان الخالق خبراً وهو ضمير الشأن، أو ضميراً مبهماً يفسره الله، وأما إذا كان الخالق صفة، فذكر الإمام أنه لما كان الله علماً، والعلم لا يدل إلا على الذات المخصوصة
The Third Proof: He said: Among them is His saying, exalted be He: 'He is Allah, the Creator'; 'And conceal your speech or declare it; indeed, He is Knower of the innermost breasts. Does He who created not know?'; 'Is there any creator besides Allah?'; 'And those they invoke besides Allah create nothing'; 'What have those besides Him created?'. These verses explicitly mention the word 'creation' (khalq), except that their indication of the desired conclusion has some degree of probability and subtlety; for this reason, he made them a separate type. For His saying 'He is Allah, the Creator' only conveys the exclusivity of creatorship to Allah if 'al-Khaliq' is a predicate (khabar), and it is a pronoun of the matter (ḍamīr al-sha'n) or an ambiguous pronoun (ḍamīr mubham) interpreted by 'Allah'. But if 'al-Khaliq' is an adjective (ṣifa), then the Imam said: since 'Allah' is a proper noun (ʿalam), and a proper noun indicates only the specific essence...
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof, citing several Quranic verses that explicitly use the word 'creation' (khalq). The verses include: 'He is Allah, the Creator' (Surah al-Hashr 24), 'Does He who created not know?' (Surah al-Mulk 14), 'Is there any creator besides Allah?' (Surah Fatir 3), 'And those they invoke besides Allah create nothing' (Surah al-Nahl 20), and 'What have those besides Him created?' (Surah Luqman 11). The author notes that although these verses contain the word 'creation', their indication of the desired conclusion (that only Allah creates) involves some subtlety and probability. Therefore, the author treats them as a separate type of proof. He then focuses on the verse 'He is Allah, the Creator' and explains that it only implies exclusive creatorship if the word 'al-Khaliq' is taken as a predicate (khabar) with 'He' being either the pronoun of the matter (ḍamīr al-sha'n) or an ambiguous pronoun (ḍamīr mubham) that is interpreted by 'Allah'. If, however, 'al-Khaliq' is an adjective (ṣifa) describing 'Allah', then the exclusivity is not directly proven because 'Allah' is a proper noun (ʿalam) that indicates only the specific essence without necessarily implying that the attribute is exclusive. The passage ends with this grammatical analysis, noting the Imam's opinion on the matter.

Key Terms
خالق
الخلق
خبر
صفة
ضمير الشأن
ضمير مبهم
علم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any verse containing the word 'khalq' (creation) straightforwardly proves that only Allah creates.
Correction: The passage shows that grammatical structure matters: in 'Huwa Allah al-Khaliq', exclusivity depends on whether 'al-Khaliq' is taken as a predicate or an adjective.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, under which grammatical condition does the verse 'Huwa Allah al-Khaliq' (He is Allah, the Creator) imply exclusive creatorship?
A) When 'al-Khaliq' is an adjective (ṣifa)
B) When 'al-Khaliq' is a predicate (khabar) and 'Huwa' is a pronoun of the matter or ambiguous pronoun
C) When 'al-Khaliq' is a proper noun (ʿalam)
D) Under no condition

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'Huwa Allah al-Khaliq' only proves exclusive creation if 'al-Khaliq' is taken as a predicate, not an adjective.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Huwa Allah al-Khaliq', do you take it as a statement of exclusive creation without considering its grammatical depth? How might your certainty be affected if you ignored precise wording? Commit to examining every Quranic proof for tawhid with similar care.
?
This lesson shows that even a seemingly straightforward verse requires careful analysis to avoid misunderstanding. Have you ever accepted a theological claim without verifying its exact evidence? Are there areas in your belief where you might be relying on surface-level understanding? Resolve to study the precise language of Quranic arguments.
?
The author distinguishes between predicate and adjective to protect tawhid. Is there any belief you hold that you have not subjected to such rigorous scrutiny? What would it cost you to correct a mistaken understanding? Ask Allah to guide you to the precise truth.

The Argument from 'Does He Who Created Not Know?'
Author argues that God's knowledge of inner thoughts implies their creation by Him.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the verse 'ألا يعلم من خلق' is used to prove God's knowledge of inner thoughts through the logical relationship of necessary consequence (luzum), and the debate over whether it implies human creation of actions.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of what is in hearts proves He created those inner states.

In this passage, the author unpacks a key Qur'anic argument for God's knowledge of our innermost thoughts. The verse 'Does He who created not know?' is presented as a proof that God's knowledge of inner thoughts (ذوات الصدور) is a necessary consequence of His creating them. However, the author also points out a subtle debate: does this verse also imply that humans create their own actions? Let's follow the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the grammatical clarification
The author begins by noting a grammatical nuance: when a judgment is like an indication, it cannot refer back to the entity itself but to its description. This sets the stage for interpreting the verse.
This part is about the phrase 'بمنزلة الإشارة'.
2
Examine the verse argument
The verse 'ألا يعلم من خلق' is used to prove God's knowledge of inner thoughts (motives, beliefs, etc.) by showing that knowledge is a necessary consequent (lazim) of creation (malzum).
Label the logical relationship clearly.
3
Consider the verse's style and its dual use
The author notes that the verse's style indicates the clarity of both the necessitation and the existence of creation. This allows the verse to be used both for God's knowledge and, by some, to argue that the servant creates his own actions.
This is where the debate arises.
4
Assess the caution and conclusion
The author warns that 'what is in the breasts' may not be voluntary actions (الأفعال الاختيارية) which are in dispute. So the verse's implication for human creation is not straightforward. The lesson ends with an invitation to ponder.
This shows the author's careful approach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بمنزلة الإشارة لم يجز أن يكون الحكم عائداً إليه ، إذ لا معنى لقولنا : إن هذا المعين ليس إلا هذا المعين. ولزم أن يكون عائداً إلى الوصف على معنى أنه الخالق لا غيره وفيه ضعف لا يخفى على العارف بأساليب الكلام وقوله تعالى: ﴿ وأسروا قولكم أو اجهروا به إنه عليم بذات الصدور ألا يعلم من خلق (۲) احتجاج على علمه تعالى بما في القلوب من الدواعي والصوارف والعقائد والخواطر ، بكونه خالقاً لها على طريق ثبوت اللازم. أعني العلم بثبوت ملزومه أعني الخلق وفي أسلوب الكلام إشارة إلى أن كلاً من اللزوم وثبوت الملزوم واضح لا ينبغي أن يشك فيه، ولهذا يستدل بالآية (۳) على نفس كون العبد خالقاً لأفعاله على طريق نفس الملزوم. أعني خلقه لأفعاله ينفي اللازم أعني علمه بتفاصيلها لكن كون ذوات الصدور من (4) قبيل الأفعال الاختيارية التي فيها النزاع محل بحث وكذا دلالة الآية على كون العلم لوازم الخلق على الإطلاق بل على تقدير كون الخالق هو اللطيف الخبير فليتأمل.
Being in the position of indication, it is not permissible that the judgment be attributed to it, for there is no meaning to our saying: 'This specific thing is nothing but this specific thing.' And it is necessary that it be attributed to the description in the sense that He is the Creator, no one else. And in this is a weakness not hidden from one who knows the styles of speech. And His saying, the Exalted: 'And conceal your speech or proclaim it; indeed He is Knower of that within the breasts. Does He who created not know?' is an argument for His knowledge, the Exalted, of what is in the hearts of motives, deterrents, beliefs, and thoughts, by His being their Creator, by way of establishing the necessary consequent. That is, knowledge of the existence of its necessary condition, i.e., creation. And in the style of the speech there is an indication that both the necessitation and the existence of the necessary condition are clear, such that they should not be doubted. And for this reason the verse is used to argue for the very fact that the servant is the creator of his actions, by way of the same necessary condition. That is, his creation of his actions negates the necessary consequent, i.e., His knowledge of their details. But the fact that 'what is in the breasts' is of the category of voluntary actions which are in dispute is a subject of investigation. Similarly, the verse's indication that knowledge is a necessary consequent of creation in an absolute sense, but on the assumption that the Creator is the Subtle, the Aware, so let one ponder.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the argument from the verse 'ألا يعلم من خلق' (Does He who created not know?) as a proof for God's knowledge of inner thoughts. The author begins by clarifying a grammatical point: when a judgment is like an indication, it cannot refer back to the thing itself but to its description. Then he explains that the verse is used to prove that God knows what is in hearts because He creates those inner states. This is based on the logical relationship of necessary concomitance (luzum): knowledge is a necessary consequent of creation. The style of the verse shows that both the necessitation and the existence of the necessary condition (creation) are clear. However, some use this verse to argue that the servant creates his own actions, because if the servant creates his actions, then God would know them. But the author cautions that the inner states (ذوات الصدور) may not be voluntary actions (الأفعال الاختيارية) which are the subject of dispute. So the verse's implication for human creation of actions is debatable. The author ends with 'let one ponder'.

Key Terms
خلق
علم
لازم
ملزوم
ذوات الصدور
أفعال اختيارية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse directly proves that humans create their own actions.
Correction: The passage notes that the verse's implication for human creation is debatable because the inner thoughts (ذوات الصدور) may not be the voluntary actions in dispute.

Checkpoint
What is the main logical relationship (luzum) used in the argument from the verse "ألا يعلم من خلق"?
A) Creation is a necessary condition for knowledge.
B) Knowledge is a necessary consequent of creation.
C) The servant's creation of actions implies God's knowledge.
D) God's knowledge implies creation.

Key Takeaway
The verse 'ألا يعلم من خلق' argues that God's knowledge of inner thoughts is a necessary consequence of His creating them, but its use to prove human creation of actions requires careful examination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe that Allah knows every hidden thought and motive in your heart because He created them. What is one secret thought or feeling you have been nursing that you now realize you must bring to Him in complete submission, knowing He already knows it and created it? What will you do about it today?
?
If Allah's knowledge of your inner states is a necessary consequence of His creating them, then your very doubts, desires, and fears are under His sovereign control. Which of your internal struggles have you been treating as if they are outside of His knowledge and authority? How will you entrust that struggle to Him starting now?
?
The verse 'Does He who created not know?' is used to argue for both God's knowledge and, by some, human agency. Examine your own heart: do you ever resist acknowledging Allah's complete knowledge over your inner states because you fear it would negate your own responsibility? Where do you need to surrender the false autonomy of your inner world to the One who created and knows it fully?

Is There a Creator Other Than Allah? The Verse and a Scholarly Objection
Author examines verse 35:3 and an objection regarding angels as providers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the verse 'Is there any creator other than Allah...' is interpreted to preserve divine uniqueness in creation and provision, and the weakness in a response involving angels as creators of their actions.

Core Idea
Only Allah is both creator and provider from heaven; angels do not create their actions.

In this passage, the author examines a key Qur'anic argument for Allah's exclusive role as creator and provider. He clarifies that the verse does not deny any creator absolutely, but specifically one who provides sustenance from heaven. An objection from Imam al-Razi regarding angels is presented and critiqued, revealing important nuances in Ash'ari theology about agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation

2
Understand the author's clarification
The author distinguishes between absolute denial of other creators and denial of a creator who also provides from heaven.
3
Analyze the objection and response
Examine the objection about angels as providers and the author's critique of that response.
4
Reflect on the theological implications
Consider why this distinction matters for Ash'ari views on causality and divine unity.

وقوله تعالى هل من خالق غير الله يرزقكم من السماء والأرض (٥) لا ينفي خالقاً سوى الله على الإطلاق بل بوصف كونه رازقاً لنا من السماء والعبد ليس كذلك. وأجاب الإمام بأن ملائكة السماء الساعين في إنزال الأمطار رازقون لنا بمعنى التمكين من الانتفاع بأنواع النبات والثمار كما يقال : رزق السلطان فلاناً، فلو كانوا خالقين لأفعالهم لوجد خالق غير الله يرزق من السماء وفيه ضعف .

And the Imam answered that the angels of the heaven who strive in sending down rain are providers for us in the sense of enabling us to benefit from the various kinds of plants and fruits, just as it is said: 'The sultan provided for so-and-so.' So if they were creators of their actions, there would exist a creator other than Allah who provides from the heaven, but there is weakness in this.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage examines the verse 'هل من خالق غير الله يرزقكم من السماء والأرض' (Is there any creator other than Allah who provides for you from the heaven and earth?). The author notes that this verse does not deny any creator besides Allah absolutely, but only one who also provides sustenance from heaven. The servant (al-'abd) is not a provider from heaven. Then an objection by the Imam (likely al-Razi) is mentioned: the angels who cause rain are providers, so if they created their own actions, there would be another creator. But the author says this response is weak.

The Fourth Proof: Verses with 'Ja'ala' (Make)
Author uses Qur'anic commands 'make us Muslims' etc. to prove that God makes actions of servants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Arabic verb 'ja'ala' (to make) when used with two objects in the Quran indicates that human actions are created by Allah.

Core Idea
The verb 'ja'ala' in the Quran implies divine creation of human actions.

This lesson examines the fourth proof from the Quran, focusing on verses where Allah is asked to 'make' the servant into a particular state. The author argues that the grammatical construction of 'ja'ala' with two objects necessarily implies that the resulting state—including actions like prayer—is brought about by Allah's creation. This proof challenges those who claim that humans independently produce their own acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then the literal translation. Get a sense of the argument's flow.
The passage is incomplete; it cuts off as it begins to mention the Mu'tazila.
2
Identify the Quranic verses cited
Find the three verses that use 'ja'ala' with two objects. Notice how each verse asks Allah to 'make' the servant into something (Muslim, establisher of prayer, pleasing).
The verses are: 'واجعلنا مسلمين', 'رب اجعلني مقيم الصلاة', 'واجعله رب رضيا'.
3
Understand the linguistic point
The author explains that 'ja'ala' when it takes two objects means 'to transform' (tasyir), i.e., to bring about a new state. This is key to the proof.
4
Grasp the theological conclusion
Since the second object (the desired state) is an action of the servant (e.g., being Muslim, establishing prayer), the verse shows that action is 'by the making and creation of Allah'.
This directly opposes the Mu'tazilite view that humans create their own actions.

Source Text

For the verb 'ja'ala' that takes two objects is in the sense of making (tasyir), i.e., obtaining one state in place of another. So when its second object is from the actions of servants, it indicates that they are by the making of Allah and by His creation. And the Mu'tazila...
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof from Quranic verses that use the verb 'ja'ala' (to make) with two objects, e.g., 'واجعلنا مسلمين', 'رب اجعلني مقيم الصلاة', 'واجعله رب رضيا'. The author explains that 'ja'ala' in such constructions means to transform one state into another (tasyir). When the second object is an action of the servant, it indicates that the action is by the making and creation of Allah. The text cuts off as it mentions the Mu'tazila (presumably their objection). The proof is incomplete in this window.

?
The Mu'tazila denied that Allah creates human actions, fearing it would negate human accountability. Yet the Qur'an repeatedly commands servants to ask Allah to 'make' them righteous. Are you guilty of the same fear—holding back from full dependence on Allah because you want to feel in control? What would it cost you to surrender this control?

The Fifth Proof: God Does What He Wills
The author presents the fifth proof from verses and refutes a misinterpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth proof from the Qur'an that God is the creator of all actions, including acts of obedience, based on the apparent meaning of verses about His will.

Core Idea
God's will attaches to every action, so He is their creator.

This lesson examines the fifth proof from the text, which uses Qur'anic verses to demonstrate that God's will encompasses all acts, including faith and obedience. The author argues that to interpret the verses as merely meaning God does what He intends is to miss the point; the clear sense is that He actively creates the actions He wills.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the direct citation of verses.
2
Identify the key verses
The passage quotes two similar expressions: 'Doer of what He wills' and 'Allah does what He wills and decrees what He desires'. Find them in the Arabic.
3
Understand the logical argument
The author argues: God's will pertains to all acts, including faith and obedience. Therefore, the doer (fa'il) and originator (mujid) of those acts must be God. Trace this reasoning step by step.
This is the core of the proof.
4
Note the rejection of the alternative interpretation
The author warns against reading 'He does what He wills' as 'He does what He intends to do'. Why is that a departure from the apparent meaning?
The apparent meaning is that God actually creates the actions, not just that He intends them.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this proof supports the doctrine that God creates all human actions, including acts of obedience. How does it relate to the broader discussion of qadar?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدليل الخامس قال ومنها مثل : وفعال لما يريد . يفعل الله ما يشاء ويحكم ما يريد . هذه آيات تدل على أن الله تعالى يفعل كل ما يتعلق به إرادته ومشيئته وهي بالإيمان وسائر الطاعات أيضاً، فيجب أن يكون فاعلها أي موجدها هو الله تعالى، وحمل الكلام على أنه يفعل ما يريد فعله عدول عن الظاهر.
The fifth proof. He said: And among them is like: 'And Doer of what He wills' (Quran 11:107), 'Allah does what He wills and decrees what He desires' (from Quran). These verses indicate that Allah, the Exalted, does everything that His will (irada) and desire (mashiyya) pertain to, and that includes faith and all other acts of obedience. Thus, their doer (fa'il), i.e., their originator (mujid), must be Allah, the Exalted. To interpret the speech as meaning that He does what He wills to do is a departure from the apparent meaning.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof. It quotes verses stating that God is 'Doer of what He wills' and that He 'does what He wills and decrees what He desires.' The author argues that these verses prove God does everything that His will and desire attach to. Since His will includes faith and all acts of obedience (as well as other things), it follows that God is the doer (fa'il) and originator (mujid) of those acts. The author then rejects a possible misinterpretation: some might read 'He does what He wills to do' as a tautology (i.e., He does what He intends to do), but that would be a departure from the apparent meaning (zahir) of the text, which indicates that the actions themselves are willed and done by God. Thus, God creates even human acts of obedience.

Key Takeaway
This proof from the Qur'an shows that since God's will includes all actions, He is the creator of all actions, including human acts of obedience.

God's Wisdom Precludes Creating Evil
Second proof: some acts are inherently evil, so a wise God cannot create them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument that intrinsically evil acts cannot be created by a wise God.

Core Idea
A wise God cannot create what is inherently evil.

This brief but dense passage presents the second rational argument of the Mu'tazilites. It claims that certain acts are objectively ugly, and it would be incompatible with God's wisdom for Him to create them. This argument is central to their defense of human free will, as it forces the conclusion that such acts must be created by the servant, not God.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the key terms: 'qaba'ih' (ugly acts) and 'al-Hakim' (the Wise).
2
Identify the examples
The passage lists three examples of ugly acts: injustice (zulm), polytheism (shirk), and claiming a son (ithbat al-walad). Reflect on why these are considered inherently evil.
3
Understand the logical structure
The argument has two premises: (1) Some acts are intrinsically ugly. (2) It is ugly (inappropriate) for a wise being to create such acts. The conclusion is that God, being perfectly wise, does not create them. Therefore, the servant must be the agent.
This leads to the Mu'tazilite doctrine of free will.
4
Connect to the broader debate
Consider why this argument is controversial. It assumes acts have objective moral qualities independent of God's command. How would an Ash'arite respond?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أن من الأفعال قبائح يقبح من الحكيم خلقها كالظلم والشرك، وإثبات الولد ونحو ذلك .
Second: That among acts there are ugly acts which it is ugly for the Wise to create, such as injustice, polytheism, claiming a son, and the like.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage presents the second rational argument of the Mu'tazilites. It states that there are certain acts that are intrinsically ugly or evil (qaba'ih), and it would be ugly (yaqbuh) for the Wise (al-Hakim) — i.e., God — to create them. Examples given are injustice (zulm), polytheism (shirk), and claiming a son (ithbat al-walad). The argument implies that since God is wise and cannot do evil, He cannot be the creator of such acts. Therefore, these acts must be created by the servant, who is the actual agent. This argument relies on the objective moral quality of actions and the necessary connection between God's wisdom and His actions.

Key Terms
القبائح
الحكيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies God's omnipotence over evil acts.
Correction: The argument does not deny that God has power over everything, but holds that His wisdom prevents Him from creating evil. Evil acts are within God's power, but His wisdom precludes their creation.

Checkpoint

B) The objective ugliness of certain acts and God's wisdom
C) Human intuition
D) Scriptural texts

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites argue that objectively evil acts cannot be created by a wise God, thus attributing them to human agency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's wisdom prevents Him from creating evil, then where do the evil acts you commit come from? How does this truth change the way you view your own sins and your need for repentance?
?
The passage lists 'injustice' as an inherently ugly act. Examine your own life: in what situations have you committed injustice—even small ones—that you have excused? Will you repent and make amends today?
?
Do you truly believe that God is wise in everything He commands? Or have you ever thought that some of His commands are arbitrary or too difficult? How does this lesson challenge that thought, and what will you do to align your heart with His wisdom?

The Fourth Doubt: Does God Become the Doer of Actions He Creates?
Presents and refutes the fourth Mu'tazilite doubt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth Mu'tazilite doubt against divine creation of actions and how it is refuted by distinguishing between the one who brings action into existence and the one in whom the action subsists.

Core Idea
Creating an action does not make the Creator its doer; moral names attach to the substrate, not the originator.

The Mu'tazila argued that if God creates human actions, He would be their doer and thus be described by them—leading to absurdities like calling God a 'kafir'. Sanusi presents this doubt and immediately refutes it by a key conceptual distinction: the names of actions apply only to the being in which the action inheres, not to the one who causes its existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the doubt
Understand the Mu'tazilite reasoning: if God creates actions, He is their doer; if doer, then described by them; then God would be called kafir, etc.
The doubt is presented in a compact logical chain.
2
Identify the refutation
The author says such names only apply to the one in whom the action subsists, not the one who brings it into existence.
This is the core distinction: mūjid vs. qā'im.
3
Appreciate the analogy
Just as created attributes like heat exist in a substrate but the substrate is described as hot, God creates actions but the servant is the substrate and thus described.
The analogy strengthens the refutation.
4
Recognize the polemical tone
The author expresses astonishment that this doubt appears in Mu'tazilite books, attributing it to fanaticism.
Sanusi is clearly frustrated with the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : أن الله تعالى لو كان موجداً لأفعال العباد لكان فاعلاً لها، لأن معناها واحد، ولو كان فاعلاً لها لكان متصفاً بها لأنه لا معنى للكافر والظالم مثلاً إلا فاعل الكفر والظلم وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون الباري تعالى وتقدس كافراً ظالماً فاسقاً أكلاً شارباً قائماً قاعداً إلى غير ذلك من الفواحش التي لا يستطيع العاقل إجزاءها على اللسان، بل إخطارها بالبال وهذه الشبهة كنا نسمعها من حمقى العوام والسوقية. من المعتزلة فنتعجب حتى وجدناها في كتبهم المعتبرة، فتحققنا أن التعصب يغطي على العقول، وعنده تعمى القلوب التي في الصدور، ولا أدري كيف ذهب عليهم أن مثل هذه الأسامي إنما تطلق على من قام به الفعل لا من أوجد الفعل أو لا يرون أن كثيراً من الصفات قد أوجدها الله تعالى في مجالها ، وفاقاً، ولا تتصف بها إلا المحال . نعم إذا ثبت بالدليل أن الموجد هو الله تعالى لزمهم صحة هذه التسمية بناء على أصلهم الفاسد في إطلاق المتكلم على الله تعالى لا يجاده الكلام في بعض الأجسام، وكأن قول القائل لخصمه : مذهبك باطل حجه لكونه كلام الله تعالى عن ذلك علواً كبيراً. وهم الجهلهم يوردون مثل هذا الإلزام على أهل الحق ، ويجعلون قول السني للمعتزلي آذيتني، أو طلبتك، أو أقبل علي، وما أشبه ذلك تركاً للمذهب، ويعتقدون أن إسناد الأفعال إلى العباد مجاز عند أهل السنة [ وتمادوا في ذلك حتى زعم بعض من يعتقده الشيعة أعلم الناس أن مثل طلعت الشمس مجاز عند أهل السنة ]
The fourth: That if God Almighty were the creator of the actions of servants, He would be their doer, because their meaning is one. And if He were their doer, He would be described by them, because there is no meaning of 'kafir' and 'wrongdoer' for example except as the doer of kufr and wrongdoing. Then it would be necessary that the Exalted and Transcendent Creator be a kafir, a wrongdoer, a transgressor, an eater, a drinker, one who stands, one who sits, and other such obscenities which a rational person cannot bear to utter, rather even to bring to mind. This doubt we used to hear from foolish commoners of the Mu'tazila and we were astonished until we found it in their authoritative books. Then we realized that fanaticism covers minds, and with it the hearts in the breasts are blind. And I do not know how it escaped them that such names only apply to the one in whom the action subsists, not to the one who brings the action into existence. Or do they not see that many attributes God Almighty has brought into existence in their substrates, unanimously, and only the substrates are described by them? Indeed, if it is established by proof that the creator is God Almighty, then the correctness of this naming is necessary for them based on their corrupt principle in applying 'speaker' to God Almighty by His creating speech in some bodies. It is as if the statement of a person to his opponent: 'Your doctrine is false, its proof is that it is speech of God'—exalted is God above that greatly. And due to their ignorance, they raise such an objection against the people of truth, and they consider the statement of a Sunni to a Mu'tazili: 'You harmed me', or 'I sought you', or 'Come to me', and the like, as an abandonment of the doctrine, and they believe that ascribing actions to servants is metaphorical among the Ahl al-Sunnah. And they persisted in that until some of those who follow the Shi'a claimed—the most knowledgeable of people—that expressions like 'the sun rose' are metaphorical among the Ahl al-Sunnah.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage is an argument refutation. The author presents the fourth doubt of the Mu'tazila: if God creates human actions, then He would be the 'doer' of those actions, and thus would be described by them—meaning He would be called a kafir, a wrongdoer, etc., which is impossible for God. The author then refutes this by distinguishing between the one who brings an action into existence (al-mūjid) and the one in whom the action subsists (man qāma bihi al-fiʿl). The names (kāfir, ẓālim) are ascribed only to the latter, not to the former. He points out that many attributes (like heat, cold) are created by God in substrates but only the substrates are described by them. He further criticizes the Mu'tazila for inconsistency: they accept that God is called 'speaker' because He creates speech in some body, yet they resist calling Him 'doer' of evil actions in a similar sense. He accuses them of ignorance and fanaticism. Finally, he notes that they incorrectly think that Sunnis consider all action ascriptions to servants as metaphorical, and he cites an extreme example from the Shi'a. The argument preserves the Sunni position that actions are truly created by God while also being acquired by the servant, and that divine attributes are not compromised.

Key Terms
الموجد
القائم به الفعل
الفاعل
التسمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God creates a sinful action, He is the doer of that sin and thus sinful Himself.
Correction: The names of actions are applied to the being in whom the action inheres (the servant), not to the being who brings it into existence (God).

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does creating an action not make God a kāfir or ẓālim?
A) Because those names apply to the one who brings the action into existence.
B) Because those names apply to the one in whom the action subsists, not the one who creates it.
C) Because God is beyond such descriptions altogether.
D) Because the Mu'tazila are mistaken about the meaning of kāfir.

Key Takeaway
Creating an action does not attribute its moral quality to the Creator; moral responsibility is tied to the subject in which the action inheres.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever find yourself mentally excusing it by thinking, 'God created this action, so it's not entirely my fault'? How does this passage's distinction between creator and doer shatter that excuse and place the full weight of your sin on you?
?
If you truly believed that God creates your actions but you are the one described by them, would that make you more vigilant about your deeds? What specific sin would you start fighting differently knowing that you alone bear its name?
?
This lesson says that moral names like 'kafir' and 'wrongdoer' belong only to the one in whom the action subsists. When you look at your own actions, what name do they deserve? Are you willing to call them what they are, knowing that God is not tainted by them but you are?

The Mu'tazilite Inconsistency: From God as Speaker to Doer of Evil
Further refutation and criticism of Mu'tazilite inconsistency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author further refutes the Mu'tazila by pointing out their inconsistency regarding the term 'speaker' and exposing the fallacy in their objection against the Sunnis.

Core Idea
If the Mu'tazila accept that God is called 'speaker' by creating speech in a body, they should consistently accept that God creates evil actions without being evil.

Having refuted the fourth doubt by the substrate distinction, Sanusi now turns to a deeper inconsistency in Mu'tazilite thought. They accept that God is called 'speaker' because He creates speech in some bodies, yet they deny that God could be called 'doer' of evil actions in a similar sense. Moreover, they mistakenly think that Sunnis consider ascription of actions to servants as mere metaphor, and they use this to press absurd objections.

Guided Path
1
Follow the consistency argument
Sanusi says if it is established that God is the creator, then based on the Mu'tazilite's own principle of applying 'speaker' to God (by creating speech in a body), they must accept that God could be called 'doer' of actions by creating them in bodies.
This turns their own logic against them.
2
Understand the absurd objection
The author criticizes the Mu'tazila for raising objections like saying to a Sunni, 'You harmed me, I sought you' etc., as if that implies the Sunni has abandoned his doctrine.
They misunderstand the Sunni position on kasb.
3
Expose the misunderstanding of metaphor
The Mu'tazila believe that Sunnis consider all ascriptions of actions to servants as metaphorical; some even claim 'the sun rose' is metaphorical according to Sunnis, which is extreme.
Sunnis affirm real acquisition (kasb), not pure metaphor.
4
Recognize the tone
The author uses strong language to criticize the Mu'tazila's ignorance and fanaticism.
Sanusi is exasperated by their inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : أن الله تعالى لو كان موجداً لأفعال العباد لكان فاعلاً لها، لأن معناها واحد، ولو كان فاعلاً لها لكان متصفاً بها لأنه لا معنى للكافر والظالم مثلاً إلا فاعل الكفر والظلم وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون الباري تعالى وتقدس كافراً ظالماً فاسقاً أكلاً شارباً قائماً قاعداً إلى غير ذلك من الفواحش التي لا يستطيع العاقل إجزاءها على اللسان، بل إخطارها بالبال وهذه الشبهة كنا نسمعها من حمقى العوام والسوقية. من المعتزلة فنتعجب حتى وجدناها في كتبهم المعتبرة، فتحققنا أن التعصب يغطي على العقول، وعنده تعمى القلوب التي في الصدور، ولا أدري كيف ذهب عليهم أن مثل هذه الأسامي إنما تطلق على من قام به الفعل لا من أوجد الفعل أو لا يرون أن كثيراً من الصفات قد أوجدها الله تعالى في مجالها ، وفاقاً، ولا تتصف بها إلا المحال . نعم إذا ثبت بالدليل أن الموجد هو الله تعالى لزمهم صحة هذه التسمية بناء على أصلهم الفاسد في إطلاق المتكلم على الله تعالى لا يجاده الكلام في بعض الأجسام، وكأن قول القائل لخصمه : مذهبك باطل حجه لكونه كلام الله تعالى عن ذلك علواً كبيراً. وهم الجهلهم يوردون مثل هذا الإلزام على أهل الحق ، ويجعلون قول السني للمعتزلي آذيتني، أو طلبتك، أو أقبل علي، وما أشبه ذلك تركاً للمذهب، ويعتقدون أن إسناد الأفعال إلى العباد مجاز عند أهل السنة [ وتمادوا في ذلك حتى زعم بعض من يعتقده الشيعة أعلم الناس أن مثل طلعت الشمس مجاز عند أهل السنة ]
The fourth: That if God Almighty were the creator of the actions of servants, He would be their doer, because their meaning is one. And if He were their doer, He would be described by them, because there is no meaning of 'kafir' and 'wrongdoer' for example except as the doer of kufr and wrongdoing. Then it would be necessary that the Exalted and Transcendent Creator be a kafir, a wrongdoer, a transgressor, an eater, a drinker, one who stands, one who sits, and other such obscenities which a rational person cannot bear to utter, rather even to bring to mind. This doubt we used to hear from foolish commoners of the Mu'tazila and we were astonished until we found it in their authoritative books. Meanings of Qada' and Qadar Across Schools
Comparative definitions of qada' and qadar from three theological schools.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinct meanings of qada' and qadar as defined by the Ash'aris, Mu'tazila, and Philosophers, and how they are supported by Qur'anic usage.

Core Idea
Qada' and qadar have multiple valid meanings, but the Ash'ari primary sense is creation and determining.

The terms qada' and qadar are central to Islamic theology but are understood differently by various schools. This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid lays out the definitions of the Ash'aris, Mu'tazila, and Philosophers, each drawing on specific Qur'anic verses. By examining these positions, you will see how a single term can carry distinct theological implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the Ash'ari definition
Start by reading the first sentence: 'وأفعاله بقضاء الله تعالى وقدره بمعنى خلقه، وتقديره...' Understand that for Ash'aris, qada' and qadar mean creation and determining, and that approval pertains only to the decree itself.
Notice the distinction between qada' (decree) and maqdi (decreed thing).
2
Identify the Mu'tazili and Philosopher positions
Read the sentences about the Mu'tazila and Philosophers. The Mu'tazila restrict the terms to informing/writing or obligation, while the Philosophers define qada' as collective existence in the intellect and qadar as detailed existence in matter.
The Philosophers' view introduces the idea of evil entering by subordination.
3
Examine the evidence from the Qur'an
Read the later part where the author cites verses to support the meanings of creation, obligation, and informing. See how each verse is used to justify a particular interpretation.
The phrase 'فمعنى القضاء والقدر الخلق والتقدير' is key for the Ash'ari position.
4
Reflect on the diversity of meanings
Consider that the author acknowledges multiple valid meanings but asserts that for the people of truth (Ash'aris), the primary meaning is creation. This shows how theological schools interpret scripture differently.
The conclusion 'فعلى هذا جميع الأفعال بالقضاء والقدر' applies to the informing meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال وأفعاله بقضاء الله تعالى وقدره بمعنى خلقه، وتقديره ابتداء أو بوسط موجب والرضا إنما يجب بالقضاء دون المقضي، وعند المعتزلة لا يصلح إلا بمعنى الإعلام والكتبة أو بمعنى الإلزام في الواجبات خاصة، وقالت الفلاسفة : القضاء وجود الكائنات في العالم العقلي مجملة والقدر وجودها في موادها الخارجية مفصلة . ودخول الشر في القضاء بالتبعية. قد اشتهر من بين أكثر الملل أن الحوادث بقضاء الله تعالى وقدره، وهذا يتناول أفعال العباد، وأمره ظاهر عند أهل الحق لما تبين أنه الخالق لها نفسها، أو الخالق للقدرة والداعية الموجبتين لها ، فمعنى القضاء والقدر الخلق والتقدير كما في قوله تعالى فقضاهن سبع سموات وقوله تعالى وقدر فيها أقواتها ولا يستقيم هذا عند القدرية، وقد يكون القضاء والقدر معنى الإيجاب والإلزام كما في قوله تعالى. وقضى ربك ألا تعبدوا إلا إياه وقوله تعالى نحن قدرنا بينكم الموت فيكون الواجبات بالقضاء والقدر دون البواقي، وقد يراد بهما الإعلام والتبيين لقوله تعالى وقضينا إلى بني إسرائيل في الكتاب لتفسدن في الأرض الآية وقوله تعالى إلا امرأته قدرناها من الغابرين أي أعلمنا بذلك وكتبناه في اللوح فعلى هذا جميع الأفعال بالقضاء والقدر.
He said: And His actions are by the decree (qada') of Allah Almighty and His predestination (qadar), meaning His creation and His determining initially or through an intermediary that necessitates, and approval is only required for the decree (qada') not the decreed thing (maqdi). According to the Mu'tazila, it is only valid in the sense of informing (i'lam) and writing (kitaba) or in the sense of obligation (ilzam) specifically in obligatory acts. The Philosophers said: qada' is the existence of beings in the intellectual world collectively, and qadar is their existence in their external matters in detail. And the entry of evil into qada' is by subordination. It is well-known among most religious sects that events are by the decree (qada') of Allah Almighty and His predestination (qadar), and this includes the actions of servants. Its matter is apparent to the people of truth (ahl al-haqq) because it has been established that He is the Creator of the actions themselves, or the Creator of the power (qudra) and the motivation (da'iya) that necessitate them. So the meaning of qada' and qadar is creation (khalq) and determining (taqdir), as in His saying: 'Then He completed them as seven heavens' and His saying: 'And He determined therein their provisions.' This is not valid according to the Qadariyya. And qada' and qadar may mean obligation (ijab) and compulsion (ilzam), as in His saying: 'And your Lord has decreed that you worship none but Him' and His saying: 'We have decreed death among you.' So obligatory acts are by qada' and qadar, but not others. And they may be intended to mean informing (i'lam) and clarification (tabyin), as in His saying: 'And We decreed to the Children of Israel in the Scripture' and His saying: 'except his wife, We decreed her to be among those who remain behind,' meaning We informed of that and wrote it in the Preserved Tablet. Thus, on this meaning, all actions are by qada' and qadar.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage is a comparative presentation of the meanings of qada' and qadar across different theological schools. The author begins by stating his own position: that God's actions are by His qada' and qadar, meaning His creation and determining, either directly or through a cause. Approval is only due to the decree itself, not the decreed thing. Then he critiques the Mu'tazila, who restrict the terms to meanings of informing and writing, or obligation only for obligatory acts. The Philosophers are quoted as defining qada' as the collective existence of beings in the intellectual world and qadar as their detailed existence in external matter, with evil entering only subordinately. The author then elaborates that it is widespread among religious sects that events are by qada' and qadar, including human acts. For the people of truth (Ash'aris), this is clear because God is the Creator of the acts themselves or of the power and motivation that cause them. He cites Qur'anic verses to support the meaning of creation and determining. He also acknowledges other valid meanings: obligation (ijab) and informing (i'lam), giving examples from the Qur'an. He concludes that according to the last meaning (informing), all actions are by qada' and qadar. The passage thus shows that the term has multiple valid interpretations, but the primary meaning for the Ash'aris is creation.

Key Terms
قضاء
قدر
خلق
تقدير
إعلام
إلزام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think qada' and qadar only mean creation and that other meanings are invalid.
Correction: The author shows that the terms also legitimately mean obligation (ijab) and informing (i'lam), though the primary Ash'ari sense is creation.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the primary meaning of qada' and qadar for the people of truth (ahl al-haqq)?
A) Informing and writing
B) Obligation and compulsion
C) Creation and determining
D) Collective and detailed existence

Key Takeaway
Qada' and qadar are understood differently across schools, but for Ash'aris the core meaning is divine creation and determining, while other valid meanings include obligation and informing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every action of yours, even the mistakes and sins, is created by Allah? How does that make you feel about your personal responsibility? Are you willing to accept that your evil actions are also part of His decree, even if by subordination?
?
When you face a difficult situation, do you see it as a direct decree from Allah that you must accept with contentment (rida), or do you resist it? How does the distinction between qada' and maqdi (the decreed thing) help you to have proper tawakkul?
?
The Mu'tazila say qada' and qadar only apply to obligations and informings, not to all events. Have you ever secretly thought that some of your actions are outside Allah's control? Are you ready to submit to the Ash'ari teaching that 'all actions are by qada' and qadar'?

Qada' and Qadar in the Philosophers' Framework
The Philosophers define qada' and qadar as existence in the intellectual and material worlds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Philosophers define qada' and qadar within their emanationist cosmology.

Core Idea
Qada' is the unified blueprint in the intellect; qadar is its unfolding in matter.

The Philosophers saw qada' and qadar as two stages of the same creative process. Before we can appreciate their view on evil, we must first grasp these foundational terms. This passage lays out their definitions rooted in the concept of intellectual and material worlds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the philosophical terms like 'ibda' and 'al-awwal al-wajib'.
2
Identify the two definitions
The passage defines qada' and qadar explicitly. Find these definitions and note how they are contrasted: one is collective and summarized, the other detailed and gradual.
The definitions are introduced by 'fa-al-qada''' and 'wa-al-qadara'.
3
Connect to the Quranic verse
The passage ends with a Quranic quotation. Reflect on how the verse supports the philosophical concept of measured descent.
The verse is about treasures and a known measure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالت الفلاسفة لما كانت جميع صور الموجدات الكلية والجزئية حاصلة حيث هي معقولة في العالم العقلي بإبداع الأول الواجب إياها، وكان إيجاد ما يتعلق منها بالمادة في المادة مختلفاً على سبيل الإبداع ممتنعاً إذ هي غير متأتية لقبول صورتين معاً فضلاً عن أكثر، وكان الجود الإلهي مقتضياً لتكميل المادة بإبداع تلك الصور فيها، وإخراج ما فيها بالقوة من قبول تلك الصور إلى العقل قدر بلطيف حكمته زماناً يخرج فيه تلك الأمور من القوة إلى الفعل، فالقضاء عبارة من وجود جميع الموجودات في العالم العقلي مجتمعة ومجملة على سبيل الإبداع، والقدرة عبارة عن وجودها في موادها الخارجية مفصلة واحداً بعد واحد، كما قال عز من قائل وإن من شيء إلا عندنا خزائنه وما ننزله إلا بقدر معلوم. قالوا ودخول الشر في القضاء الإلهي على سبيل التبع، فإن الموجود إما خير محض كالعقول والأفلاك، أو الخير غالب عليه كما في هذا العالم، فإن المرض وإن كثر فالصحة أكثر منه، ولما امتنع عقلاً إيجادها في هذا العالم مبرأ عن الشرور بالكلية، فإن المطر المخصب للبلاد يخرب بعض الدور بالضرورة وجب في الحكمة إيجاده لأن ترك الخير الكثير، لأجل الشر القليل شر كثير، فدخل الشر في القضاء، وإن كان مكروهاً غير مرضى.
And the Philosophers said: Since all forms of existents, universal and particular, are present—being intelligible in the intellectual world by the origination (ibda') of the First Necessary (al-awwal al-wajib) of them, and since bringing into existence what pertains to matter in matter in multiple ways by way of origination is impossible, because matter is not capable of receiving two forms together, let alone more, and since divine generosity requires perfecting matter by originating those forms in it, and bringing out what is in it potentially from acceptance of those forms to actuality, He allotted by His subtle wisdom a time in which those things emerge from potentiality to actuality. So qada' is the expression for the existence of all existents in the intellectual world, gathered and summarized, by way of origination, and qadar is the expression for their existence in their external matters, detailed one after another, as He said, Glory to Him: 'And there is not a thing but with Us are its treasures, and We do not send it down except according to a known measure.' They said: And the entry of evil into the divine qada' is by way of subordination. For the existent is either pure good, like the intellects and spheres, or good predominates over it, as in this world. For illness, even if frequent, health is more frequent. Since it is impossible rationally to bring them into existence in this world completely devoid of evils—for the rain that fertilizes countries necessarily destroys some houses—it is required by wisdom to bring it into existence, because leaving much good for the sake of a little evil is a great evil. So evil entered into qada', even though it is disliked and not approved.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the Philosophers' (falasifa) doctrine on qada' and qadar, followed by their theodicy. The author begins by explaining the philosophical framework: all forms of existents, universal and particular, exist intelligibly in the intellectual world ('aql) due to the origination (ibda') of the First Necessary Being (the First Cause). However, bringing these forms into matter directly through multiple acts of origination is impossible because matter cannot receive two forms at once. Since divine generosity necessitates perfecting matter, God, in His subtle wisdom, allotted a time for the forms to emerge from potentiality to actuality. Hence, qada' is defined as the collective, summarized existence of all things in the intellectual world by way of origination, while qadar is their detailed existence in external matter, unfolding one after another. This is supported by the Qur'anic verse about treasures and a known measure. The passage then addresses the problem of evil. The Philosophers argue that evil enters divine qada' only as subordinate (bi al-taba'). They classify existents into pure good (e.g., intellects, celestial spheres) and those where good predominates (e.g., this world). They illustrate that even though illness is frequent, health is more common. Since it is rationally impossible to create this world entirely free from evils—rain that fertilizes land inevitably destroys some houses—wisdom dictates that it is better to bring about the good despite the accompanying evil. Abandoning much good for the sake of avoiding a little evil is itself a great evil. Thus, evil is included in qada' involuntarily, not as something approved or desirable, but as a necessary consequence of the greater good.

Key Terms
فلاسفة
عالم عقلي
إبداع
قضاء
قدر
مواد خارجية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Philosophers, what is the difference between qada' and qadar?
A) Qada' is detailed, qadar is summarized
B) Qada' is in the intellectual world, qadar is in the material world
C) Qada' is decreed, qadar is optional
D) There is no difference

Key Takeaway
For the Philosophers, qada' is the all-encompassing intellectual plan, and qadar is its step-by-step actualization in the physical world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If qada' is the all-encompassing intellectual plan and qadar is its step-by-step unfolding, how does this affect your patience when events in your life seem chaotic or delayed? Are you willing to trust that each moment is part of a wise, measured descent from a deeper reality?
?
The passage says divine generosity requires perfecting matter over time. Where in your life are you demanding immediate perfection from yourself or others, rather than allowing the gradual emergence that God's wisdom has ordained?
?
Do you live as if your actions are merely random occurrences in matter, or do you see them as the detailed actualization of a timeless plan in the intellect of the First Necessary? What would change in your daily choices if you truly believed that every detail is sent down 'according to a known measure'?
