The Philosophers' Theodicy: Evil as Subordinate to Good
The Philosophers explain that evil enters qada' only as subordinate, since avoiding it would lose greater good.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Philosophers argue that evil enters divine qada' only as a subordinate consequence of a greater good.

Core Idea
Evil is not intended; it is the unavoidable byproduct of a world that is mostly good.

Having defined qada' and qadar, the Philosophers now face a major objection: how can a good God decree evil? Their answer is that evil is not decreed for its own sake but is permitted as a necessary subordinate element in a world where good predominates. This passage presents their rational theodicy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Pay attention to the phrase 'على سبيل التبع' (by way of subordination).
2
Identify the classification of existents
The passage divides existents into two categories: pure good and those where good predominates. Find these categories and note their examples.
Examples: intellects and spheres for pure good, this world for predominant good.
3
Understand the argument from the greater good
Trace the logic from the rain example to the conclusion. Why is it rational to allow evil? Focus on the phrase 'ترك الخير الكثير لأجل الشر القليل شر كثير'.
This is a utilitarian argument: maximizing good justifies unavoidable minor evils.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالت الفلاسفة لما كانت جميع صور الموجدات الكلية والجزئية حاصلة حيث هي معقولة في العالم العقلي بإبداع الأول الواجب إياها، وكان إيجاد ما يتعلق منها بالمادة في المادة مختلفاً على سبيل الإبداع ممتنعاً إذ هي غير متأتية لقبول صورتين معاً فضلاً عن أكثر، وكان الجود الإلهي مقتضياً لتكميل المادة بإبداع تلك الصور فيها، وإخراج ما فيها بالقوة من قبول تلك الصور إلى العقل قدر بلطيف حكمته زماناً يخرج فيه تلك الأمور من القوة إلى الفعل، فالقضاء عبارة من وجود جميع الموجودات في العالم العقلي مجتمعة ومجملة على سبيل الإبداع، والقدرة عبارة عن وجودها في موادها الخارجية مفصلة واحداً بعد واحد، كما قال عز من قائل وإن من شيء إلا عندنا خزائنه وما ننزله إلا بقدر معلوم. قالوا ودخول الشر في القضاء الإلهي على سبيل التبع، فإن الموجود إما خير محض كالعقول والأفلاك، أو الخير غالب عليه كما في هذا العالم، فإن المرض وإن كثر فالصحة أكثر منه، ولما امتنع عقلاً إيجادها في هذا العالم مبرأ عن الشرور بالكلية، فإن المطر المخصب للبلاد يخرب بعض الدور بالضرورة وجب في الحكمة إيجاده لأن ترك الخير الكثير، لأجل الشر القليل شر كثير، فدخل الشر في القضاء، وإن كان مكروهاً غير مرضى.
And the Philosophers said: Since all forms of existents, universal and particular, are present—being intelligible in the intellectual world by the origination (ibda') of the First Necessary (al-awwal al-wajib) of them, and since bringing into existence what pertains to matter in matter in multiple ways by way of origination is impossible, because matter is not capable of receiving two forms together, let alone more, and since divine generosity requires perfecting matter by originating those forms in it, and bringing out what is in it potentially from acceptance of those forms to actuality, He allotted by His subtle wisdom a time in which those things emerge from potentiality to actuality. So qada' is the expression for the existence of all existents in the intellectual world, gathered and summarized, by way of origination, and qadar is the expression for their existence in their external matters, detailed one after another, as He said, Glory to Him: 'And there is not a thing but with Us are its treasures, and We do not send it down except according to a known measure.' They said: And the entry of evil into the divine qada' is by way of subordination. For the existent is either pure good, like the intellects and spheres, or good predominates over it, as in this world. For illness, even if frequent, health is more frequent. Since it is impossible rationally to bring them into existence in this world completely devoid of evils—for the rain that fertilizes countries necessarily destroys some houses—it is required by wisdom to bring it into existence, because leaving much good for the sake of a little evil is a great evil. So evil entered into qada', even though it is disliked and not approved.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the Philosophers' (falasifa) doctrine on qada' and qadar, followed by their theodicy. The author begins by explaining the philosophical framework: all forms of existents, universal and particular, exist intelligibly in the intellectual world ('aql) due to the origination (ibda') of the First Necessary Being (the First Cause). However, bringing these forms into matter directly through multiple acts of origination is impossible because matter cannot receive two forms at once. Since divine generosity necessitates perfecting matter, God, in His subtle wisdom, allotted a time for the forms to emerge from potentiality to actuality. Hence, qada' is defined as the collective, summarized existence of all things in the intellectual world by way of origination, while qadar is their detailed existence in external matter, unfolding one after another. This is supported by the Qur'anic verse about treasures and a known measure. The passage then addresses the problem of evil. The Philosophers argue that evil enters divine qada' only as subordinate (bi al-taba'). They classify existents into pure good (e.g., intellects, celestial spheres) and those where good predominates (e.g., this world). They illustrate that even though illness is frequent, health is more common. Since it is rationally impossible to create this world entirely free from evils—rain that fertilizes land inevitably destroys some houses—wisdom dictates that it is better to bring about the good despite the accompanying evil. Abandoning much good for the sake of avoiding a little evil is itself a great evil. Thus, evil is included in qada' involuntarily, not as something approved or desirable, but as a necessary consequence of the greater good.

Key Terms
فلاسفة
شر بالتبعية
خير محض
خير غالب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Philosophers believe God directly creates evil as an intended act.
Correction: They believe evil enters qada' only as a subordinate consequence, not as a direct intention, and it is disliked and not approved.

Checkpoint
According to the Philosophers, why does evil enter divine qada'?
A) Because God is not powerful enough to prevent it
B) By way of subordination, because avoiding it would result in losing greater good
C) Because evil is an illusion
D) Because evil is not part of qada' at all

Key Takeaway
According to the Philosophers, evil is a necessary part of a world designed to maximize good, and it is not intended for its own sake.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face personal suffering—like illness, loss, or disappointment—do you instinctively see it as a direct punishment or as an unavoidable part of a larger goodness? How does the philosophers' principle that 'leaving much good for a little evil is a great evil' challenge you to rethink your own trials? Will you accept that your pain may serve a good beyond your understanding, or do you demand a world without any suffering?
?
Think of a time you chose a small evil to achieve a greater good—for example, enduring a difficult medical treatment for long-term health. Does your reasoning mirror the philosophers' argument? If you accept this logic in your own decisions, why do you struggle to accept it from God? What does that say about your trust in divine wisdom?
?
The philosophers claim that a world without any evil is rationally impossible. Do you truly believe that? If you reject that premise, what kind of world would you create if you were in charge? Consider the rain that destroys homes but fertilizes land—would you forbid that rain, knowing it brings both good and harm? How does your answer reveal your own assumptions about what is 'best'?

The Universality of God's Will
Establishes that all existents are willed by God, with proofs and definition of will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that everything that occurs is willed by Allah, based on the consensus of the Salaf, divine attributes of creation and knowledge, and the definition of will as preponderance and specification.

Core Idea
God's will encompasses all things; nothing occurs without His will.

Sanusi begins this section by affirming the Ash'ari position that every existent is willed by Allah. He grounds this in three core evidences: the consensus of the Salaf, Allah's role as Creator and Knower, and the very nature of will as an attribute that specifies possibilities. Let's examine these proofs carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice that it starts with a clear statement: 'حق أن كل كائن مراد له، وبالعكس'. Then look for the evidence that follows.
The passage uses the phrase 'وبالعكس' to emphasize the mutual relationship between existence and will.
2
Understand the three proofs
The passage provides three arguments: consensus of the Salaf, Allah being the Creator and Willer of all, and His knowledge that what does not occur will never occur, so He does not will it.
3
Grasp the definition of will (al-irāda)
Sanusi defines will as an attribute that gives preponderance (tarjīḥ) and specification (takhṣīṣ) to one of two equals (al-mutasāwiyān) in relation to power (al-qudra) and the motive (al-dā'iya) leading to the proof.
This definition shows that will is not arbitrary but operates upon possibilities.
4
Reflect on the universality implied
Consider why this definition and the proofs lead to the conclusion that everything that happens is willed by God. How does this shape your understanding of divine attribute?
This is the foundation for refuting Mu'tazila in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني في عموم إرادته تعالى (في عموم إرادته الحق أن كل كائن مراد له، وبالعكس، لما أجمع عليه السلف أن ما شاء الله كان وما لم يشأ لم يكن، ولأنه خالق للكل مريده، وعالم بعدم وقوع ما لم يقع فلا يريده، لأن الإرادة صفة شأنها الترجيح والتخصيص لأحد المتساويين بالنظر إلى القدرة وصرف الداعية إلى الدليل ولو بالغير من الإرادة والنصوص الشاهدة بما ذكرنا أكثر من أن تحصى. والمعتزلة لم يكتفوا بقطع إرادته عن القبائح، بل جزموا بأنها متعلقة بأضدادها. فجعلوا أكثر ما يجري في ملكه خلاف مراده، تمسكاً بأن إرادة القبيح قبيحة . وأن العقاب على ما أريد ظلم، وأن الأمر بما لا يراد والنهي عما يراد سفه، وأن الإرادة تستلزم الأمر والرضاء والمحبة والكل فاسد ولا تمسك لهم بمثل قوله تعالى : ( وما الله يريد ظلماً للعباد ) قل إن الله لا يأمر بالفحشاء و ولا يرضى لعباده الكفر والله لا يحب الفساد . وأما الرد على الذين قالوا لو شاء الله ما أشركنا ولا آباؤنا فلقصدهم الاستهزاء ولذلك قال الله تعالى كذلك كذب الذين من قبلهم حتى ذاقوا
The second investigation: On the universality of His will (may He be exalted). (Regarding the universality of His will, the truth is that every existent is willed by Him, and conversely, because the Salaf are agreed that what God wills comes to be and what He does not will does not come to be, and because He is the Creator of all and the Willer of it, and He knows that what does not occur will not occur, so He does not will it, because will (al-irāda) is an attribute whose function is to give preponderance and specification to one of two equals with respect to power (al-qudra) and directing the motive to the proof, even if through something other than will, and the texts witnessing what we have mentioned are too many to count. The Mu'tazila were not content to exclude evil deeds from His will, but they affirmed that it relates to their opposites. Thus they made most of what occurs in His dominion contrary to His will, clinging to the fact that willing evil is evil, and that punishment for what is willed is injustice, and that commanding what is not willed and prohibiting what is willed is foolish, and that will entails command, approval, and love — all of which is corrupt. And they have no support from such verses as 'And Allah does not will injustice for the servants' (40:31), 'Say: Indeed, Allah does not command immorality' (7:28), 'And He does not approve for His servants disbelief' (39:7), and 'Allah does not like corruption' (2:205). As for the reply to those who said, 'If Allah had willed, we would not have associated partners nor our fathers' (6:148), it is that they intended mockery, and therefore Allah said, 'Thus did those before them deny until they tasted Our punishment.' (6:148)
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage opens a new section on the universality of God's will. The author first states the Ash'ari position: everything that exists is willed by God, and conversely, whatever God wills must exist. This is supported by the consensus of the Salaf ('What God wills is, and what He does not will is not'), by the fact that God is the Creator and Willer of all things, and by His knowledge that what does not occur will never occur, so He does not will it. Will (al-irāda) is defined as an attribute that specifies one of two equal possibilities in relation to power and the motive to act. The author then criticizes the Mu'tazila, who not only exclude evil from God's will but claim His will is attached to the opposites of evil. This leads them to assert that most events in God's kingdom are contrary to His will. They argue that willing evil is evil, that punishing what one wills is unjust, that commanding what one does not will is foolish, and that will implies command, approval, and love. The author dismisses all these arguments as corrupt and notes that the Mu'tazila cannot support their view with the Qur'anic verses they cite (e.g., Allah does not will injustice, does not command immorality, does not approve disbelief, does not like corruption). Finally, the passage addresses the verse 'If Allah had willed, we would not have associated partners' (6:148). The author explains that those who said this were mocking, and God's response is that disbelievers before them also denied the truth until they tasted punishment. The implication is that the verse does not negate God's universal will; rather, it rebukes their mockery.

Key Terms
عموم الإرادة
الإرادة
الترجيح
التخصيص
القدرة
الداعية
المتساويان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari position presented, what is the function of God's will (al-irāda)?
A) To create all things directly
B) To give preponderance and specification to one of two equals
C) To command good and forbid evil
D) To approve only good deeds

Key Takeaway
God's will is universal: everything that happens is willed by Him, and nothing occurs without His will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every event in your life—including your failures and sins—occurs by Allah's will, how does that reshape your approach to repentance and forgiveness? Will you own your responsibility while trusting that His will encompasses even your shortcomings, and what specific sin will you turn to Him for today?
?
The Salaf's statement 'مَا شَاءَ اللَّهُ كَانَ وَمَا لَمْ يَشَأْ لَمْ يَكُنْ' leaves no room for chance. Does your heart truly find peace in this absolute decree, or do you secretly resent it when life doesn't go your way? What worry will you consciously surrender to Him right now?
?
The passage defines will as giving preponderance to one of two equals. In your daily decisions, you constantly choose between options. Are you actively seeking Allah's guidance to preponderate what pleases Him, or do you rely on your own preference? Name one decision today where you will pause and ask for His will to prevail.

Refuting Mu'tazilite Objections on God's Will
Refutes Mu'tazilite arguments and explains the rebuttal of the mocking verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite arguments against the universality of God's will and the Ash'ari refutation, including the correct interpretation of the verse about mockery.

Core Idea
Mu'tazila wrongly exclude evil from God's will, but their reasoning collapses upon examination.

The Mu'tazila not only deny that God wills evil deeds, but they assert that He wills their opposites. This leads them to claim that most events in the kingdom are contrary to His will. Sanusi now refutes their arguments and clarifies the true meaning of certain Qur'anic verses they rely on.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite position
Read the Arabic passage from 'والمعتزلة لم يكتفوا' onward. Notice their claim: they cut off God's will from evil and affirm it attaches to opposites.
2
Identify the four Mu'tazilite arguments
The passage lists four reasons they give: willing evil is evil, punishment for what is willed is unjust, commanding what is not willed is foolish, and will entails command, approval, and love.
These arguments are meant to show that God cannot will evil.
3
Understand the Ash'ari refutation
Sanusi dismisses all these as corrupt (fāsid) and notes that the Mu'tazila have no support from the verses they cite—rather those verses speak of command, approval, and love, not will.
The key is distinguishing will (irāda) from command (amr), approval (riḍā), and love (maḥabba).
4
Examine the reply to the mocking verse
The passage concludes with the explanation that those who said 'If Allah had willed, we would not have associated partners' were mocking, and God's response shows their denial led to punishment.
Thus the verse does not negate universal will; it rebukes mockery.

How does the Ash'ari argument respond to the Mu'tazilite claim that willing evil is evil?

?

Will you now trust that whatever He wills has wisdom, even if He does not approve of the evil itself?
?
The Mu'tazila thought that willing evil would make God responsible for injustice. Are there areas in your life where you mistakenly think that if God wills something, He must approve of it? How will you realign your understanding of God's will and command, especially regarding your own sins?
?
The disbelievers said, 'If Allah had willed, we would not have associated partners' — a mockery. Have you ever used fate or God's will as an excuse to avoid responsibility? This lesson shows that God's will does not negate accountability. What is one area where you need to stop hiding behind 'it was God's will' and take action?

beginner
5 min
The Generality of Allah's Will: Statement and Proof
Sanusi states the doctrine of the generality of Allah's will and provides consensus and rational proofs, followed by a definition of will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that every occurring thing is willed by Allah, the proof from consensus and reason, and the precise definition of divine will as an attribute of preferential selection.

Core Idea
Allah wills everything that happens; nothing occurs outside His will.

Sanusi opens this investigation with a clear declaration of the Sunni doctrine: everything that occurs is willed by Allah, and conversely, whatever does not occur is not willed by Him. He then supports this with a two-fold proof: the consensus of the Salaf reflected in the famous hadith, and a rational argument based on Allah's creative power and knowledge. Finally, he defines the attribute of will precisely to avoid confusion with mere desire or command.

Guided Path
1
Read the statement and its inverse
The passage begins with the doctrine: every occurring thing (kā'in) is willed (murād) by Allah, and conversely. Understand that 'conversely' means that non-occurring things are not willed—this establishes a perfect correspondence between will and actuality.
This doctrine is a cornerstone of Sunni theology.
2
Examine the two proofs
First, notice the consensus argument: the Salaf agreed that 'what Allah wills occurs, and what He does not will does not occur.' Second, consider the rational proof: Allah is the Creator of all, so whatever occurs He creates and wills; and He knows what does not occur, so He does not will it.
The rational proof ties together creation and foreknowledge.
3
Understand the definition of will
Sanusi defines will (irādah) as an attribute whose function is preferential selection (tarjīḥ) and specification (takhṣīṣ) of one of two equal possibilities. This distinguishes Allah's will from human desire or command.
The definition mentions ability (qudrah) and motive (dā'iyah) as related concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من المبحث الثاني في عموم إرادته تعالى (في عموم إرادته الحق أن كل كائن مراد له، وبالعكس، لما أجمع عليه السلف أن ما شاء الله كان وما لم يشأ لم يكن، ولأنه خالق للكل مريده، وعالم بعدم وقوع ما لم يقع فلا يريده، لأن الإرادة (۱) صفة شأنها الترجيح والتخصيص لأحد المتساويين بالنظر إلى القدرة وصرف الداعية إلى الدليل ولو بالغير من الإرادة والنصوص الشاهدة بما ذكرنا أكثر من أن تحصى.
From the second investigation concerning the generality of His will – exalted is He –: (Concerning the generality of His will, the truth is that every occurring thing is willed by Him, and conversely, due to the consensus of the Salaf that what Allah wills occurs and what He does not will does not occur, and because He is the Creator of all, willing it, and He knows the non-occurrence of what does not occur, so He does not will it, because will is an attribute whose function is preferential selection and specification of one of two equal possibilities with respect to ability and directing the motive to the proof, even if by other than will, and the texts witnessing to what we have mentioned are too many to be counted.)
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage defines the central doctrine of the generality of Allah's will. The author begins by stating that every occurring thing (kā'in) is willed (murād) by Allah, and conversely, every non-occurring thing is not willed. He provides two proofs: first, the consensus of the Salaf expressed in the hadith 'What Allah wills occurs and what He does not will does not occur'; second, a rational argument: since Allah is the Creator of all and knows everything, whatever occurs He creates and wills, and whatever does not occur He knows would not occur and therefore does not will it. He then defines will (irādah) as an attribute that performs the function of preferring one of two equal possibilities (tarjīḥ wa takhṣīṣ) in relation to ability (qudrah) and directing the motive (dā'iyah) to the proof (dalīl), even if through something other than the will itself—this definition distinguishes Allah's will from mere desire. The passage concludes by noting that scriptural proofs supporting this are abundant.

Key Terms
إرادة
مراد
ترجيح
تخصيص
قدرة
داعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that evil events are not willed by Allah because He is good.
Correction: This passage teaches that every occurring thing, including evil, is willed by Allah. Will here means pre‑ordainment and preferential selection, not moral approval.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the primary function of the attribute of will (irādah)?
A) To create all possible things
B) To prefer one of two equal possibilities and specify it
C) To command good and forbid evil
D) To love what is good and hate evil

Key Takeaway
Everything that occurs in the universe is willed by Allah, as proven by consensus of the Salaf and reason, and will is defined as the attribute that prefers one possibility over another.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial or disappointment, do you truly believe that it is willed by Allah, or do you secretly think it is a mistake or unfair? What would change in your heart if you fully embraced that Allah wills everything that happens? Commit to saying 'What Allah wills has occurred' the next time something displeases you.
?
You know that Allah wills everything, including your sins. Does that lead you to feel complacent about sinning, or does it humble you because your sin is still your choice? Reflect on the balance between divine will and your responsibility. How can you use this belief to increase your gratitude when you obey and your repentance when you disobey?
?
The definition of Allah’s will includes preferential selection with wisdom. How does knowing that His will is not arbitrary give you trust in His decree? Think of a specific event in your life that seemed pointless. How does this lesson help you see wisdom in it? Write down one way you will actively trust His will this week.

The Ash'ari Doctrine of Universal Will and the Mu'tazila Objection
The author states the Ash'ari position and narrates the Mu'tazila opposition with an anecdote.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari claim that God's will encompasses every existing thing and the Mu'tazila challenge to that view.

Core Idea
God's will always matches what occurs; Mu'tazila say most human acts oppose God's will.

This passage presents the foundational Ash'ari belief about divine will: that it is related to everything that exists, not to non-existents. It also introduces the Mu'tazila opposition, who argue that God wills faith from the disbeliever and obedience from the sinner, making most actual events contrary to His will. An anecdote illustrates the clash.

Guided Path
1
Read the Ash'ari claim
Read the opening statement carefully. Note that God's will is related to every being and the hadith cited.
The hadith 'ما شاء كان' is key.
2
Note the respectful wording
Understand why some Ash'aris avoid explicitly saying 'God wills disbelief' out of reverence, similar to avoiding 'Creator of filth'.
This is a subtlety, not a denial.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila objection
Identify the Mu'tazila view: God wills faith from the disbeliever even if it doesn't occur, and does not will disbelief. Consider the sarcastic remark about a village chief.
4
Reflect on the anecdote
Read the exchange between Qadi Abd al-Jabbar and Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini. What does each statement imply about divine will?
This dramatizes the disagreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مذهب أهل الحق أن إرادة الله تعالى متعلقة بكل كائن غير متعلقة بما ليس بكائن ، على ما اشتهر من السلف، وروي مرفوعاً إلى النبي عليه السلام «إن ما شاء كان وما لم يشأ لم يكن لكن منهم من منع التفصيل بأن يقال إنه يريد الكفر والظلم والفسق كما في الخلق يقال إنه خالق الكل، ولا يقال : خالق القاذورات والقردة والخنازير، وخالفت المعتزلة في الشرور والقبائح، فزعموا أنه يريد من الكافر الإيمان ، وإن لم يقع لا الكفر وإن وقع، وكذا يريد من الفاسق الطاعة لا الفسق، حتى أن أكثر ما يقع من العباد خلاف مراده . والظاهر أنه لا يصبر على ذلك رئيس قرية من عباده . حكى أنه دخل القاضي عبد الجبار داراً للصاحب بن عباد فرأى الأستاذ أبي إسحق الاسفرائني . فقال سبحان من تنزه عن الفحشاء. فقال الأستاذ على الفور : سبحان من لا يجري في ملكه إلا ما يشاء والتقصي عن ذلك، بأنه أراد من العباد الإيمان والطاعة برغبتهم واختيارهم، فلا عجز ولا نقيصة ولا مغلوبية له في عدم وقوع ذلك، كالملك إذا أراد دخول القوم داره رغبة واختياراً لا كرهاً واضطراراً، فلم يدخلوا ليس بشيء لأنه لم يقع هذا المراد ووقع مرادات العبيد والخدم، وكفى بهذا نقيصة ومغلوبية لنا على إرادته للكائنات أنه خالق لها بقدرته من غير إكراه، فيكون مريداً لها ضرورة أن الإرادة هي الصفة المرجحة لأحد طرفي الفعل والترك، وعلى عدم إرادته لما ليس بكائن أنه علم عدم وقوعه فعلم استحالته لاستحالة انقلاب علمه جهلاً والعالم باستحالة الشيء لا يريده البتة.
The doctrine of the people of truth is that the will of God Almighty is related to every being, not related to what is not a being, according to what is known from the predecessors, and it is narrated raised to the Prophet عليه السلام: 'Indeed what He wills is, and what He does not will is not.' But some of them forbid the detail that it is said that He wills disbelief, injustice, and sin, just as in creation it is said that He is the Creator of all, and it is not said: Creator of filth, monkeys, and pigs. And the Mu'tazila opposed in matters of evil and ugly things, claiming that He wills from the disbeliever faith, even if it does not occur, not disbelief even if it occurs, and similarly He wills from the sinner obedience, not sin, to the point that most of what occurs from servants is contrary to His will. And it is apparent that no village chief would tolerate that from his servants. It is narrated that Qadi Abd al-Jabbar entered a house of al-Sahib ibn Abbad and saw the master Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini. He said: 'Glorified is He who is free from obscenity.' The master immediately said: 'Glorified is He from whose dominion nothing runs except what He wills.' And the investigation of that: that He willed from the servants faith and obedience by their desire and choice, so there is no incapacity, deficiency, or being overcome for Him in the non-occurrence of that, like a king who wants the people to enter his house by desire and choice, not by force and compulsion, and they do not enter; it is nothing because this desired thing did not occur and the desires of the servants and slaves occurred. And sufficient is this as a deficiency and being overcome for us over His will. For beings, He creates them by His power without compulsion, so He is willing for them necessarily because the will is the attribute that gives preference to one of the two sides of action and abandonment, and regarding His not willing what is not a being, it is because He knew its non-occurrence, so He knew its impossibility because of the impossibility of His knowledge turning into ignorance, and one who knows the impossibility of a thing does not will it at all.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage presents the core Ash'ari doctrine on divine will: God's will encompasses every existing thing and does not apply to non-existent things. The hadith 'ما شاء كان وما لم يشأ لم يكن' is cited as proof. However, some Ash'aris avoid explicitly saying 'God wills disbelief' out of reverence, just as they avoid saying 'Creator of filth' even though they affirm God creates all. The Mu'tazila oppose this, claiming God wills faith from the disbeliever and obedience from the sinner, meaning most actual acts oppose His will. The author sarcastically remarks that no village chief would tolerate such a situation. An anecdote follows: Qadi Abd al-Jabbar (a Mu'tazili leader) exclaims that God is free from obscenity, and Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini retorts that nothing occurs in His dominion except what He wills. The author then explains that God wills faith and obedience from humans by their free choice, so His not compelling them is not a deficiency. The analogy of a king wanting subjects to enter his house willingly illustrates that their refusal does not indicate the king's impotence. Finally, a rational argument: since God creates all beings by His power, He must will them—will being the attribute that prefers one side of action—and since He knows what will not occur, He knows its impossibility and thus does not will it.

Key Terms
إرادة
كائن
المعتزلة
خالق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Ash'ari view implies God explicitly wills evil in the same sense as good.
Correction: Ash'aris affirm God wills all events, but many avoid explicit wording out of reverence, similar to avoiding 'Creator of filth'.

Checkpoint
Why do some Ash'aris avoid saying 'God wills disbelief'?
A) They deny that God wills evil acts.
B) Out of reverence, similar to avoiding 'Creator of filth'.
C) Because disbelief is not within God's power.
D) Because will only applies to good deeds.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari doctrine affirms God's universal will, while the Mu'tazila restrict it to good acts only.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever struggled with the idea that God wills everything that happens, including evil and suffering? How does this lesson's claim that God's will is attached to every 'being' challenge or comfort you? What would it mean for your trust in God if you believed that most of your actions oppose His will?
?
When you face personal failure or sin, do you tend to excuse yourself by saying 'God didn't will it' or do you accept that it occurred within His will? How would your repentance change if you truly believed that nothing happens outside of what God wills?

Defending Divine Will: The King Analogy and Rational Proof
The author provides a rational defense of the Ash'ari doctrine using an analogy and logical argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'ari theologians defend the consistency of God's will with human free choice using an analogy and a rational argument.

Core Idea
God's will for good acts by free choice is no deficiency; His will for all beings follows from His creative power.

After presenting the Mu'tazila objection, the author provides a detailed defense. He explains that God's will for faith and obedience from humans is by their own free choice, so its non-occurrence does not imply divine impotence. A king's desire for subjects to enter his house willingly illustrates this. Then a rational proof shows that since God creates all beings, He must will them, and since He knows non-beings are impossible, He does not will them.

Guided Path
1
Understand the key point: free choice
Read how God wills faith and obedience by human desire and choice, not coercion.
Non-occurrence does not imply God's incapacity.
2
Analyze the king analogy
Consider the analogy of a king wanting subjects to enter his house willingly. What does it illustrate about God's will?
The king's will is for voluntary action; their refusal doesn't make him weak.
3
Follow the rational proof
Understand the two parts: (1) God creates beings, so He must will them (will is the attribute that prefers one side); (2) God's knowledge of non-occurrence makes it impossible, so He does not will it.
This argument connects creation, will, and knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Objection and Answer on Willing the Known Non-Occurrence
The author responds to an objection that God could will something He knows will not happen.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari response distinguishes between will (irada) and mere wishful thinking (tamanni) in response to an objection about God's will.

Core Idea
True will specifies and prefers; wishful thinking is not will.

In this passage, the author addresses an objection that God could will something He knows will never happen. The objector uses an analogy of a prophet's foretelling to argue that one can will something known not to occur. The author's answer hinges on a crucial distinction between genuine volition and mere wishful thinking, clarifying the nature of divine will.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Identify the two parts of the objection: (1) what is contrary to known is within God's power and can be willed, (2) the analogy of the prophet's report.
The objector is trying to show that willing the known non-occurrence is possible.
2
Identify the objector's analogy
The objector gives an example: a truthful prophet informs a person that someone will kill him; that person knows the killing will happen but does not will it; instead he wills the killer's life. The objector uses this to argue that one can will something that will not occur.
This analogy is meant to parallel God's will and knowledge.
3
Understand the author's answer
The author responds by stating that the example is not a case of will (irada) but of wishful thinking (tamanni). True will is the attribute that specifies and gives preference. Therefore, the objection fails because it confuses two different concepts.
Key terms: إرادة (will) and تمن (wishful thinking).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترض بأن خلاف المعلوم مقدور له في نفسه، والمقدور إذا كان متعلق المصلحة يجوز أن يكون مراداً وإن علم أنه لا يقع البتة وبأن من أخبره النبي الصادق بأن فلاناً يقتله البتة يعلم ذلك قطعاً مع أنه لا يريد قتله بل حياته . والجواب أن هذا تمن لا إرادة، فإنها الصفة التي شأنها التخصيص والترجيح
And it was objected that what is contrary to what is known is within His power in itself, and the capable thing, if it is related to benefit, may be willed even if it is known that it will never occur, and because one whom the truthful prophet informed that so-and-so will certainly kill him knows that decisively, yet he does not will his death but rather his life. The answer is that this is wishful thinking, not will, for the will is the attribute whose function is specification and preference.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage contains an objection to the Ash'ari claim that God does not will what He knows will not occur. The objector argues: something known not to happen is still within God's power; if it has benefit, it could be willed even if it is known to never occur. The example: a prophet informs someone that a certain person will kill him; the person knows the killing will happen but does not will it; rather he wills the killer's life. So, the objector says, God could will something He knows will not occur. The answer distinguishes true will (إرادة) from wishful thinking (تمن). True will is the attribute that specifies and gives preference; it is not mere desire. The example given is a case of wishful thinking, not volitional will. Therefore the objection fails.

Key Terms
إرادة
تمن
تخصيص
ترجيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objector's analogy of the prophet's report successfully shows that God could will something He knows will not occur.
Correction: The analogy actually demonstrates wishful thinking (تمن), not genuine will (إرادة), because true will necessarily involves specification and preference, leading to the occurrence of the willed thing. Thus, the objection is invalid.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the objection based on the prophet's report?
A) He denies the prophet can foretell future events.
B) He says the example shows wishful thinking, not genuine will.
C) He claims God's knowledge is different from human knowledge.
D) He argues that the killer's death is not in God's power.

Key Takeaway
Divine will is not mere desire but an attribute of specification and preference, so God's will always aligns with what He knows will occur.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there areas of your life where you are merely wishing for something good to happen (tamanni) rather than sincerely willing it (irada) by taking action and submitting to Allah's decree? What would true will look like in those situations?
?
When you face a difficulty that Allah has decreed, do you find yourself wishing it were otherwise, or do you actively specify and prefer what He has chosen for you? How can you train your heart to align your will with His?
?
The objector used an analogy of a person who knows he will be killed but wills the killer's life. In your own life, when you know something is inevitable by Allah's decree, do you submit your will to His, or do you secretly wish for a different outcome? What is one concrete step you can take today to replace wishful thinking with true submission?

Defining Divine Will and Distinguishing It from Wishful Thinking
Sanusi defines divine will and answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of divine will (irada) as the attribute of preponderance and how it differs from mere wishful thinking (tamanni).

Core Idea
Divine will is not a wish; it specifies and necessitates what actually occurs.

This passage from the Ash'ari tradition defines the essence of divine will. The author clarifies that God's will is not a passive desire; it actively determines what exists. Understanding this distinction is vital to avoid the common confusion between willing something and merely wishing for it. We will explore the definition, the proof that God wills only what occurs, and the rebuttal to the objection that impossible things could still be willed.

Guided Path
1
Understand the king analogy
The king analogy illustrates that if God's will were ineffective, it would imply deficiency. Note how the author sets up the reductio ad absurdum.
The analogy is crucial for the objection.
2
Learn the definition of irada
The passage defines irada as the attribute that preponderates one side of action or inaction. This is the core definition that underlies the entire argument.
3
Distinguish irada from tamanni
The objection tries to separate will from actuality, but the answer emphasizes that true will entails specification and realization. Simply wanting something without it happening is wishful thinking, not divine will.
This distinction preserves God's power and wisdom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوع ذلك، كالملك إذا أراد دخول القوم داره رغبة واختياراً لا كرهاً واضطراراً، فلم يدخلوا ليس بشيء لأنه لم يقع هذا المراد ووقع مرادات العبيد والخدم، وكفى بهذا نقيصة ومغلوبية لنا على إرادته للكائنات أنه خالق لها بقدرته من غير إكراه، فيكون مريداً لها ضرورة أن الإرادة هي الصفة المرجحة لأحد طرفي الفعل والترك، وعلى عدم إرادته لما ليس بكائن أنه علم عدم وقوعه فعلم استحالته لاستحالة انقلاب علمه جهلاً والعالم باستحالة الشيء لا يريده البتة. واعترض بأن خلاف المعلوم مقدور له في نفسه، والمقدور إذا كان متعلق المصلحة يجوز أن يكون مراداً وإن علم أنه لا يقع البتة وبأن من أخبره النبي الصادق بأن فلاناً يقتله البتة يعلم ذلك قطعاً مع أنه لا يريد قتله بل حياته . والجواب أن هذا تمن لا إرادة، فإنها الصفة التي شأنها التخصيص والترجيح
The occurrence of that, like the king when he wants the people to enter his house willingly and by choice, not by force and compulsion, and they do not enter, that is nothing because this desired thing did not occur and the desires of the slaves and servants occurred. This is enough as a deficiency and overpowering of us over His will for existent things that He is their Creator by His power without compulsion. So He is willing them necessarily because will (al-irada) is the attribute that preponderates one of the two sides of action and abandonment. And [the proof] for His not willing what does not occur is that He knew its non-occurrence, so He knew its impossibility, because it is impossible for His knowledge to turn into ignorance, and one who knows the impossibility of a thing never wills it. And it was objected that what contradicts known [reality] is within His power in itself, and what is within [His] power, if it is connected to benefit, may be willed even if He knows that it will never occur, and [also] by the case of someone whom a truthful prophet informed that a certain person will surely kill him, he knows that with certainty, yet he does not want to kill him but rather his life. And the answer is that this is wishful thinking (tamanni), not will (irada), for it is the attribute whose function is specification and preponderance.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage defines divine will and refutes a Mu'tazilite objection. It begins with an analogy: a king wants his people to enter his house willingly, but they do not enter; the author argues that if God's will were not realized, it would imply deficiency. Then he states that God necessarily wills whatever occurs, because will is the attribute that preponderates one side of action or inaction. For what does not occur, God does not will it because He knows its impossibility; His knowledge cannot become ignorance, and one who knows a thing impossible never wills it. The objection claims that something known not to occur could still be willed if it has benefit, citing the example of a person who is informed by a truthful prophet that a certain man will kill him; that person knows the murder will happen but does not will it; rather he wills the man's life. The answer distinguishes true will (irada) from wishful thinking (tamanni). True will entails actual specification (takhsis) and preponderance (tarjih), not mere desire without realization.

Key Terms
الإرادة
التمني
الترجيح
التخصيص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God can will something without it happening, like a wish.
Correction: The passage clarifies that divine will necessarily results in its object; otherwise it would be deficiency. True will is not mere desire; it is the attribute of specification and preponderance that brings about actuality.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the defining characteristic of divine will (irada)?
A) It is a mere wish that may not be realized.
B) It is the attribute that specifies and preponderates one side of action or inaction.
C) It is identical to knowledge.
D) It is only for good things.

Key Takeaway
Divine will is the attribute that necessarily specifies and brings about what occurs; it is not a mere wish.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you desire something that you know will not happen, do you ever comfort yourself by saying 'maybe God wills it'? If His will necessarily specifies and brings about what occurs, what does that mean about your desires that remain unfulfilled? Could it be that they were never truly willed by God? How does that change your understanding of your own wishes? Take a moment to repent from treating God's will as a tool for your fantasies.
?
In your life, have you confused wishful thinking with divine will? Are there things you assume God must want based on your own hopes, even when they have not occurred? How can you train yourself to distinguish between your tamanni and the actual irada that is always effective? Commit to studying God's attributes with humility.
?
The passage says that one who knows impossibility never wills it. God knows all impossibilities. But do you, as a human, sometimes will things that are impossible or against reality? Reflect on the arrogance of wishing against God's decree. How can you align your will with what He actually brings about?

Quranic Evidence for God's Universal Will
The author presents a series of Quranic verses demonstrating God's absolute will over guidance and misguidance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Quranic basis for the Sunni belief that God's will encompasses all things, including faith and guidance.

Core Idea
The Quran repeatedly affirms that God alone guides and misguides whom He wills.

Before engaging with the Mu'tazilite counterarguments, the author establishes the overwhelming Quranic evidence for God's absolute will over human faith. These verses are presented as clear and numerous, leaving no room for doubt that God's will is sovereign over all, including the act of belief.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic verses carefully
Read the Arabic verses and their translation. Notice how each verse directly attributes guidance or misguidance to God's will.
Pay attention to the verbs 'yashaa' (wills) and 'yurid' (intends).
2
Reflect on the author's claim
The author begins by stating that the evidence is 'clearer than to be hidden.' Consider why he emphasizes this before presenting the verses.
3
Identify the common theme
All the verses share the theme that God's will is decisive in matters of guidance. Make a list of the key phrases that indicate this.
For example: 'لا يؤمنون إلا أن يشاء الله' and 'إنك لا تهدي من أحببت ولكن الله يهدي من يشاء'.
4
Prepare for the refutation
The lesson ends with the author's assertion that the Mu'tazila have corrupt interpretations. This sets up the next lesson on their specific arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الآيات والأحاديث فى هذا الباب، فأظهر من أن تخفى، وأكثر من أن تحصى، ولو أننا نزلنا إليهم الملائكة، وكلمهم الموتى وحشرنا عليهم كل شيء قبلاً ما كانوا ليؤمنوا إلا أن يشاء الله فمن يرد الله أن يهديه يشرح صدره للإسلام ومن يرد أن يضله يجعل صدره ضيقاً حرجاً قل لا ينفعكم نصحي إن أردت أن أنصح لكم إن كان الله يريد أن يغويكم ولو شاء الله لجمعهم على الهدى ولو شاء لهداكم أجمعين أولئك الذين لم يرد الله أن يطهر قلوبهم إنما يريد الله ليعذبهم بها في الحياة الدنيا وتزهق أنفسهم وهم كافرون إنك لا تهدي من أحببت ولكن الله يهدي من يشاء والله يدعو إلى دار السلام ويهدي من يشاء إلى صراط مستقيم. وللمعتزلة فيها تأويلات فاسدة، وتعسفات باردة يتعجب منها الناظر، ويتحقق أنهم فيها محجوجون، وبوهقها مخنوقون، ولظهور الحق في هذه المسألة يكاد عامتهم به يعترفون ويجري على ألسنتهم أن ما لم يشأ الله لا يكون. ثم العمدة القصوى لهم في الجواب عن أكثر الآيات حمل المشيئة على مشيئة القسر والإلجاء، وحين سئلوا معناها تحيروا . فقال العلاف معناها خلق الإيمان والهداية فيهم بلا اختيار منهم . ورد بأن المؤمن حينئذ يكون هو الله لا العبد على ما زعمتم في إلزامنا حين قلنا بأن الخالق هو الله تبارك وتعالى وعز وجل مع قدرتنا واختيارنا وكسبنا، فكيف بدون ذلك ؟ فقال الجبائي : معناها خلق العلم الضروري بصحة الإيمان، وإقامة الدلائل المثبتة لذلك العلم الضروري. ورد بأن هذا لا يكون إيماناً والكلام فيه على أن في بعض الآيات دلالة على أنهم لو رأوا كل آية ودليل لا يؤمنون البتة فقال ابنه أبو هاشم معناها : أن خلق لهم العلم الضروري بأنهم لو لم يؤمنوا لعذبوا عذاباً شديداً وهذا أيضاً فاسد، لأن كثيراً من الكفار كانوا يعلمون ذلك، وكذا إبليس ولا يؤمنون على أنه قال تعالى ولو شئنا لآتينا كل نفس هداها ولكن حق القول منى لأملأن جهنم من الجنة والناس أجمعين يشهد بفساد تأويلاتهم لدلالته على أنه إنما لم يهد الكل لسبق الحكم بملء جهنم ولاخفاء في أن الإيمان والهداية بطريق الجبر لا يخرجهم عن استحقاق جهنم سيما عند المعتزلة. وتمام تفصيل هذا المقام وتزييف تأويلاتهم في المطولات وكتب التأويلات والمعتزلة تمسكوا في دعواهم بوجوه :
As for the verses and hadiths on this topic, they are clearer than to be hidden, and more numerous than to be counted. 'Even if We sent down angels to them, and the dead spoke to them, and We gathered before them every thing face to face, they would not believe unless God wills.' 'So whoever God wills to guide, He expands his breast to Islam; and whoever He wills to misguide, He makes his breast tight and constricted.' 'Say: My advice will not benefit you, even if I desire to advise you, if God intends to lead you astray.' 'And if God had willed, He would have gathered them to guidance.' 'And if He had willed, He would have guided you all.' 'Those are the ones for whom God did not will to purify their hearts; God intends to punish them in the life of this world, and their souls will depart while they are disbelievers.' 'Indeed, you cannot guide whom you love, but God guides whom He wills.' 'And God invites to the abode of peace and guides whom He wills to a straight path.' And the Mu'tazila have corrupt interpretations (ta'wilat) regarding them, and cold distortions that the observer marvels at, and one realizes that they are refuted therein, and in their noose they are choked. Due to the manifest truth in this matter, most of them admit it, and it runs on their tongues that what God does not will does not occur. Then their main reliance in answering most of the verses is to interpret 'will' (mashi'a) as the will of compulsion and coercion. When asked about its meaning, they were confused. Al-'Allaf said: 'Its meaning is creating faith and guidance in them without their choice.' It was refuted by [saying] that then the believer would be God, not the servant, as you claimed in your argument against us when we said that the Creator is God Almighty with our power, choice, and acquisition, so how [could it be] without that? Al-Jubba'i said: 'Its meaning is creating necessary knowledge of the truth of faith and establishing proofs for that necessary knowledge.' It was refuted that this is not faith, and the discussion is that some verses indicate that even if they saw every sign and proof, they would not believe at all. So his son Abu Hashim said: 'Its meaning is that He creates for them necessary knowledge that if they do not believe, they will be severely punished.' This is also corrupt, because many of the disbelievers knew that, as did Iblis, and they do not believe. Moreover, He said, 'And if We had willed, We would have given every soul its guidance, but the word from Me will be fulfilled: I will surely fill Hell with jinn and people all together.' This testifies to the corruption of their interpretations because it indicates that He did not guide all due to the prior decree to fill Hell. And there is no hiddenness in that faith and guidance by way of compulsion does not remove them from deserving Hell, especially according to the Mu'tazila. And full detail of this topic and the refutation of their interpretations is in the lengthy works and books of interpretations. And the Mu'tazila cling in their claim to some arguments:
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage presents abundant Quranic evidence for God's absolute will over faith and guidance, then refutes Mu'tazilite interpretations. It first cites multiple verses that clearly attribute guidance and misguidance to God's will. Then it states that the Mu'tazila have corrupt interpretations (ta'wilat) and distort these verses. It notes that even most Mu'tazila admit the principle that what God does not will does not occur. Their main argument is to interpret 'will' (mashi'a) in these verses as the will of compulsion (qasr) and coercion (ilja'). When asked for the precise meaning, they became confused. The author then presents three specific proposals from leading Mu'tazilite scholars and refutes each: (1) Al-'Allaf claimed it means creating faith without choice; refuted because then the believer would be God, not the servant, contradicting their own principle that God creates with our acquisition. (2) Al-Jubba'i claimed it means creating necessary knowledge of faith and providing proofs; refuted because this is not genuine faith, and some verses show that even seeing every sign, they would not believe. (3) Abu Hashim claimed it means creating necessary knowledge that they would be severely punished if they do not believe; refuted because many disbelievers (like Iblis) had that knowledge yet did not believe. Moreover, Quran 32:13 is cited: God says He did not guide all because He had decreed to fill Hell, showing that the lack of guidance is not due to compulsion but a prior decree. The passage concludes that full detail is in other works, and the Mu'tazila cling to some arguments (which are not elaborated here).

Key Terms
المشيئة
الهدى
الضلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the verses about God's will in guidance only refer to compulsion, not to His absolute sovereignty.
Correction: The verses show that God's will is the primary cause of guidance, but they do not negate human responsibility; the refutation later demonstrates that the Mu'tazilite interpretation of compulsion is flawed.

Checkpoint
According to the first verse quoted (Quran 6:111), what would cause the disbelievers to believe?
A) Seeing angels and the dead speaking
B) Seeing every sign
C) Only if God wills
D) Hearing the Prophet's advice

Key Takeaway
The Quran repeatedly affirms that God's will is the decisive factor in guidance, leaving no doubt about His absolute sovereignty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that your own faith is entirely due to God's will, or do you secretly credit your own intelligence, upbringing, or efforts? In what moments do you act as if you are the one who guides yourself?
?
When you see others who are not guided—family members, friends, or strangers—do you feel self-righteous or frustrated, forgetting that only God could have guided them and that you are no different? How would this change your attitude toward them?
?
The Prophet himself could not guide his beloved uncle. How does that reality shake your reliance on your own da'wah, parenting, or teaching? Are you trusting God's will more than your persuasion skills? If not, what would it look like to truly surrender the outcome to Him?

Refutation of Mu'tazilite Interpretations of Divine Will
The author refutes three Mu'tazilite interpretations of divine will as compulsion, citing logical flaws and a concluding Quranic verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main Mu'tazilite interpretations of the verses on God's will and their refutations by the author.

Core Idea
Mu'tazilite attempts to explain away God's will as mere compulsion are internally inconsistent and fail to account for the Quranic text.

After presenting the Quranic evidence, the author now directly confronts the Mu'tazilite strategy of interpreting 'will' as compulsion (qasr) and coercion (ilja'). He recounts three specific proposals from leading Mu'tazilite scholars and refutes each, showing that they distort the meaning of faith and contradict the Quran.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazilite main interpretative strategy
Read the opening sentences about their main reliance on interpreting 'mashi'a' as the will of compulsion and coercion. Notice how the author describes their confusion when asked for details.
The term 'qasr' and 'ilja' are key; they imply forcing someone against their will.
2
Study the three specific proposals
The passage presents three Mu'tazilite views: Al-'Allaf's, Al-Jubba'i's, and Abu Hashim's. For each, identify the proposed meaning and the author's refutation.
Use the guided breakdown to help track each view.
3
Analyze the refutations
For each refutation, note the logical argument. For example, Al-'Allaf's view equates the believer with God; Al-Jubba'i's view reduces faith to necessary knowledge; Abu Hashim's view is contradicted by the fact that many disbelievers already know the punishment.
4
Reflect on the concluding Quranic verse
The author cites Quran 32:13 as further evidence against the Mu'tazilite position. Understand how this verse shows that God's withholding of guidance is due to His decree, not compulsion.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage presents abundant Quranic evidence for God's absolute will over faith and guidance, then refutes Mu'tazilite interpretations. It first cites multiple verses that clearly attribute guidance and misguidance to God's will. Then it states that the Mu'tazila have corrupt interpretations (ta'wilat) and distort these verses. It notes that even most Mu'tazila admit the principle that what God does not will does not occur. Their main argument is to interpret 'will' (mashi'a) in these verses as the will of compulsion (qasr) and coercion (ilja'). When asked for the precise meaning, they became confused. The author then presents three specific proposals from leading Mu'tazilite scholars and refutes each: (1) Al-'Allaf claimed it means creating faith without choice; refuted because then the believer would be God, not the servant, contradicting their own principle that God creates with our acquisition. (2) Al-Jubba'i claimed it means creating necessary knowledge of faith and providing proofs; refuted because this is not genuine faith, and some verses show that even seeing every sign, they would not believe. (3) Abu Hashim claimed it means creating necessary knowledge that they would be severely punished if they do not believe; refuted because many disbelievers (like Iblis) had that knowledge yet did not believe. Moreover, Quran 32:13 is cited: God says He did not guide all because He had decreed to fill Hell, showing that the lack of guidance is not due to compulsion but a prior decree. The passage concludes that full detail is in other works, and the Mu'tazila cling to some arguments (which are not elaborated here).

Key Terms
المشيئة
التأويل
القسر
الإلجاء
العلم الضروري
الجبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Mu'tazilite interpretation of 'will' as compulsion is a valid way to reconcile God's will with human free will.
Correction: The author shows that this interpretation leads to incoherence and is refuted by the very verses the Mu'tazila try to explain.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main refutation of Al-'Allaf's view that God creates faith without human choice?
A) It contradicts the Quranic verses
B) Then the believer would be God, not the servant
C) It makes faith involuntary
D) It denies human responsibility

Key Takeaway
The three Mu'tazilite attempts to interpret 'will' as compulsion fail logically and are contradicted by the Quran itself, affirming that God's will is sovereign without implying coercion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses like 'And if We had willed, We would have given every soul its guidance' (32:13), do you feel a tension between God's absolute control and your personal responsibility? How does this lesson's refutation of compulsion free you from using divine will as an excuse for spiritual negligence?
?
Think of a time when you attributed your own failure to seek knowledge or guidance to 'God's will.' How does the author's argument that God's will is not compulsion expose the dishonesty of that excuse? What will you change today to take ownership of your faith?
?
The Mu'tazila twisted clear verses to fit their theology. Where in your own life have you twisted a clear command of God to avoid personal accountability? How will you now submit to the plain meaning of the Quran without evasion?

First Objection: Willing Evil is Evil
Al-Taftazani states and refutes the first objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against divine will for evil and al-Taftazani's refutation that nothing from God is truly evil.

Core Idea
Nothing from Allah is evil; hidden wisdom lies behind apparent evil.

The first objection claims that since willing evil is evil, God cannot will evil. Al-Taftazani refutes by stating that nothing from God is evil; the apparent evilness is due to our limited understanding. This establishes a key Ash'ari principle: God's actions are not subject to moral evaluation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and refutation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note how the objection is framed and the direct response.
Notice the concise style: objection followed by 'رد'.
2
Understand the objection's logic
Why would someone argue that willing evil is evil? Consider the ethical assumption that a good being cannot will evil.
This is a common critique against the Ash'ari position.
3
Grasp the refutation's premise
Al-Taftazani denies that God's action can be called evil. The core idea: divine actions are beyond human moral categories.
The phrase 'لا قبيح منه' is decisive.
4
Reflect on the hidden goodness
Even if something appears evil to us, it has a 'وجه حسن' hidden from us. This invokes the idea of divine wisdom.
This is not a proof but a defense of divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : أن إرادة القبيح قبيحة، والله منزه عن القبائح. ورد بأنه لا قبيح منه غاية الأمر أنه يخفي علينا وجه حسنه الثاني: أن العقاب على ما أراده ظلم ورد بالمنع فإنه تصرف في ملكه الثالث: أن الأمر بما لا يراد والنهي عما يراد سفه ورد بالمنع إذ ربما لا يكون غرض الآمر الإتيان بالمأمور .. كالسيد إذا أمر العبد امتحاناً له هل يطيعه أم لا؟ فإنه لا يريد شيئاً من الطاعة والعصيان، أو اعتذاراً عن ضربه بأنه لا يطيعه فإنه يريد منه العصيان، وكالمكره على الأمر بنهب أمواله، وكذا النهي. فإن قيل : مأمور السلطان يبادر إلى المأمور به معللاً بأنه مراد السلطان قلنا : لا مطلقاً. بل إذا ظهر أمارة الإرادة ، وإنما يعلل مطلقاً بالأمر والإشارة والحكم. الرابع : لو كان الكفر مراداً الله تعالى لكان طاعة لأن معناها تحصيل مراد المطاع لدورانه معه وجوداً وعدماً. ورد بالمنع بل هي موافقة الأمر وإنما تدور معه علمت الإرادة أو لم تعلم . الخامس : لو كان مراداً لكان قضاء فوجب الرضاء به والملازمة وبطلات اللازم ورد بأنه مقضى لاقضاء، ووجوب الرضا إنما هو بالقضاء دون المقضى ، ودعوى أن المراد بالقضاء الواجب الرضا به هو المقضى من المحن والبلايا والمصائب والرزايا لا الصفة الذاتية لله تعالى بهت بل هو الخلق والحكم والتقدير. وقد يجاب بأن الرضا بالكفر من حيث انه من قضاء الله تعالى طاعة ، ولا من هذه الحيثية كفر وفيه نظر .
First: that willing evil is evil, and God is transcendent above evils. It is answered that nothing from Him is evil; at most, its aspect of goodness is hidden from us. Second: that punishing for what He wills is injustice. It is answered with denial, for it is disposal in His dominion. Third: that commanding what is not willed and forbidding what is willed is foolishness. It is answered with denial, for sometimes the commander's purpose is not the performance of the commanded action — like the master who commands his servant to test whether he will obey or not, for he does not will anything of obedience or disobedience; or as an excuse for striking him because he does not obey, for he wills disobedience from him; and like one compelled to command the plundering of his wealth. The same applies to prohibition. If it is said: the subject of the sultan hastens to the commanded action, explaining it as being the sultan's will. We say: not absolutely, but only when an indication of will appears; and it is explained absolutely by command, indication, and judgment. Fourth: if disbelief were willed by God Almighty, it would be obedience, because the meaning of obedience is achieving the will of the one obeyed, as it goes with it in existence and non-existence. It is answered with denial; rather, it is compliance with command, and it goes with it whether will is known or not known. Fifth: if it were willed, it would be decree, so satisfaction with it would be obligatory — the implication and the consequence are false. It is answered that it is decreed, not decree; and the obligation of satisfaction is with the decree, not the decreed. The claim that what is meant by decree with which satisfaction is obligatory is the decreed of trials, calamities, afflictions, and misfortunes — not the essential attribute of God Almighty — is an absurdity; rather, it is creation, judgment, and predestination. And it may be answered that satisfaction with disbelief inasmuch as it is from God's decree is obedience, and not from this aspect it is disbelief. But there is consideration in this.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage enumerates five objections against the Ash'ari position that God wills all things, including evil, and provides refutations. The first objection states that willing evil is evil, and God is free from evils. The refutation explains that nothing from God is evil; the apparent evilness is due to our ignorance of its good aspect. The second objection claims that punishing for what God wills would be unjust. The refutation denies this, as God's actions are disposal of His own dominion. The third objection asserts that commanding what God does not will and forbidding what He does will is foolish. The refutation provides counterexamples: a master may command to test obedience without willing a specific outcome, or as an excuse for punishment. The objection that the sultan's subject hastens because it is the sultan's will is answered: he only does so when there is an indication of will, but the general inference is from command itself. The fourth objection argues that if disbelief were willed by God, it would be obedience, since obedience is achieving the will of the obeyed. The refutation distinguishes: obedience is compliance with command, not necessarily with will. The fifth objection claims that if disbelief were willed, it would be God's decree, so one must be satisfied with it. The refutation distinguishes between decree (qada') and the decreed (maqdi): the obligation of satisfaction is with the decree itself, not the specific decreed events like disbelief. A further response suggests that satisfaction with disbelief as from God's decree is obedience, but not from that aspect it remains disbelief, though this is considered problematic.

Key Terms
إرادة القبيح
القبيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God wills something bad, then He is evil or unjust.
Correction: Al-Taftazani clarifies that there is no evil from God; His actions are always good, though we may not perceive the goodness.

Checkpoint
According to al-Taftazani's refutation, why is the objection that willing evil is evil invalid?
A) Because God can will evil but is still good.
B) Because nothing from God is evil; the good aspect is hidden.
C) Because evil is not real.
D) Because God's will is different from human will.

Key Takeaway
Nothing from Allah is evil; perceived evilness stems from our limited understanding.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face hardship or see evil in the world, do you instinctively question God's wisdom? Can you name one specific trial where you struggled to see any hidden good? What would it take for you to genuinely trust that 'nothing from Him is evil' — even when your heart denies it?
?
If everything from Allah has a hidden aspect of goodness, what does that demand from you when you encounter suffering in your own life? Will you commit to pausing before complaining, and instead ask Allah to show you the hidden wisdom?

Second Objection: Punishing for Willed Action is Injustice
Al-Taftazani states and refutes the second objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection that punishing for what God wills would be unjust and al-Taftazani's refutation based on divine sovereignty.

Core Idea
God's actions are His disposal in His dominion; no one can call it injustice.

The second objection argues that if God wills an evil act and then punishes for it, that would be unjust. Al-Taftazani refutes by denying the premise: God's actions are exercises of His absolute sovereignty over His creation, not subject to human notions of justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and refutation
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the objection and the simple 'ورد بالمنع'.
2
Understand the objection
The objector assumes that punishment for something God Himself willed is unjust, like a ruler punishing for his own command.
3
Grasp the refutation
Al-Taftazani denies the claim of injustice: God is the absolute owner of all things, so His actions are like a master disposing of his property.
Key phrase: 'تصرف في ملكه'.
4
Reflect on divine sovereignty
This refutation relies on God's complete ownership and authority. Nothing He does can be considered unjust because He is the sovereign.
This is a common Ash'ari argument.

Third Objection: Commanding What is Not Willed is Foolishness
Al-Taftazani states and refutes the third objection with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection regarding commanding what is not willed and its refutation with counterexamples.

Core Idea
Commanding without willing is not always foolish; there can be wise purposes.

The third objection claims that if God commands something He does not will (or forbids what He wills), that would be foolish. Al-Taftazani refutes by providing real-life examples where such commands are meaningful, like a master testing his servant or an excuse for punishment.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and refutation
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the examples given.
2
Understand the objection
The objector thinks that commanding something you don't want done is senseless.
3
Analyze the counterexamples
Al-Taftazani gives cases: a master commanding to test obedience, or as an excuse to punish, or a person forced to command plunder of his own wealth.
These show that the command can have purposes other than getting the action done.
4
Address the sultan's subject objection
The objector says subjects obey because it is the sultan's will. Al-Taftazani responds: they obey due to command, not necessarily will, unless a sign of will appears.
Key distinction: command vs. will.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a divine command that feels overwhelming or against your desires, do you assume Allah wills your hardship? How can the truth that command and will can be separate free you to obey without resentment?
?
In your own role as a parent or leader, have you ever commanded something without truly willing it? How can you ensure your commands are motivated by wisdom, not just authority?
?
This lesson shows that even testing has purpose. What command of Allah are you avoiding because you doubt His will behind it? Will you act on the command regardless, trusting His wisdom?
