Fourth Objection: If Disbelief is Willed, It Would Be Obedience
Al-Taftazani states and refutes the fourth objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection that if disbelief were willed by God it would be obedience, and al-Taftazani's refutation distinguishing obedience from achieving will.

Core Idea
Obedience is compliance with command, not necessarily with will.

The fourth objection argues that if God wills disbelief, then disbelief would be obedience because obedience means fulfilling the will of the one obeyed. Al-Taftazani refutes: obedience means complying with command, not necessarily with will. One can obey without knowing God's inner will.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and refutation
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the distinction made.
2
Understand the objection's syllogism
Obedience = achieving the will of the obeyed. Disbelief, if willed by God, would achieve His will, hence be obedience.
3
Grasp the refutation's key distinction
Al-Taftazani says obedience is 'موافقة الأمر' (compliance with command), not achieving will. It goes with command whether will is known or not.
So even if disbelief achieves will, it is not obedience because it violates command.
4
Reflect on the implication
This preserves the idea that obedience is defined by following God's revealed law, not His hidden will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : أن إرادة القبيح قبيحة، والله منزه عن القبائح. ورد بأنه لا قبيح منه غاية الأمر أنه يخفي علينا وجه حسنه الثاني: أن العقاب على ما أراده ظلم ورد بالمنع فإنه تصرف في ملكه الثالث: أن الأمر بما لا يراد والنهي عما يراد سفه ورد بالمنع إذ ربما لا يكون غرض الآمر الإتيان بالمأمور .. كالسيد إذا أمر العبد امتحاناً له هل يطيعه أم لا؟ فإنه لا يريد شيئاً من الطاعة والعصيان، أو اعتذاراً عن ضربه بأنه لا يطيعه فإنه يريد منه العصيان، وكالمكره على الأمر بنهب أمواله، وكذا النهي. فإن قيل : مأمور السلطان يبادر إلى المأمور به معللاً بأنه مراد السلطان قلنا : لا مطلقاً. بل إذا ظهر أمارة الإرادة ، وإنما يعلل مطلقاً بالأمر والإشارة والحكم. الرابع : لو كان الكفر مراداً الله تعالى لكان طاعة لأن معناها تحصيل مراد المطاع لدورانه معه وجوداً وعدماً. ورد بالمنع بل هي موافقة الأمر وإنما تدور معه علمت الإرادة أو لم تعلم . الخامس : لو كان مراداً لكان قضاء فوجب الرضاء به والملازمة وبطلات اللازم ورد بأنه مقضى لاقضاء، ووجوب الرضا إنما هو بالقضاء دون المقضى ، ودعوى أن المراد بالقضاء الواجب الرضا به هو المقضى من المحن والبلايا والمصائب والرزايا لا الصفة الذاتية لله تعالى بهت بل هو الخلق والحكم والتقدير. وقد يجاب بأن الرضا بالكفر من حيث انه من قضاء الله تعالى طاعة ، ولا من هذه الحيثية كفر وفيه نظر .
First: that willing evil is evil, and God is transcendent above evils. It is answered that nothing from Him is evil; at most, its aspect of goodness is hidden from us. Second: that punishing for what He wills is injustice. It is answered with denial, for it is disposal in His dominion. Third: that commanding what is not willed and forbidding what is willed is foolishness. It is answered with denial, for sometimes the commander's purpose is not the performance of the commanded action — like the master who commands his servant to test whether he will obey or not, for he does not will anything of obedience or disobedience; or as an excuse for striking him because he does not obey, for he wills disobedience from him; and like one compelled to command the plundering of his wealth. The same applies to prohibition. If it is said: the subject of the sultan hastens to the commanded action, explaining it as being the sultan's will. We say: not absolutely, but only when an indication of will appears; and it is explained absolutely by command, indication, and judgment. Fourth: if disbelief were willed by God Almighty, it would be obedience, because the meaning of obedience is achieving the will of the one obeyed, as it goes with it in existence and non-existence. It is answered with denial; rather, it is compliance with command, and it goes with it whether will is known or not known. Fifth: if it were willed, it would be decree, so satisfaction with it would be obligatory — the implication and the consequence are false. It is answered that it is decreed, not decree; and the obligation of satisfaction is with the decree, not the decreed. The claim that what is meant by decree with which satisfaction is obligatory is the decreed of trials, calamities, afflictions, and misfortunes — not the essential attribute of God Almighty — is an absurdity; rather, it is creation, judgment, and predestination. And it may be answered that satisfaction with disbelief inasmuch as it is from God's decree is obedience, and not from this aspect it is disbelief. But there is consideration in this.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage enumerates five objections against the Ash'ari position that God wills all things, including evil, and provides refutations. The first objection states that willing evil is evil, and God is free from evils. The refutation explains that nothing from God is evil; the apparent evilness is due to our ignorance of its good aspect. The second objection claims that punishing for what God wills would be unjust. The refutation denies this, as God's actions are disposal of His own dominion. The third objection asserts that commanding what God does not will and forbidding what He does will is foolish. The refutation provides counterexamples: a master may command to test obedience without willing a specific outcome, or as an excuse for punishment. The objection that the sultan's subject hastens because it is the sultan's will is answered: he only does so when there is an indication of will, but the general inference is from command itself. The fourth objection argues that if disbelief were willed by God, it would be obedience, since obedience is achieving the will of the obeyed. The refutation distinguishes: obedience is compliance with command, not necessarily with will. The fifth objection claims that if disbelief were willed, it would be God's decree, so one must be satisfied with it. The refutation distinguishes between decree (qada') and the decreed (maqdi): the obligation of satisfaction is with the decree itself, not the specific decreed events like disbelief. A further response suggests that satisfaction with disbelief as from God's decree is obedience, but not from that aspect it remains disbelief, though this is considered problematic.

Key Terms
الطاعة
المطاع
الأمر
الإرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God wills something, doing it is automatically obedience.
Correction: Obedience is defined by command, not will. One can be doing God's will (predestination) while disobeying His command.

Checkpoint
According to al-Taftazani, what is the definition of obedience?
A) Achieving the will of the obeyed.
B) Compliance with command.
C) Doing what is good.
D) Following one's nature.

Key Takeaway
Obedience is following God's commands, not necessarily His will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about a sin you struggle with, do you ever find yourself thinking, 'If God didn't will it, it wouldn't happen'? How does this lesson challenge you to stop excusing your disobedience based on God's will?
?
In your daily life, are you more focused on fulfilling what you think God's will is (e.g., 'things happen for a reason') or on obeying His clear commands? What specific change can you make today to prioritize His command over your assumptions about His will?
?
This lesson teaches that obedience is about compliance with command, not with will. Consider one area of your life where you know God's command but ignore it because you feel it's 'not meant to be'. How can you take full responsibility and act against that feeling right now?

Fifth Objection: If Willed, It Would Be Decree, So Satisfaction Obligatory
Al-Taftazani states and refutes the fifth objection with a key distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth objection that if something is willed it is decreed, so we must be satisfied with it, and al-Taftazani's refutation distinguishing between decree (qada') and the decreed (maqdi).

Core Idea
We must be satisfied with the decree, not necessarily with the decreed evil.

The fifth objection claims that if disbelief is willed by God, it becomes His decree, and we must be satisfied with it, which contradicts the obligation to hate disbelief. Al-Taftazani refutes by distinguishing the divine attribute of decree from the decreed events. We are obliged to be satisfied with the decree itself, but not necessarily with the decreed content like disbelief. He also critiques an alternative answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and refutation
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the distinction between 'قضاء' and 'مقضى'.
2
Understand the objection's implication
If God wills something, it becomes His decree, so we must be pleased with it, which seems to require approving of disbelief.
3
Grasp the primary refutation
Al-Taftazani says: it is 'مقضى' (decreed), not 'قضاء' (decree). Obligation of satisfaction is with the decree itself, not the decreed. We are satisfied with God's decree as an attribute, not with the specific event of disbelief.
Key: satisfaction is with the divine attribute, not the content.
4
Analyze the alternative answer and critique
Some say satisfaction with disbelief from the perspective of decree is obedience, but from the perspective of disbelief it is not. Al-Taftazani finds this problematic.
The primary distinction is preferred.

Source Text

It is answered with denial; rather, it is compliance with command, and it goes with it whether will is known or not known. Reconciling Quranic Verses that Deny Divine Will for Evil
Al-Taftazani presents the sixth objection from Quranic verses and answers them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari tradition interprets Quranic verses that seem to negate God's will for evil, by distinguishing between divine will, command, approval, and love.

Core Idea
God wills all things, even evil, but He does not command, approve, or love evil.

The sixth objection to the Ash'ari doctrine that God wills all things comes from Quranic verses that appear to say the opposite. Verses like 'Allah does not intend injustice for His servants' and 'Allah does not love corruption' seem to deny that God wills evil. This lesson walks through al-Taftazani's careful response, showing how these verses actually affirm God's transcendence without removing His universal will.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the verses cited
Start by reading the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the list of Quranic verses the objector uses: they negate God's will for injustice, immorality, disbelief, and corruption.
The polytheists' statement 'If Allah had willed...' is also part of the objection.
2
Understand the first part of the answer: injustice
Al-Taftazani argues that injustice is inconceivable from God because His actions are His rightful disposal in His dominion. The negation of injustice in the verses is a negation of any injustice, which necessarily negates the will for it.
He clarifies the verses do not say 'He does not will injustice from Zayd to Amr' but rather 'He does not will injustice from Himself'.
3
Distinguish will from command, approval, and love
The author agrees that God does not command evil, approve of disbelief, or love corruption. But these actions do not negate His will; He wills them for a wise purpose while hating and punishing them.
This is the key distinction that protects God's transcendence without limiting His will.
4
Analyze the rebuttal of the polytheists' claim
The objector says God rebuked polytheists for claiming 'If Allah had willed...'. The answer: the polytheists said this mockingly to excuse themselves, so God punished them for their denial (takdhib), not for the factual truth of the statement.
The verse uses 'kadhdhaba' (deny) not 'kadhaba' (lie), showing they were exposed as insincere.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس : الآيات الشاهدة بنفي إرادته للقبائح وبالتوبيخ والرد على من يقول بذلك كقوله تعالى وما الله يريد ظلماً للعباد و وما الله يريد ظلماً للعالمين إن الله لا يأمر بالفحشاء ولا يرضى لعباده الكفر والله لا يحب الفساد وما خلقت الجن والإنس إلا ليعبدون سيقول الذين أشركوا لو شاء الله ما أشركنا ولا آباؤنا ولا حرمنا من شيء الآية وذلك لأن الله تعالى ذم المشركين ووبخهم على ادعائهم أن الكفر بمشيئة الله تعالى ، وكذبهم وآباءهم في ذلك وعاقبهم عليه ، وحكم بأنهم يتبعون فيه الظن دون العلم ، وأنه كذب صراح . والجواب : أنه لا يتصور منه الظلم لأن ما يفعله بالعباد تصرف منه في ملكه . فالإتيان نفي للظلم بنفي لازمه أعني الإرادة لأن ما يفعله القادر المختار لا يكون إلا مراداً، وليس فيهما أنه لا يريد ظلم زيد على عمر لظهور أن المعنى على أنه لا يريد ظلماً منه، وأما نفي الأمر والرضاء و المحبة فلا نزاع فيه لما في المحبة والرضا من الاستحسان وترك الاعتراض وإرادة الإنعام فهو يريد كفر الكافر ويخلقه، هذا يبغضه وينهاه عنه ويعاقبه عليه ولا يرضاه. وأما رد مقال المشركين فلقصدهم بذلك الهزء والسخرية، وتمهيداً لعذر في الإشراك كما إذا قال القدري استهزاء بالسني، وقصد إلى إلزامه لوشاء الله رجوعي إلى مذهبكم وخلق في عقائدكم لرجعت والدليل عليه أنه قال تعالى وكذلك كذب الذين من قبلهم فجعل مقالهم تكذيباً لا كذباً، ورتب عذاب الآباء على تكذيبهم
The sixth: the verses that witness to the negation of His will for evils, and to rebuke and refutation of those who say such. Like His saying: 'And Allah does not intend injustice for His servants' (40:31), 'And Allah does not intend injustice for the worlds' (3:108), 'Indeed, Allah does not order immorality' (7:28), 'And He does not approve for His servants disbelief' (39:7), 'And Allah does not like corruption' (2:205), 'And I did not create the jinn and mankind except to worship Me' (51:56). 'Those who associate partners will say, "If Allah had willed, we would not have associated, nor our fathers, nor would we have forbidden anything."' (6:148) And that is because God Almighty blamed the polytheists and rebuked them for their claim that disbelief is by God's will, and declared them and their fathers liars in that, and punished them for it, and judged that they follow therein assumption rather than knowledge, and that it is plain falsehood. The answer: that injustice is not conceivable from Him, because what He does with His servants is His disposal in His dominion. So the bringing of the verses is a negation of injustice by negating its necessary condition, namely will — because whatever the free agent does cannot be except willed. And in these two verses it is not that He does not will injustice of Zayd against Amr, because it is apparent that the meaning is that He does not will injustice from Him. As for the negation of command, approval, and love, there is no dispute about it, because approval and love imply approval, lack of objection, and desire for beneficence. So He wills the disbelief of the disbeliever and creates it, yet He hates it, forbids it, punishes for it, and does not approve of it. As for the rebuttal of the polytheists' statement, it is because they intended by that mockery and derision, and to prepare an excuse for polytheism, as when the Qadari says derisively to the Sunni, intending to compel him: 'If Allah had willed, I would have returned to your doctrine and created in your beliefs, then I would have returned.' The evidence for that is that He said: 'Thus did those before them deny' (6:148), so He made their statement a denial (takdhib) not a lie (kadhib), and He attached the punishment of the fathers to their denial.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth objection to the Ash'ari doctrine that God wills all things, including evil. The objector cites several Quranic verses that appear to negate divine will for injustice, immorality, disbelief, and corruption, and also notes that God rebukes the polytheists who claimed that if God willed, they would not have associated partners. The answer begins by asserting that injustice is not conceivable from God because all His actions are His disposal in His dominion. The verses that negate 'will for injustice' are interpreted as negating the very occurrence of injustice from God, not negating that He could will it; rather, the negation is of the action itself, which necessarily implies negation of will since a free agent does nothing without will. However, the verses do not say that God does not will Zayd's injustice to Amr; the obvious meaning is that God does not will injustice from Himself. As for verses negating command, approval, and love, the Ash'ari agrees: God does not command evil, does not approve of disbelief, and does not love corruption. But that does not contradict His willing and creating disbelief; He wills it for a wise purpose, yet hates it and punishes it. Finally, the polytheists' statement 'if Allah had willed, we would not have associated' is refuted as mockery and an excuse. God called it 'denial' (takdhib) rather than 'lie' (kadhib), and punished them for denial, not for the factual truth of the statement. This indicates their intention was not to assert God's will seriously but to deride.

Key Terms
الآيات
نفي الإرادة
الظلم
الملك
الأمر
الرضا
المحبة
المشركون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that verses negating God's command, approval, or love for evil also negate His will for it.
Correction: The passage clarifies that will is distinct from command, approval, and love. God can will and create disbelief while forbidding, hating, and punishing it.

Checkpoint
How does the author explain the polytheists' statement 'If Allah had willed, we would not have associated partners'?
A) He says it is a true statement, but they were punished for other reasons.
B) He says they meant it as mockery and an excuse, so God called it 'denial' and punished them for that.
C) He says they were mistaken because God does will polytheism.
D) He says the verse abrogates the earlier verses.

Key Takeaway
Quranic verses that appear to deny God's will for evil actually negate different attributes (injustice, command, approval, love) while preserving His universal will over all things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness evil or experience injustice, do you ever secretly question God's goodness or power? How does this lesson's distinction between what God wills and what He approves challenge you to trust His wisdom? What specific thought can you hold onto when you face such trials?
?
The polytheists used God's decree as an excuse to avoid responsibility. Where in your life do you find yourself hiding behind 'it was my qadar' rather than repenting or striving? What would it cost you to stop using that excuse and instead take ownership before Allah today?
?
If you truly believe that God hates disbelief but still created disbelievers, does that convict you to see them with mercy rather than contempt? How will you treat someone who disbelieves tomorrow, remembering that your will to guide them is distinct from God's sovereign will?

Eighth Objection: Evil Is Disliked – Does That Deny Divine Will?
Sanusi presents and refutes an objection based on a Quranic verse about evil being disliked.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the objection that the Quranic verse calling evil 'disliked' implies it cannot be willed by Allah.

Core Idea
God's dislike differs from human dislike; the verse refers to human perception.

This passage addresses a key objection from the Quran: if Allah calls evil deeds 'disliked,' then He cannot will them, since will and dislike are opposites. Sanusi responds by clarifying the perspective of the verse and dismissing a weak metaphorical interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its basis
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the objection is based on the Quranic verse (17:38) where evil deeds are described as 'disliked' (makruh) at God.
The objector uses the verse to argue that what is disliked cannot be willed, because will and dislike are opposites.
2
Identify the logical problem
Understand the core dilemma: if Allah dislikes evil, then He cannot will it. But if He does not will it, how can it exist? The objector claims this contradicts divine omnipotence.
3
Understand Sanusi's response
Sanusi concedes that the verse refers to actual prohibitions, but reinterprets the 'dislike' as human perception, not divine. If the dislike were attributed to Allah, it would lead to impossibility (muhal).
The key move is the distinction between human and divine perspectives.
4
Examine the dismissal of the metaphor interpretation
Sanusi rejects the suggestion that 'disliked' is merely a metaphor for 'prohibited,' calling it meaningless because the verse clearly refers to the prohibited acts themselves.
This shows that the literal meaning of the verse must be addressed, not evaded.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثامن: قوله تعالى: كل ذلك كان سيئه عند ربك مكروهاً (۱) جعل المنهيات مكروهة فلا تكون مرادة، لأن الإرادة والكراهة ضدان، ورد بعد تسليم كونه إشارة إلى المنهيات الواقعة ليلزم كونها مرادة بأن المعنى أنها مكروهة عند الناس وفي مجال العادات لا عند الله فيلزم المحال . وأما جعل المكروه مجازاً عن المنهي فلغو من الكلام لكون ذلك إشارة إلى المنهي .
The eighth: His statement, exalted is He: 'All that, its evil is at your Lord detested (makruhan)' (1) He made the prohibitions disliked, so they are not willed, because will and dislike are opposites. And it was responded, after conceding that it is an indication to the actual prohibitions, that it necessitates them being willed, by [saying] that the meaning is that they are disliked among people and in the realm of customs, not with God, so the impossible would follow. And as for making 'disliked' (al-makruh) a metaphor for 'prohibited' (al-manhi), it is meaningless speech because that is an indication to the prohibited.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The author presents the eighth objection based on the Quranic verse from Surah al-Isra (17:38) that calls evil deeds 'disliked' (makruh) at God. The objector argues: if prohibitions are disliked, then they cannot be willed (murada) by God, since will and dislike are opposites. The author responds by conceding that the verse refers to actual prohibitions but argues that the dislike is from a human perspective (among people and customs), not from God. If the dislike were from God, that would entail impossibility (muhal). Then the author dismisses the suggestion that 'disliked' is simply a metaphor for 'prohibited' as meaningless (laghw), because the verse is clearly referring to the prohibited acts themselves, not a mere figure of speech.

Key Terms
إرادة
كراهة
مكروه
منهي
مراد
محال
مجاز
لغو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse shows that God dislikes some actions in the same way humans do, thus they cannot be willed by Him.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the 'dislike' in the verse refers to human perspective (among people and customs), not to God. Attributing literal dislike to God would lead to impossibility.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the objection that prohibitions cannot be willed because they are disliked?
A) By denying that the verse refers to prohibitions.
B) By distinguishing between human and divine perspectives of dislike.
C) By claiming the verse is metaphorical.
D) By agreeing that prohibitions are not willed.

Key Takeaway
The objection fails because the 'dislike' in the verse refers to human judgment, not divine will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever rationalized a sin by telling yourself that it must be part of God's plan, and therefore He doesn't truly dislike it? How does this lesson challenge that assumption?
?
When you read Quran 17:38, do you instinctively project your own feeling of dislike onto God? What would it mean for you to accept that God's will is not limited by human categories of like and dislike?
?
If the 'dislike' in the verse is from human perspective, does that change how you view your own moral judgments? Are you willing to submit your dislikes to what God has actually prohibited, even when your custom says otherwise?

Sixth Argument: Intrinsic Good/Evil Negates Divine Free Choice
Al-Taftazani presents the sixth argument against intrinsic good/evil: it would negate divine free choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth argument against intrinsic moral properties: that it would negate divine free choice, and the objection that God's non-performance of evil is due to wisdom, not inability.

Core Idea
If actions are intrinsically good or evil, God would be compelled to judge accordingly, negating His free choice.

Al-Taftazani now presents a powerful objection to the thesis that actions have intrinsic moral value. He argues that such a view would strip God of free choice, compelling Him to judge according to the rationally preponderant. Yet the opponent counters that God's avoidance of evil is due to His perfect wisdom, not any lack of power. This lesson unpacks the argument and the initial objection, though the full response continues beyond this page.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the flow from the claim to the entailment and the objection.
The passage includes footnote markers (3) and (4) which refer to commentary not included here.
2
Identify the entailment
Understand why intrinsic good/evil leads to compulsion in divine judgment. The key phrase: 'لم يكن الباري مختاراً في الحكم' (God would not be free in judgment).
The argument assumes that a judgment contrary to the rationally known would be evil for God.
3
Reflect on the objection
Consider the opponent's objection: God's non-performance of evil is due to the 'deterrent of wisdom' (ṣārif al-ḥikma), not inability. Does this preserve free choice?
The objection also questions how judgment can be free if it is determined by the action's nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس : لو حسن الفعل أو قبح لذاته أو لصفاته وجهاته (٣) لم يكن الباري مختاراً في الحكم واللازم باطل بالإجماع. وجه اللزوم أنه لا بد في الفعل من حكم ، والحكم على خلاف ما هو المعقول قبيح لا يصح عن الباري. بل يتعين عليه الحكم بالمعقول الراجح بحيث لا يصح تركه، وفيه نفي للاختيار. واعترض بأنه وإن لم يفعل القبيح لصارف الحكمة (٤)، لكنه قادر عليه، بتمكن منه، ولو سلم فالامتناع لصارف الحكمة لا ينفي الاختيار على أن الحكم عندكم ، فكيف يكون بالاختيار ، اللهم إلا أن يقصد الإلزام أو يراد جعلـه متعلقـاً قديم ، بالأفعال .
The sixth: If an action were good or evil by itself or by its attributes and respects, then the Creator would not be free (mukhtār) in judgment, and the consequent is false by consensus. The aspect of the entailment is that there must be a judgment concerning the action, and a judgment contrary to what is rationally known is evil and not proper to the Creator. Rather He would necessarily judge according to the rationally preponderant such that it is not proper to leave it, and this negates free choice (ikhtiyār). And it is objected that even if He does not do evil due to the deterrent of wisdom (ḥikma), He is nonetheless capable of it, having power over it; and if granted, then the impossibility due to the deterrent of wisdom does not negate free choice, especially since according to you the judgment [is determined], so how can it be by free choice? Unless it is meant to compel (ilzām) or to make it related as eternal (qadīm) to actions.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth argument against intrinsic moral properties, followed by an objection whose answer is incomplete in this window. The argument: if actions are good or evil by themselves or by their attributes, then God would not be free in His judgment—He would be compelled to judge actions according to their intrinsic moral status, and a judgment contrary to the rationally preponderant would be evil, which is impossible for God. Thus, He would be necessitated to judge accordingly, negating His free choice (ikhtiyār). The objection raises that God's non-performance of evil is due to the 'deterrent of wisdom' (ṣārif al-ḥikma), not inability; He remains capable, so free choice is preserved. The objection also questions how judgment can be free if it is determined by the action's nature. The author seems to imply that the objector's own position (that judgment is determined) undermines their argument, and that perhaps the aim is to force a concession or to relate judgment as eternal (qadīm) to actions. However, the full response is not present in this page window.

Key Terms
حسن
قبح
باري
مختار
حكم
اختيار
صارف الحكمة
إلزام
قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God does not do evil because He is unable to do evil.
Correction: God does not do evil due to His perfect wisdom (ṣārif al-ḥikma); He is fully capable of it, but His wisdom prevents it, preserving free choice.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence for God if actions are intrinsically good/evil, according to the sixth argument?
A) He would become unjust
B) He would not be free in judgment
C) He would be ignorant
D) He would be imperfect

Key Takeaway
The sixth argument shows that intrinsic moral properties would compel God's judgment, thereby negating His free choice—a conclusion rejected by the Ahl al-Sunna.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God is free to judge as He wills, or do you secretly believe that certain actions are so evil that God must punish them? How does that affect your view of His mercy and forgiveness?
?
The objection says God is capable of evil but does not do it due to wisdom. Does this make you uncomfortable? Will you trust God's wisdom even when you cannot understand why He allows certain evils?
?
If God's judgment were compelled by the nature of actions, then your own salvation would depend on the intrinsic value of your deeds. How does the Ash'ari view of God's free judgment give you hope? Are you willing to surrender your own moral calculus to His decree?

Why God Does No Ugly: Ash'ari vs Mu'tazila
Opens fourth investigation, contrasts Ash'ari and Mu'tazila on God not doing ugly.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fundamental difference between Ash'ari and Mu'tazila justifications for why God does not commit ugly acts.

Core Idea
Ugliness is by Sharia for Ash'ari, but by reason for Mu'tazila.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid opens the fourth investigation into the divine attribute of not doing ugly. The author first states the Ash'ari position that ugliness is determined by Sharia and thus inconceivable from God. Then he describes the Mu'tazila position which holds that ugliness is known by reason and God must avoid it. He notes a superficial agreement but deep disagreement on the basis. Let's read the text carefully to grasp this crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage once
Read the Arabic and translation to get the flow. Note the key terms: 'qabih' (ugly), 'wajib' (obligatory), 'shar' (Sharia).
2
Identify the two positions
Look for 'as for us' and 'as for the Mu'tazila' to see the contrast. Notice the author criticizes the Mu'tazila position as arbitrary.
3
Analyze the agreement
The passage says all agree God does no ugly, but reasons differ. Understand why Ash'ari say it's by Sharia and Mu'tazila by rational obligation.
4
Consider the implication
Ask yourself: if ugliness is by Sharia, then God's actions are beyond moral categories. If by reason, then God is bound by rational norms. Which view preserves divine sovereignty?
This connects to the broader debate on divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (المبحث الرابع) لا قبيح من الله تعالى لا قبيح من أفعاله بحكم الشرع . والمعتزلة لما قالوا بوجوب أشياء عليه، وثبوت قبائح بالعقل ذهبوا إلى أن يفعل البتة ما وجب ويترك ما قبح فوقع الايقان على أنه لا يفعل قبيحاً، ولا يترك واجباً، واضطروا في تفسير الواجب عليه تعالى ثم اضطروا إلى أن معناه أنه يفعله البتة، وإن جاز تركه وهو مع كونه رجماً بالغيب مجرد تسمية) . الله تعالى وإن كان هو الخالق للكل، ولا واجب عليه وإن حسن لا خلاف في أن الباري لا يفعل قبيحاً، ولا يترك واجباً. أما عندنا فلأنه لا قبح منه ولا واجب عليه لكون ذلك بالشرع ، ولا يتصور في فعله . وأما عند المعتزلة فلأن كل ما هو قبيح منه فهو يتركه البتة، وما هو واجب عليه فهو يفعله البتة. وسيجيء ذكر ما أوجبوا عليه
He said: (The Fourth Investigation) There is nothing ugly from God, nothing ugly from His acts by the ruling of the Sharia. The Mu'tazila, when they said that things are obligatory upon Him and that ugliness is established by reason, went to the position that He definitely does what is obligatory and leaves what is ugly, so it became established that He does not do ugly and does not leave obligatory. They were compelled in interpreting the obligation upon Him, then compelled to mean that He definitely does it, even if leaving it is possible, and it is, despite being a shot in the dark, mere naming. God, even though He is the Creator of all, and nothing is obligatory upon Him even if good, there is no dispute that the Creator does not do ugly and does not leave obligatory. As for us, it is because there is no ugliness from Him and no obligation upon Him, since that is by the Sharia, and it is not conceivable in His act. As for the Mu'tazila, it is because everything that is ugly from Him He definitely leaves, and everything obligatory upon Him He definitely does. And there will come mention of what they made obligatory upon Him.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage opens the fourth investigation (al-mabhash al-rabi') on the topic that nothing ugly comes from God. The author first states the Ash'ari position: by Sharia, nothing ugly proceeds from God. Then he describes the Mu'tazila position: they affirm obligations and ugliness by reason, so God must obey these obligations. The Ash'ari criticizes this as arbitrary naming. Then the author clarifies that there is agreement that God does not do ugly or leave obligatory, but for different reasons. Ash'ari: because ugliness and obligation are defined by Sharia, and God's actions are not subject to it. Mu'tazila: because God avoids ugliness and does what is obligatory. The passage ends with a promise to mention the specific obligations the Mu'tazila assert.

Key Terms
القبيح
الواجب
الشرع
المعتزلة
الإيجاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Muslims agree on the same reason why God does not do ugly.
Correction: The passage highlights different justifications: Ash'ari by Sharia, Mu'tazila by rational obligation.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari view in this passage, why does God not do ugly things?
A) Because He is obligated to avoid them.
B) Because ugliness is defined by Sharia and God's acts are not subject to Sharia.
C) Because He knows they are bad.
D) Because He has no choice.

Key Takeaway
Ash'ari holds God's actions are beyond Sharia-based ugliness; Mu'tazila holds God is rationally obligated to avoid ugliness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever doubted God's goodness because something in your life seemed unjust? Does this lesson's explanation that God's actions are beyond our moral categories challenge you to trust Him even when you don't understand? What would it cost you to truly believe that nothing ugly comes from Him?
?
When you face a situation that seems 'wrong' from your perspective, do you tend to judge God by your own standards of justice, or do you submit to the Sharia-based understanding that ugliness is defined by revelation? Which position—Ash'ari or Mu'tazila—does your heart naturally lean toward, and why?
?
The Mu'tazila felt compelled to defend God's justice by imposing rational obligations on Him. Do you sometimes try to 'defend' God by explaining His actions in human terms? Could this lesson's critique of 'shot in the dark' naming be a warning against over-defining God's nature? What can you do today to increase your awe and acceptance of God's absolute sovereignty?

Creating Ugly Things Is Not Ugly
First objection and answer on God creating ugly things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari distinction between creating evil and being the doer of evil, and why creating the ugly is not itself ugly.

Core Idea
God creates ugly things but is not the doer of them.

One of the strongest objections against divine creation of all things is that God would then be responsible for evil. The Ash'ari response draws a crucial distinction between causing existence and moral agency. This lesson unpacks that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the objection lists three ugly things, and the answer distinguishes between creating and doing.
The answer uses the terms 'mujid' (originator) and 'fa'il' (doer).
2
Understand key terms
Focus on 'mujid' vs 'fa'il'. In Ash'ari theology, God is the originator of all actions, but moral qualities apply only to the doer in a specific sense. Why is creating the ugly not ugly?
Ugliness is a moral judgment tied to the agent's capacity and command. God is beyond such judgments.
3
Reflect on divine transcendence
Consider how this answer protects God from being associated with evil. How does it align with divine transcendence (tanzih)?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : الكفر والظلم والمعاصي كلها قبائح، وقد خلقها الله تعالى . قلنا : نعم إلا أن خلق القبيح ليس بقبيح، فهو موجد القبائح لا فاعل لها . فإن قيل : فلا يفعل الحسن أيضاً لأنه لا حكم عليه أمراً كما لا حكم عليه نهياً والإجماع على خلافه . قلنا : قد ورد الشرع بالثناء عليه في أفعاله فكانت حسنة لكونها متعلق المدح، والثناء عند الله تعالى، وأما إذا اكتفى في الحسن بعدم استحقاق الذم في حكم الله تعالى فالأمر أظهر. فإن قيل : الذي ثبت من مذهبنا هو أنه لا واجب عليه بمعنى أن شيئاً من أفعاله
If it is said: Disbelief (kufr), injustice (zulm), and sins (ma'asi) are all ugly (qaba'ih), and God created them. We say: Yes, except that creating the ugly is not ugly; He is the originator (mujid) of ugly things, not the doer (fa'il) of them. If it is said: Then He does not do good either, because there is no command upon Him just as there is no prohibition upon Him, and the consensus is contrary to that. We say: The Sharia has come with praise upon Him in His acts, so they are good because they are objects of praise and commendation in the sight of God. But if one suffices in goodness with the absence of deserving blame in God's judgment, then the matter is clearer. If it is said: What has been established from our madhhab is that nothing is obligatory upon Him, meaning that none of His acts...
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage contains three objections and answers. The first objection points out that God creates kufr and injustice, which are ugly. The answer distinguishes between creating the ugly (which is not ugly) and being the doer of ugly. God is the originator (mujid) but not the doer (fa'il) in the sense of being subject to moral judgment. The second objection argues that if God is not commanded or prohibited, then His acts are not good either. The answer states that Sharia praises God's acts, making them good. Alternatively, if goodness is defined as not deserving blame, then God's acts are good because He does not deserve blame. The third objection is incomplete; it begins to reiterate that nothing is obligatory upon God.

Key Terms
الكفر
الظلم
المعاصي
خلق القبيح
موجد
فاعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God creates evil, He is evil or does evil.
Correction: God creates ugly things but is not the doer of them. Creating the ugly is not ugly because ugliness is a moral judgment that does not apply to the Creator.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari response, is creating the ugly (khalq al-qabih) itself ugly?
A) Yes, because the act is ugly.
B) No, because creating the ugly is not the same as doing the ugly.
C) It depends on the context.
D) The passage does not say.

Key Takeaway
God creates ugly things but is not the doer of them; creating the ugly is not ugly because moral judgments apply only to agents under command.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you inwardly blame God for creating the act or your inclination? How does today’s lesson challenge you to take full ownership of your choices as the actual ‘doer’?
?
Consider a time you questioned God’s goodness because of tragedy or evil. Does the distinction between creating and doing make any difference to your heart? Why or why not?
?
If creating ugly things is not ugly, then what does that mean for how you defend God’s justice when others accuse Him of wrong? Are you ready to give this answer with conviction, or do you still feel a tension in your own soul?

God Does Good Even Without Command
Second objection and answer on God not doing good.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari respond to the objection that if God is not commanded, His acts are not good, by appealing to divine praise and the absence of blame.

Core Idea
God's acts are good because He is praised for them in Sharia.

A second objection follows logically: if God is not subject to commands, then His acts cannot be called 'good' either. The Ash'ari answer draws on revelation and a definition of goodness as absence of blame. This lesson explains that response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the objection uses analogical reasoning from God not being commanded to God not doing good. The answer gives two arguments.
The first argument is from Sharia praise; the second is from absence of blame.
2
Understand the two arguments
Identify the two ways the Ash'ari establish that God's acts are good. First, Sharia praises them. Second, if goodness is defined as not deserving blame, God's acts qualify.
The second argument is conditional ('if one suffices...').
3
Connect to divine transcendence
See how both arguments preserve God's transcendence: His goodness is not dependent on human moral standards but on divine revelation and His own judgment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

If it is said: Disbelief (kufr), injustice (zulm), and sins (ma'asi) are all ugly (qaba'ih), and God created them. We say: Yes, except that creating the ugly is not ugly; He is the originator (mujid) of ugly things, not the doer (fa'il) of them. If it is said: Then He does not do good either, because there is no command upon Him just as there is no prohibition upon Him, and the consensus is contrary to that. We say: The Sharia has come with praise upon Him in His acts, so they are good because they are objects of praise and commendation in the sight of God. But if one suffices in goodness with the absence of deserving blame in God's judgment, then the matter is clearer. If it is said: What has been established from our madhhab is that nothing is obligatory upon Him, meaning that none of His acts...
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage contains three objections and answers. The first objection points out that God creates kufr and injustice, which are ugly. The answer distinguishes between creating the ugly (which is not ugly) and being the doer of ugly. God is the originator (mujid) but not the doer (fa'il) in the sense of being subject to moral judgment. The second objection argues that if God is not commanded or prohibited, then His acts are not good either. The answer states that Sharia praises God's acts, making them good. Alternatively, if goodness is defined as not deserving blame, then God's acts are good because He does not deserve blame. The third objection is incomplete; it begins to reiterate that nothing is obligatory upon God.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God is not commanded, then He does not do good.
Correction: God's acts are good because Sharia praises them, and because He does not deserve blame in His own judgment.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari answer, why are God's acts good?
A) Because God commands them.
B) Because they are praised in Sharia.
C) Because humans judge them good.
D) Because they are not prohibited.

Key Takeaway
God's acts are good because Sharia praises them and because He does not deserve blame in His own judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face hardship, do you secretly suspect that God's action toward you is not good? How does this lesson's teaching that God's acts are good because He is praised in Sharia and free from blame challenge that suspicion? Will you now consciously affirm God's goodness even when you don't understand His act?
?
This lesson says God's goodness isn't based on a command upon Him. Do you ever act as if God owes you something—as if He is commanded to be good to you? How can you release that entitlement and instead trust that whatever He does is good simply because He does it?
?
If God's acts are good because Sharia praises them, are you truly praising God for His acts in your life, even the painful ones? Or do you withhold praise when things don't go your way? Make a specific commitment today to praise God for one difficult situation you are currently facing.

Objection: Allah Creates Kufr and Sins – Response: Creating Ugly Is Not Ugly
Taftazani answers the objection that Allah creates ugly acts like kufr and injustice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari distinction between creating ugly acts and performing them, and how this answers the objection that Allah creates evil.

Core Idea
Creating ugly is not ugly; Allah originates, He does not commit ugliness.

One of the most challenging objections against divine justice is that Allah creates disbelief and sin. How can He be good if He creates evil? Taftazani meets this objection head-on with a precise conceptual distinction: creating an ugly act is not the same as performing it. This lesson unpacks that distinction and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the objection-response structure repeated twice.
2
Identify the first objection and response
The opponent says: disbelief, injustice, sins are ugly, and Allah created them. Find how Taftazani responds and the key distinction he makes.
He says: 'creating ugly is not ugly' and distinguishes between being the originator (mujid) and the doer (fa'il).
3
Identify the second objection and response
The opponent then says: if Allah does not do ugly, He also does not do good because no command applies to Him. Find Taftazani's answer involving Shariah praise.
He argues that Shariah praises Allah's acts, making them good; alternatively, goodness can be defined by absence of blame.
4
Understand the key distinction: mujid vs fa'il
Reflect on why this distinction is crucial. Creating an act is not the same as performing it; the moral quality of an act depends on the agent's action, not the act of bringing it into existence.
This distinction allows Allah to be the source of all existence while remaining free from evil.
5
Reflect on the role of Shariah praise
Consider how Taftazani uses Shariah praise to define goodness for Allah. This shows that divine acts are not subject to human moral categories but are defined by divine revelation.
The alternative definition (absence of blame) is even simpler and avoids reliance on external criteria.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : الكفر والظلم والمعاصي كلها قبائح، وقد خلقها الله تعالى .
قلنا : نعم إلا أن خلق القبيح ليس بقبيح، فهو موجد القبائح لا فاعل لها .
فإن قيل : فلا يفعل الحسن أيضاً لأنه لا حكم عليه أمراً كما لا حكم عليه نهياً والإجماع على خلافه .
قلنا : قد ورد الشرع بالثناء عليه في أفعاله فكانت حسنة لكونها متعلق المدح، والثناء عند الله تعالى، وأما إذا اكتفى في الحسن بعدم استحقاق الذم في حكم الله تعالى فالأمر أظهر.
If it is said: Disbelief, injustice, and sins are all ugly acts, and Allah has created them. We reply: Yes, except that creating ugly is not ugly; so He is the originator of ugly acts, not the doer of them. If it is said: Then He does not do good either, because no command applies to Him just as no prohibition applies, and the consensus is contrary to that. We reply: The Shariah has come with praise upon Him for His acts, so they are good because they are objects of praise and commendation from Allah Almighty. But if one is satisfied in goodness with lack of deserving blame in the judgment of Allah, the matter is even clearer.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage presents two objections to the Ash'ari position and their answers. The first objection points out that Allah created kufr, injustice, and sins, which are ugly acts. The answer makes a crucial distinction: creating an ugly act is not itself ugly. Allah is the originator (mujid) of ugly acts, not the doer (fa'il) of them. The second objection argues that if Allah does not do ugly acts, then by the same logic He does not do good acts either, since no command or prohibition applies to Him. The answer explains that the Shariah praises Allah for His acts, which makes them good because they are objects of praise from Allah. Alternatively, if goodness is defined simply as the absence of blame in Allah's judgment, then the point is even stronger.

خلق القبيح
موجد
فاعل
حسن
مدح
ذم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Creating ugly acts makes Allah ugly.
Correction: Creating an ugly act is not itself ugly; Allah is the originator, not the doer, and Shariah praise makes His acts good.

Checkpoint

B) He says that those acts are not actually ugly.
C) He says that creating ugly is not itself ugly, and Allah is the originator, not the doer.
D) He says that Allah only creates good acts.

Key Takeaway
Allah creates all acts, but creating ugly is not ugly; His acts are good because they are praised by Shariah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The Shariah praises Allah for all His acts. Is there any area of your life where you are not praising Allah—where you harbor resentment or complaint? What belief about Allah's goodness must change for you to surrender that complaint to Him?

Divine Knowledge and Impossibility: Essential vs. Accidental Entailment
Taftazani presents and refutes an objection about divine knowledge and impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an act that would entail an impossibility due to divine knowledge or news is not itself impossible, through the distinction between essential and accidental entailment.

Core Idea
What entails impossibility by accident is not itself impossible.

This passage addresses a crucial nuance in rational theology: when a hypothetical scenario leads to an impossibility, the cause of that impossibility matters. The objection claims that if Allah knows something won't happen, then supposing it happens leads to absurdity, so the event is impossible. Taftazani refutes this by distinguishing between essential and accidental entailment.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the objector's claim: divine knowledge or news makes the act impossible because supposing it occurs leads to Allah being ignorant or lying.
Notice the structure: 'If it is said...' then the objection.
2
Identify the refutation
Find where the author says 'The answer is: the denial of the major premise.' Understand that he denies the general rule that anything entailing impossibility is itself impossible.
The key phrase is 'منع الكبرى' (denial of the major premise).
3
Grasp the essential vs. accidental distinction
The author says the rule only holds if the entailment is by essence (لذاته), not by accident (لعارض) as in the case of divine knowledge or news.
Accidental attributes like knowledge do not change the intrinsic possibility of the act.
4
Apply the distinction
See that the act remains possible in itself (ممكناً في نفسه). The impossibility arises only from the external accident, not from the act's own nature.
This preserves divine foreknowledge without making the act impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : ما علم الله أو أخبر بعدم وقوعه يلزم من فرض وقوعه محال، هو جهله أو كذبه تعالى عن ذلك وكل مايلزم من فرض وقوعه محال، فهو محال ضرورة امتناع وجود الملزوم بدون اللازم فجوابه منع الكبرى، وإنما يصدق لو كان لزوم المحال لذاته . أما لو كان لعارض كالعلم أو الخبر فيما نحن فيه فلا ، الجواز أن يكون هو ممكناً في نفسه، ومنشأ لزوم المحال هو ذلك العارض، ولعل لهذه النكتة في بعض كتبنا تقريراً آخر
If it is said: That which Allah knows or informed would not occur, supposing it occurs necessarily entails something impossible (muhal), namely His ignorance or lying – exalted is He above that – and everything that is entailed by supposing its occurrence is impossible, then it is impossible by necessity of the impossibility of the existence of the logical cause (malzum) without the necessary consequent (lazim). The answer is: the denial of the major premise. It would be true only if the entailment of the impossible were by essence. But if it is by an accident, such as knowledge or news in our case, then it is not. The act itself is possible in itself, and the source of the necessary impossibility is that accident. Perhaps this subtlety has another formulation in some of our books.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its refutation. The objection: if Allah knows or informs that something will not happen, then assuming it happens leads to Allah being ignorant or lying, which is impossible. Therefore the act itself is impossible. The refutation: The major premise 'everything that entails an impossibility is impossible' is only true when the entailment is essential. If the impossibility arises from an accidental attribute (like Allah's knowledge or news), then the act can be possible in itself, and the impossibility is due to the external accident. So the act is not inherently impossible.

Key Terms
علم
خبر
محال
لازم
ملزوم
عارض
جواز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that any impossibility entailed by a supposition makes the act itself impossible, without distinguishing essential from accidental entailment.
Correction: Only essential entailment makes the act impossible. Accidental entailment, as with divine knowledge, does not render the act intrinsically impossible.

Checkpoint
The author refutes the objection by denying which premise?
A) That Allah's knowledge is infallible
B) The major premise that everything entailing an impossibility is itself impossible
C) That the act is possible in itself
D) That the objection is valid

Key Takeaway
The distinction between essential and accidental entailment preserves the possibility of an act in itself despite divine foreknowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that Allah knows every sin you will ever commit, does that knowledge make you feel trapped or doomed? How does Taftazani's distinction between essential and accidental impossibility free you from that trap? Will you truly embrace your accountability today, knowing your actions are possible in themselves?
?
You often blame your failures on Allah's decree. But this lesson says the impossibility of an act is only accidental, not essential. Where in your life have you used predestination as an excuse to avoid responsibility? Are you ready to take ownership of your choices from this moment onward?
?
If the act itself remains possible despite divine knowledge, then nothing in Allah's foreknowledge forces your hand. What one specific decision have you been postponing, thinking it was 'written' not to happen? Will you act on it now, with the conviction that it is truly possible in itself?

The Foreknowledge Argument and Its Refutation
Taftazani presents the foreknowledge argument and its rebuttal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the argument from divine foreknowledge is used to question obligation for the unbeliever, and how Taftazani refutes the counter-objection by showing it does not address the actual case.

Core Idea
Divine foreknowledge does not change, but the known object determines what is possible.

This passage presents a third argument from those who permit impossible obligations: if Allah knows someone will die an unbeliever, how can they be obligated to believe? The counter-objection claims that if they did believe, Allah's knowledge would have been different from eternity. Taftazani shows that this counter only works in a hypothetical scenario, not in the actual case of certain foreknowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal translation. Notice the dialectical structure: argument, counter, response, conclusion.
The passage uses technical terms like 'انقلاب العلم' (change of knowledge) and 'العلم تابع للمعلوم' (knowledge follows the known).
2
Identify the main parts
Locate the initial argument, the counter-objection, Taftazani's refutation, and his final comment that this example is not the disputed point.
3
Understand the counter-objection's logic
The counter says that if the person believed, then from eternity Allah's knowledge would have been that he dies a believer, so it's a substitution of knowledge, not a change. Focus on the principle 'knowledge follows the known'.
This is the core of the counter: the object of knowledge determines the knowledge itself.
4
See why Taftazani rejects it
Taftazani points out that the counter only holds if the object of knowledge were different from the start, but we are discussing the actual case where Allah's knowledge is certain that the person dies an unbeliever. Hence, assuming belief would necessitate a change.
He also notes that this example is not the real point of dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها من علم الله تعالى منه أنه لا يؤمن ، بل يموت على الكفر مكلف بالإيمان وفاقاً، مع استحالته منه لأنه لو آمن لزم انقلاب علم الله تعالى جهلاً. صح أن يقال لا نسلّم أنه لو آمن لزم انقلاب العلم جهلاً ، بل لزم أن يكون العلم المتعلق به من الأزل، أنه يموت مؤمناً ، فإن العلم تابع للمعلوم، فيكون هذا تقدير علم مكان علم لا تغيير علم إلى جهل كما إذا قدرت الآتي بالقبيح آتياً بالحسن، فإنه يكون من أول الأمر مستحقاً للمدح لا منقلباً من استحقاق الذم إلى استحقاق المدح ، لأنا نقول الكلام فيمن تحقق العلم بأنه يموت كافراً، فعلى تقدير الإيمان يكون الانقلاب ضرورياً، وكذا الكلام فيمن أخبر الله تعالى بأنه لا يؤمن كأبي جهل ، وأبي لهب وأضرابهما ، وقد عرفت أن هذا ليس من المتنازع ، فلا يكون الدليل على هذا وارداً على محل النزاع
And among them is that from whom Allah knows that he will not believe, but will die in unbelief, is obligated to believe unanimously, despite its impossibility for him, because if he believed, Allah's knowledge would necessarily change into ignorance. It is correct to say: we do not concede that if he believed, knowledge would change into ignorance; rather, the knowledge related to him would be from eternity that he dies a believer, for knowledge follows the known. So this is an estimation of knowledge in place of knowledge, not a change of knowledge into ignorance, just as if you suppose the one who actually does evil as doing good, then from the very beginning he would deserve praise, not a turning from deserving blame to deserving praise. We say: the discussion concerns one about whom knowledge is certain that he dies an unbeliever; so on the assumption of faith, the change is necessary. Similarly for those whom Allah informed that they will not believe, like Abu Jahl, Abu Lahab and their likes. And you have known that this is not the disputed point, so this evidence is not directed at the place of dispute.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This is a third argument of the permitters: that someone whom Allah knows will die in unbelief is nevertheless commanded to believe, even though it is impossible for him because it would imply a change in Allah's knowledge. The counter-objection: No, if he believed, then from eternity Allah's knowledge would have been that he dies a believer, since knowledge follows the known. So it is a substitution of knowledge, not a change. The author (Taftazani) then responds that this counter only holds if we suppose a different object of knowledge; but we are discussing the actual case where Allah's knowledge is that he dies an unbeliever. So assuming belief would necessarily change that knowledge. However, the author concludes that this example is not the disputed point anyway.

Key Terms
علم الله
انقلاب العلم
العلم تابع للمعلوم
تقدير علم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The counter-objection successfully refutes the argument from foreknowledge.
Correction: Taftazani shows that the counter only works if the known object were different from the start; in the actual case where divine foreknowledge is certain, assuming belief would necessarily change that knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the counter-objection, if the known object changes, what happens to divine knowledge?
A) It changes to ignorance
B) It would have been different from eternity
C) It remains the same
D) It is abolished

Key Takeaway
The foreknowledge argument rests on the impossibility of change in divine knowledge, but the counter-objection fails because it changes the known object, not the knowledge itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own future, do you live as if Allah's knowledge of your deeds is certain and unchangeable? How does that reality affect the seriousness of your religious obligations today?
?
Have you ever used hypothetical reasoning ('if Allah knew I would do X, then...') to avoid taking responsibility for your actions? What does this lesson teach you about the danger of such excuses?
?
The passage shows that divine knowledge is based on the actual, not hypotheticals. In what areas of your life are you living in fantasies or regrets rather than facing the truth of your current state before Allah?

The Ash'ari Position on Divine Purposes
Sanusi introduces the Ash'ari negation of purpose and the two types of proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari stance on God's actions not being motivated by purposes and the two types of proofs they use: absolute negation and negation of necessity.

Core Idea
God's actions are not for purposes, but some may have wisdom.

Sanusi outlines the Ash'ari doctrine that God's actions are not caused by purposes. He distinguishes two categories of arguments: those proving that purpose is impossible, and those showing that purpose is not necessary. This introductory passage sets the stage for the detailed arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the two types of proofs mentioned.
2
Identify the two types of proofs
Look for the phrases 'luzum al-nafy' and 'nafy al-luzum'. Understand what each entails.
The first type claims impossibility of purpose, the second denies necessity.
3
Reflect on the examples
The passage gives examples for each type. Think about how they illustrate the point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (وأما نفي الغرض) فمن أدلة القوم ما يفيد لزوم النفي كقولهم لو كان فاعلاً لغرض كان ناقصاً في ذاته مستكملاً بغيره. وقولهم قد ثبت استناد الكل إليه ابتداء من غير أن يكون البعض غرضاً وتبعاً للبعض، ومنها ما يفيد نفي اللزوم كقولهم لا بد من الانتهاء إلى ما يكون البعض لغرض قطعاً للسلسلة، وقولهم لا يعقل في مثل تخليد الكفار نفع لأحد، وهذا أقرب تعليل بعض الأفعال سيما شرعية الأحكام مما يشهد به النصوص، ويكاد يقع عليه الإجماع وبه يثبت القياس . ما ذهب إليه الأشاعرة أن أفعال الله تعالى ليست معللة بالأغراض، يفهم من : بعض أدلته عموم السلب ولزوم النفي بمعنى أنه يمتنع أن يكون شيء من أفعاله معللاً بالغرض، ومن بعضها سلب العموم ونفي اللزوم ، أي بمعنى أن ذلك ليس بلازم في كل فعل فمن الأول وجهان : أحدهما : لو كان الباري فاعلاً لغرض لكان ناقصاً في ذاته مستكملاً بتحصيل ذلك الغرض، لأنه لا بد في الغرض من أن يكون وجوده أصلح للفاعل من عدمه، وهو معنى الكمال، لا يقال لعل الغرض يعود إلى الغير فلا تتم الملازمة . لأنا نقول : وحصول ذلك الغرض للغير لابد أن يكون أصلح للفاعل من عدمه، وإلا لم يصلح غرضاً لفعله ضرورة، وحينئذ يعود الإلزام ، وردّ بمنع الضرورة، بل يكفي مجرد كونه أصلح للغير. وثانيهما : لو كان شيء من الممكنات غرضاً لفعل الباري لما كان حاصلاً بخلقه ابتداء بل بتبعية ذلك الفعل وتوسطه لأن ذلك معنى الغرض واللازم باطل لما ثبت استناد الكل إليه ابتداء من غير أن يكون البعض أولى بالغرضية والتبعية من البعض. لا يقال معنى استناد الكل إليه ابتداء أنه الموجد بالاستقلال لكل ممكن لا أن ممكناً. وذلك الممكن ممكناً آخر على ما يراه الفلاسفة. وهذا لا ينافي توقف تحصيل البعض على البعض كالحركة على الجسم ، والوصول إلى المنتهى على الحركة ونحو ذلك ما لا يحصى . لأنا نقول الذي يصلح أن يكون غرضاً لفعله ليس إلا إيصال اللذة إلى العبد، وهو مقدور له تعالى من غير شيء من الوسائط. وردّ بعد تسليم انحصار الغرض فيما ذكر بأن إيصال بعض اللذات قد لا يمكن إلا بخلق وسائط كالإحساس، ووجود ما يلتذ به ونحو ذلك . ومن الثاني وجهان: أحدهما أنه لا بد من انقطاع السلسلة إلى ما يكون غرضاً، ولا يكون لغرض، فلا يصح القول بلزوم الغرض وعمومه. وثانيهما أن مثل تخليد الكفار في النار لا يعقل فيه نفع لأحد، والحق أن تعليل بعض الأفعال لا سيما شرعية الأحكام بالحكم والمصالح ظاهر كايجاب الحدود، والكفارات، وتحريم المسكرات وما أشبه ذلك ، والنصوص أيضاً شاهدة بذلك كقوله تعالى وما خلقت الجن والإنس إلا ليعبدون ومن أجل ذلك كتبنا على بني إسرائيل الآية فلما قضى زيد منها وطراً زوجناكها لكيلا يكون على المؤمنين حرج الآية ولهذا
He said: 'Regarding the negation of purpose, from the proofs of the people are those that entail the necessity of negation, such as their statement: if He were an agent for a purpose, He would be deficient in His essence, perfecting Himself through another. And their statement: it is established that everything originates from Him initially, without some being a purpose and subordinate to others. And among them are those that entail negation of necessity, such as their statement: there must be an end to a series with something that is a purpose, without itself being for a purpose, to cut the chain, and their statement: in something like immortalizing the disbelievers in the Fire, no benefit for anyone is conceivable. And this is closer to explaining some acts, especially the legal rulings, as witnessed by texts, and there is near consensus on it, and by it analogy is established. The Ash'ari school holds that the acts of God Most High are not caused by purposes. It is understood from some of their proofs: absolute negation and necessity of negation, meaning that it is impossible that any of His acts should be caused by a purpose; and from some of them: negation of universality and negation of necessity, meaning that this is not necessary in every act. So from the first [type] there are two arguments: First: if the Creator were an agent for a purpose, He would be deficient in His essence, perfecting Himself through the attainment of that purpose, because a purpose must involve its existence being better for the agent than its absence, which is the meaning of perfection. One cannot say: perhaps the purpose redounds to another, so the entailment does not hold. For we say: the attainment of that purpose for the other must be better for the agent than its absence, otherwise it would not be fit as a purpose for His act necessarily, and then the objection returns. It was refuted by denying the necessity; rather, merely its being better for the other suffices. Second: if something among possibles were a purpose for the Creator's act, it would not occur by His creation initially, but rather through the dependence on and intermediacy of that act, because that is the meaning of purpose. And the consequent is false because it is established that everything originates from Him initially, without some being more deserving of purposeness and subordination than others. One cannot say: the meaning of everything originating from Him initially is that He is the independent originator of every possible, not that one possible is not a possible for another, as the philosophers hold, and this does not contradict the dependence of the attainment of some on others, like motion on a body, and reaching the goal on motion, and the like of what is countless. For we say: that which can be a purpose for His act is only the conveyance of pleasure to the servant, and that is within His power Most High without any intermediaries. It was refuted after granting the restriction of purpose to what was mentioned, by saying that the conveyance of some pleasures may not be possible except through the creation of intermediaries like sensation and the existence of what is enjoyed and the like. And from the second [type] there are two arguments: First: there must be a termination of the chain to that which is a purpose but not for a purpose, so it is not correct to say that purpose is necessary and universal. Second: that something like immortalizing the disbelievers in the Fire cannot be conceived to benefit anyone. And the truth is that explaining some acts, especially legal rulings, by underlying causes and interests is apparent, like imposing fixed punishments, expiations, prohibition of intoxicants, and the like, and the texts also bear witness to that, such as His statement Most High: 'I did not create jinn and mankind except to worship Me' (Qur'an 51:56), and 'Because of that We prescribed for the Children of Israel' (Qur'an 5:32), and 'So when Zayd had completed his desire from her, We married her to you so that there be no restriction for the believers' (Qur'an 33:37). And for this...
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ari doctrine on the negation of purpose (nafy al-gharad) in God's actions. The author first introduces two types of arguments from the Ash'ari school: those that entail the necessity of negation (luzum al-nafy) – meaning it is impossible for any action to be for a purpose – and those that entail the negation of necessity (nafy al-luzum) – meaning purpose is not necessary in every action, but some actions may have purposes. Then he restates the position: some proofs indicate absolute negation, others indicate limited negation. 

Under the first type (luzum al-nafy), he gives two arguments:
1. If God acted for a purpose, He would be deficient in His essence, perfecting Himself through that purpose. The counterargument that the purpose might benefit another is rebutted because the benefit to another must still be better for God, otherwise it wouldn't be a purpose. However, this is refuted by denying that necessity – it suffices that it is better for the other.
2. If any possible thing were a purpose, it would depend on intermediacy, contradicting the fact that everything originates from God initially without mediation. The possible response that interdependence (like motion needing a body) is allowed is refuted by specifying that the only true purpose is conveying pleasure to the servant, which God can do without intermediaries. But this is further refuted by noting that some pleasures require intermediaries.

Under the second type (nafy al-luzum), he gives two arguments:
1. There must be an end to the chain of purposes – something that is a purpose but not itself for a purpose – so purpose cannot be universal or necessary.
2. Acts like immortalizing disbelievers in Hell have no conceivable benefit for anyone, so they cannot be for a purpose.

The passage concludes by affirming that explaining some acts, especially legal rulings, by underlying causes and interests is apparent and supported by texts, giving examples from the Qur'an.

Key Terms
نفي الغرض
لزوم النفي
نفي اللزوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari negation of purpose means God's actions are random or without wisdom.
Correction: The Ash'aris deny that purposes motivate God (as a need), but affirm that actions may have benefits and wisdoms.

Checkpoint
What are the two types of Ash'ari proofs regarding God's purposes?
A) Proofs from revelation and proofs from reason
B) Proofs that entail necessity of negation and proofs that entail negation of necessity
C) Proofs about divine essence and proofs about human benefit
D) Proofs from philosophers and proofs from theologians

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari stance on divine purposes is nuanced: some proofs absolutely negate purpose, while others only show that purpose is not necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a calamity, do you instinctively search for a 'purpose' behind it, as if God must have a reason to explain to you? How does the Ash'ari teaching that God acts without purpose challenge your need for explanations, and what does that demand from your trust in Him?
?
Reflect on the statement that 'eternal punishment of disbelievers cannot be conceived to benefit anyone'. If you cannot see any benefit in it, does that make it unjust according to your human sense of fairness? How does the negation of divine purpose force you to submit to God's absolute sovereignty even when His actions seem incomprehensible?
?
The passage notes that some divine acts, like legal rulings, can be explained by wisdom (hikma). How often do you expect God's commands to be 'reasonable' by your standards? Are you willing to obey without knowing the wisdom, leaving the explanation to be a grace rather than a right?
