The Grammatical Definition of Ism and the Distinction of the Three Terms
Grammatical definition of ism and distinction of ism, musamma, tasmiya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the grammatical definition of 'ism' and the clear distinction between ism, musamma, and tasmiya.

Core Idea
Ism, musamma, and tasmiya are distinct; confusion arises when they are conflated.

This lesson begins with a grammatical definition of 'ism' (name) as a single word assigned to a meaning. The author then clearly distinguishes between three related terms: ism (name), musamma (the named), and tasmiya (naming). This distinction is foundational for understanding the subsequent discussion on Allah's names.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on how the author defines 'ism' grammatically and then defines the other two terms.
2
Identify the three terms and their definitions
Note the definitions: ism is the word itself, musamma is the meaning, tasmiya is the act of naming. Understand the author's assertion that they are distinct.
The author states 'there is no hiddenness in the distinction of these three things'.
3
Reflect on why this distinction matters
Consider that later theological debates depend on whether 'name' is identical to 'named' or not. This distinction sets the stage.
This connects to the next lesson on al-Ash'ari's division.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هو اللفظ المفرد الموضوع للمعنى على ما يعم أنواع الكلمة، وقد يقيد بالاستقلال والتجرد عن الزمان، فيقابل الفعل والحرف على ما هو مصطلح النحاة، والمسمى هو المعنى الذي وضع الاسم بإزائه، والتسمية هو وضع الاسم للمعنى وقد يراد بها ذكر الشيء باسمه كما يقال زيداً، ولم يسم عمراً، فلا خفاء في تغاير الأمور الثلاثة، وإنما الخفاء فيما ذهب إليه بعض أصحابنا من أن الاسم نفس المسمى، وفيما ذكره الشيخ الأشعري من أن أسماء الله تعالى ثلاثة أقسام، ما هو نفس المسمى، مثل الله الدال على الوجود أي الذات، وما هو غيره كالخالق، والرازق، ونحو ذلك مما يدل على فعل وما لا يقال انه هو، ولا غيره، كالعالم، والقادر، وكل ما يدل على الصفات القديمة، وأما التسمية فغير الاسم والمسمى وتوضيحه أنهم يريدون بالتسمية اللفظ وبالاسم مدلوله، كما يريدون بالوصف قول الواصف وبالصفة مدلوله، وكما يقولون إن القراءة حادثة والمقروء قديم، إلا أن الأصحاب اعتبروا المدلول المطابقي فأطلقوا القول بأن الاسم نفس المسمى للقطع بأن مدلول الخالق شيء ما له الخلق لا نفس الخلق، ومدلول
It is the single word assigned to a meaning, in a way that encompasses the types of kalimah (word). And it may be restricted by independence (al-istiqlal) and being free from time, thus contrasting with the verb and the particle according to the terminology of the grammarians. The named (almusamma) is the meaning for which the name is set, and the naming (altasmiya) is the assignment of the name to the meaning, and sometimes it is intended to mention a thing by its name, as is said 'Zaydan' and he did not name 'Amran.' So there is no hiddenness in the distinction of these three things. The hiddenness is only in what some of our companions (as-habina) have held—that the name is the named itself—and in what al-Shaykh al-Ash'ari mentioned: that the names of Allah Almighty are three divisions: what is the named itself, like 'Allah' indicating the existence, i.e., the essence; what is other than it, like 'al-Khaliq,' 'al-Razzaq' and the like indicating an action; and what is not said to be it nor other, like 'al-Alim,' 'al-Qadir' and everything indicating the eternal attributes. As for naming, it is other than the name and the named. Its clarification is that by 'naming' they intend the word, and by 'name' its referent (madlul), just as by 'description' (wasf) they intend the saying of the describer, and by 'attribute' (sifa) its referent; and just as they say that the recitation (qira'a) is originated and the recited (maqru') is eternal. However, the companions (al-ashab) considered the referent of correspondence (almutabaqa) and thus stated absolutely that the name is the named itself, because it is certain that the referent of 'al-Khaliq' is something that has creating, not the creating itself; and the referent of
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage provides a grammatical definition of 'ism' (name) and then presents al-Ash'ari's threefold classification of Allah's names. The author first defines 'ism' technically as a single word (lafz mufrad) assigned to a meaning, covering all parts of speech. In grammar, it is restricted by independence (istqlal) and being free of time, thus distinguishing it from verbs and particles. He reiterates the definitions of 'named' and 'naming' and asserts that their distinction is clear. The real source of hiddenness is the claim of some Ash'ari scholars (as-habuna) that the name is the named itself, and al-Ash'ari's division of divine names into three categories: (1) names that are identical with the named (e.g., 'Allah' indicating the existence/essence), (2) names that are other than the named (e.g., 'al-Khaliq' indicating an action), and (3) names that are neither identical nor other (e.g., 'al-Alim' indicating eternal attributes). The author then clarifies that in this scheme, 'naming' (tasmiya) is distinct from both name and named; it refers to the word itself, while 'name' refers to the referent (madlul). He draws an analogy with 'description' (wasf) being the act of describing and 'attribute' (sifa) being the thing described, and with 'recitation' (qira'a) being created (haditha) and 'the recited' (maqru') being eternal (qadim). He notes that some Ash'ari scholars considered only the referent of correspondence (madlul mutabaqi) and thus held that the name is the named itself, because, for example, the referent of 'al-Khaliq' is the entity that has the attribute of creation, not the creation itself. The passage ends abruptly due to page break.

Key Terms
اللفظ المفرد
الاستقلال
التجرد عن الزمان
المسمى
التسمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that the name (ism) is identical to the named (musamma) or that naming (tasmiya) is the same as the name.
Correction: The passage clearly distinguishes ism (the word), musamma (the meaning), and tasmiya (the act of naming) as three different things.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the grammatical definition of 'ism'?
A) A verb indicating an action.
B) A single word assigned to a meaning, covering all types of kalimah.
C) The meaning for which a word is set.
D) The act of assigning a name.

Key Takeaway
Ism, musamma, and tasmiya are three distinct concepts; ism is the word, musamma is the meaning, and tasmiya is the act of naming.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say the name 'Allah', do you ever slip into thinking that the word itself is the reality of Allah? How does this lesson's distinction between ism and musamma protect you from anthropomorphism or irreverence?
?
In your daily recitation of the Quran, do you approach the words as mere sounds or as something more? How does realizing that the tasmiya (naming) is an act of God deepen your awe and intention when you recite?
?
Have you ever argued with someone about the 'name' of Allah, only to realize you were conflating the word with the named? How can this lesson change the way you engage in such discussions?

Al-Ash'ari's Classification of Allah's Names: Three Categories
Al-Ash'ari's threefold division of Allah's names and clarification of naming vs. name.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ash'ari's threefold division of Allah's names: identical with the named, other than the named, and neither identical nor other.

Core Idea
Al-Ash'ari divided Allah's names into three categories based on their referent: essence, actions, and eternal attributes.

After establishing the distinction between name, named, and naming, the author introduces al-Ash'ari's classification of Allah's names into three categories. This division is crucial for understanding how Ash'ari theologians conceive the relationship between divine names and the Divine Essence and attributes. The passage also clarifies that 'naming' (tasmiya) refers to the word itself, while 'name' refers to its referent (madlul).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on al-Ash'ari's division
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'وإنما الخفاء' to the end. Note the three categories and the examples given.
The text is incomplete due to a page break but we have enough for the classification.
2
Identify the three categories
Find the three categories: (1) what is the named itself (e.g., Allah), (2) what is other than the named (e.g., al-Khaliq), (3) what is neither it nor other (e.g., al-Alim).
The third category includes names indicating eternal attributes.
3
Understand the clarification about naming
The author clarifies that tasmiya is distinct from ism and musamma; tasmiya is the word (lafz), while ism is the referent (madlul). An analogy with wasf/sifa and qira'a/maqru' is given.
4
Reflect on why the companions held that the name is the named
The companions considered the referent of correspondence (madlul mutabaqi) and thus said the name is the named itself, because the referent of 'al-Khaliq' is the entity that has creating, not the creating itself. This is the basis for the third category.
This connects to the earlier distinction.

Commentary
This passage provides a grammatical definition of 'ism' (name) and then presents al-Ash'ari's threefold classification of Allah's names. The author first defines 'ism' technically as a single word (lafz mufrad) assigned to a meaning, covering all parts of speech. In grammar, it is restricted by independence (istqlal) and being free of time, thus distinguishing it from verbs and particles. He reiterates the definitions of 'named' and 'naming' and asserts that their distinction is clear. The real source of hiddenness is the claim of some Ash'ari scholars (as-habuna) that the name is the named itself, and al-Ash'ari's division of divine names into three categories: (1) names that are identical with the named (e.g., 'Allah' indicating the existence/essence), (2) names that are other than the named (e.g., 'al-Khaliq' indicating an action), and (3) names that are neither identical nor other (e.g., 'al-Alim' indicating eternal attributes). The author then clarifies that in this scheme, 'naming' (tasmiya) is distinct from both name and named; it refers to the word itself, while 'name' refers to the referent (madlul). He draws an analogy with 'description' (wasf) being the act of describing and 'attribute' (sifa) being the thing described, and with 'recitation' (qira'a) being created (haditha) and 'the recited' (maqru') being eternal (qadim). He notes that some Ash'ari scholars considered only the referent of correspondence (madlul mutabaqi) and thus held that the name is the named itself, because, for example, the referent of 'al-Khaliq' is the entity that has the attribute of creation, not the creation itself. The passage ends abruptly due to page break.

Correction: The passage clarifies that names indicating attributes like 'al-Alim' are in a category where they are not said to be the named itself nor other than it, but indicate the eternal attributes.

When you reflect on your own life, which category of names do you need to call upon more—those that remind you of God's ongoing provision, or those that affirm His unchanging knowledge and power?
?

Are you content with mere recitation, or does this compel you to live out the meaning of His names—especially those like al-Alim that demand accountability for your hidden thoughts?

Al-Ash'ari's Classification of Divine Names
Presentation of al-Ash'ari's three categories and rational proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ash'ari's threefold classification of divine names and the rational argument supporting his view.

Core Idea
Divine names are not all the same: some indicate essence, actions, or attributes in a unique middle way.

Al-Ash'ari proposed a nuanced classification of divine names into three categories based on their relationship to God's essence. His approach sparked debate and relies on rational proofs. This passage introduces the classification and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
Read the passage and list the three categories al-Ash'ari distinguishes. Note the examples: 'Allah', 'Creator', 'Knower'.
The categories are: identical to the named, other than the named, neither identical nor other.
2
Understand name vs. naming vs. named
Grasp the distinction between tasmiya (naming/word), ism (name/meaning), and musamma (named/entity). The companions considered only the congruent meaning.
This distinction is crucial for understanding later debates.
3
Compare al-Ash'ari with the companions
See how al-Ash'ari takes the intended meanings (al-ma'ani al-maqsuda) rather than just the congruent meaning, leading to a different analysis of attribute names.
For attributes, the meaning is the attribute itself, not the entity.
4
Examine the rational proof
Follow the argument: if names (like 'Knower') were other than the essence, they'd be created; God would not be Knower in eternity. For action names, 'Creator' means ability, not actual creation.
This preserves God's eternality and avoids createdness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيما ذكره الشيخ الأشعري من أن أسماء الله تعالى ثلاثة أقسام، ما هو نفس المسمى مثل الله الدال على الوجود أي الذات، وما هو غيره كالخالق والرازق ونحو ذلك مما يدل على فعل، وما لا يقال إنه هو ولا غيره كالعالم والقادر وكل ما يدل على الصفات القديمة، وأما التسمية فغير الاسم والمسمى وتوضيحه أنهم يريدون بالتسمية اللفظ وبالاسم مدلوله، كما يريدون بالوصف قول الواصف وبالصفة مدلوله، وكما يقولون إن القراءة حادثة والمقروء قديم، إلا أن الأصحاب اعتبروا المدلول المطابقي فأطلقوا القول بأن الاسم نفس المسمى للقطع بأن مدلول الخالق شيء ما له الخلق لا نفس الخلق، ومدلول العالم شيء ما له العلم لا نفس العلم، والشيخ أخذ المدلول أعم، واعتبر في أسماء الصفات المعاني المقصودة، فزعم أن مدلول الخالق الخلق، وهو غير الذات ومدلول العالم العلم وهو لا عين ولا غير وتمسكوا في ذلك بالعقل والنقل، أما العقل، فلأنه لو كانت الأسماء غير الذات لكانت حادثة، فلم يكن الباري تعالى في الأزل إلهاً وعالماً وقادراً ونحو ذلك وهو محال بخلاف الخالقية فإنه يلزم من قدمها قدم المخلوق إذا أريد الخالق بالفعل، كالقاطع في قولنا السيف قاطع عند الوقوع بخلاف قولنا السيف قاطع في الغمد، بمعنى أن من شأنه ذلك، فإن الخالق ح معناه الاقتدار على ذلك
And among what Sheikh al-Ash'ari mentioned is that the names of God Almighty are three categories: that which is identical to the named, like 'Allah', which indicates existence, i.e., the essence; that which is other than it, like 'the Creator', 'the Provider', and similar names that indicate an action; and that which is not said to be it nor other than it, like 'the Knower', 'the Powerful', and every name that indicates the eternal attributes. As for naming (tasmiya), it is other than the name and the named. The clarification is that by 'naming' they mean the word, and by 'name' its meaning, just as by 'description' (wasf) they mean the statement of the describer, and by 'attribute' (sifa) its meaning; and just as they say that 'recitation' (qira'a) is created, while 'the recited' (maqru') is eternal. However, the companions considered the congruent meaning (al-madlul al-mutabiqi) and thus declared that the name is identical to the named, because they were certain that the meaning of 'Creator' is something that has creation, not creation itself, and the meaning of 'Knower' is something that has knowledge, not knowledge itself. The Sheikh took the meaning more generally, and considered in the names of attributes the intended meanings (al-ma'ani al-maqsuda), so he held that the meaning of 'Creator' is creation, which is other than the essence, and the meaning of 'Knower' is knowledge, which is neither identical nor other. They supported this with reason and tradition. As for reason: because if the names were other than the essence, they would be created, and thus the Exalted would not be in eternity a God, Knower, Powerful, etc., which is impossible. Unlike creatorship (al-khaliqiyya), for its eternity would necessitate the eternity of the creature if 'Creator' is meant in the actual sense, like 'cutter' in our statement 'the sword is cutter' upon occurrence, unlike our statement 'the sword is cutter in the scabbard', meaning that it is its property to do that. For the meaning of 'Creator' is the ability for that.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage presents al-Ash'ari's classification of divine names and the rational proof supporting it. The author states that al-Ash'ari divides the names of God into three categories: (1) those identical to the named, like 'Allah' which indicates the essence; (2) those other than the essence, like 'Creator' and 'Provider', which indicate actions; and (3) those that are neither said to be identical nor other, like 'Knower' and 'Powerful', which indicate the eternal attributes. The author then clarifies that 'naming' (tasmiya) is distinct from both name and named: 'naming' refers to the word, while 'name' refers to its meaning. He compares this to the distinction between 'description' (wasf) as the speech of the describer and 'attribute' (sifa) as its meaning, and the example of 'recitation' (qira'a) being created while 'the recited' (maqru') is eternal. The author notes that some Ash'ari companions (al-ashab) considered only the congruent meaning (al-madlul al-mutabiqi) and thus claimed that the name is identical to the named, reasoning that the meaning of 'Creator' is something that has creation, not creation itself. Al-Ash'ari, however, took the meaning more generally, considering the intended meanings of attribute names, so he held that the meaning of 'Creator' is creation itself, which is other than the essence, and the meaning of 'Knower' is knowledge, which is neither identical nor other. The proof for al-Ash'ari's view is both rational (aql) and traditional (naql). The rational proof is explained: if the names were other than the essence, they would be created, and then God would not be God, Knower, Powerful, etc., in eternity, which is impossible. In contrast, the attribute of creatorship (al-khaliqiyya) can be understood as a potentiality, like saying 'the sword is cutter' while still in the scabbard, meaning it has the property of cutting, not the actual action. Thus, the meaning of 'Creator' is the ability (iqtidar) to create, which does not necessitate temporal creation.

Correction: Al-Ash'ari posits a third category for names indicating eternal attributes (like 'Knower') which are neither identical nor other.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari's classification of divine names into three categories, supported by rational proof, preserves God's eternity and the unique status of His attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you invoke Allah as Al-'Aleem (The All-Knowing), do you truly believe that His knowledge is eternal and not a created attribute? How does this certainty affect your trust in His omniscience in moments of doubt?

Transmitted Proof: Name vs. Named
Provides Quranic proof and responds to the objection about Muhammad's prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the transmitted proof from the Quran that distinguishes between the name (word) and the named (essence), and how the objection that this distinction threatens meaningful statements is refuted.

Core Idea
The name points to the named; rulings refer to meanings, not words.

How does the Quran itself help us distinguish between a name and the thing it names? In this passage, the author presents two Quranic verses as transmitted proof that the name is not identical to the named. He then tackles a clever objection: if the name is different, then saying 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah' would not refer to the Prophet but to the word 'Muhammad'. The response reveals how language works—names are indicators, and rulings follow meanings.

Guided Path
1
Read the transmitted proof verses
Read the two Quranic verses cited: 'Glorify the name of your Lord' (87:1) and 'You worship not besides Him except names which you have named' (12:40). Notice how each verse implies a distinction between the name and the named.
The first verse shows glorification is for the essence, not the word. The second shows worship is for the named idols, not the names.
2
Understand the objection
The objection states: if the name is not the named, then the statement 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah' would not ascribe messengerhood to the Prophet but to the word 'Muhammad'—which is absurd. Identify why this objection seems plausible at first.
The opponent assumes that the name and named must be identical for the statement to work.
3
See how the refutation works
The author dismisses the objection as a feeble doubt. He explains that the name is an indicator (dalil) of the named. In normal speech, we mention words but rulings apply to the meanings they indicate. For example, 'Zayd is a writer' refers to the person, not the word. Only with contextual clues does the ruling refer to the word itself, as in 'Zayd is written'.
This shows that the objection confuses the normal function of language.
4
Note the two answers to additional objections
The passage gives two answers: (1) The meanings of divinity and knowledge are eternal; absence of the word does not negate the meaning. (2) The glorification in the verse means sanctifying the essence, not the word—or it is a metonymy. The second answer is cut off but the idea is clear.
These answers reinforce that the distinction does not undermine theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النقل فلقوله تعالى سبح اسم ربك والتسبيح إنما هو للذات دون اللفظ وقوله تعالى وما تعبدون من دونه إلا أسماء سميتموها وعبادتهم إنما هي للأصنام التي هي المسميات دون الأسامي، وأما التمسك بأن الاسم لو كان غير المسمى لما كان قولنا محمد رسول الله حكماً بثبوت الرسالة للنبي بل لغيره فشبهة واهية، فإن الاسم وإن لم يكن نفس المسمى لكنه دال عليه، ووضع الكلام على أن تذكر الألفاظ ويرجع الأحكام إلى المدلولات، كقولنا زيد كاتب أي مدلول زيد متصف بمعنى الكتابة، وقد يرجع بمعونة القرينة إلى نفس اللفظ كما في قولنا : زيد مكتوب وثلاثي، ومعرب، ونحو ذلك، وأجيب عن الأول : بأن الثابت في الأزل معنى الإلهية، والعلم، ولا يلزم من انتفاء الاسم بمعنى اللفظ، انتفاء ذلك المعنى، وعن الثاني : بأن معنى تسبيح الاسم ،تقديسه، وتنزيهه، عن أن يسمى به الغير أو عن يفسر بما لا يليق، أو عن يذكر على غير وجه التعظيم، أو هو كناية عن
As for the transmitted proof, it is His statement exalted: 'Glorify the name of your Lord' (87:1); and glorification is only of the essence, not the word. And His statement: 'You worship not besides Him except names which you have named' (12:40); and their worship is only of the idols, which are the named things, not the names. As for the claim that if the name were other than the named, then our statement 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah' would not be a ruling establishing messengerhood for the Prophet, but for other than him, it is a feeble doubt. For the name, even if not identical to the named, is an indicator of it, and the usage of speech is that words are mentioned and rulings refer to the meanings, like our saying 'Zayd is a writer', i.e., the meaning of Zayd is described by the meaning of writing. And it may refer, with the help of a contextual clue, to the word itself, as in our saying: 'Zayd is written, triliteral, and inflected', etc. And the first (objection) was answered: that the meaning of divinity and knowledge are established in eternity, and the absence of the name in the sense of the word does not necessitate the absence of that meaning. And the second (objection) was answered: that the meaning of glorification of the name is its sanctification and exaltation from being used for other than Him, or from being interpreted in an unfitting manner, or from being mentioned other than with veneration, or it is a metonymy for ...
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage presents the transmitted (naql) proof from the Quran and then responds to an objection. The author cites two Quranic verses: 'Glorify the name of your Lord' (Q. 87:1) and 'You worship not besides Him except names which you have named' (Q. 12:40). The first verse is used to show that the glorification is directed to the essence, not the word, indicating a distinction between the name (word) and the named (essence). The second verse shows that idol worship is directed at the named entities (idols), not the names themselves, again supporting that the name is not the named. The author then addresses an objection: if the name were not identical to the named, then the statement 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah' would not establish prophethood for Muhammad, but for the word 'Muhammad' instead, which is absurd. The author dismisses this as a feeble doubt, explaining that the name, though not identical, is an indicator (dalil) of the named. In normal discourse, we mention words but the rulings refer to the meanings. For example, saying 'Zayd is a writer' means the person Zayd is described with writing, not the word. Only with contextual clues does the ruling refer to the word itself, as in 'Zayd is written' (i.e., the word Zayd is written). The author then gives two answers: (1) The meanings of divinity and knowledge are eternal; the absence of the name (word) does not imply the absence of the meaning. (2) The glorification of the name in the verse means sanctifying and exalting the essence, not the word, or it is a metaphorical expression. The text cuts off during the explanation of the second answer.

The Rational Proof That Names Are Not Other Than the Essence
Author presents a rational proof for distinction between names and essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that the names of God (attributes) cannot be other than His essence, because otherwise they would be originated and God would not have been described by them in pre-eternity.

Core Idea
If names were separate from God, He would not be eternally knower.

The author presents a rational proof that names like 'Knower' and 'Powerful' are not separate entities from God's essence. He shows that if they were, they would be created, meaning God would not have possessed those descriptions in pre-eternity—a clear impossibility. He also clarifies that the attribute of 'Creator' refers to the power to create, not the act itself, to avoid implying the pre-eternity of creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the rational proof passage
Focus on the conditional reasoning: if names were other than essence, they would be originated.
2
Understand the consequence
God would not be called Knower, etc., in pre-eternity, which is impossible.
3
Note the sword analogy
The author distinguishes 'creator in act' from 'creator by power' using the example of a sword that is a cutter in the scabbard.
4
Reflect on the support for Ash'ari position
This proof supports the view that attributes are not other than the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العالم شيء ما له العلم لا نفس العلم، والشيخ أخذ المدلول أعم، واعتبر في أسماء الصفات المعاني المقصودة، فزعم أن مدلول الخالق الخلق، وهو غير الذات ومدلول العالم العلم وهو لا عين ولا غير وتمسكوا في ذلك بالعقل والنقل، أما العقل، فلأنه لو كانت الأسماء غير الذات لكانت حادثة، فلم يكن الباري تعالى في الأزل إلهاً وعالماً وقادراً ونحو ذلك وهو محال بخلاف الخالقية فإنه يلزم من قدمها قدم المخلوق إذا أريد الخالق بالفعل، كالقاطع في قولنا السيف قاطع عند الوقوع بخلاف قولنا السيف قاطع في الغمد، بمعنى أن من شأنه ذلك، فإن الخالق ح معناه الاقتدار على ذلك، وأما النقل فلقوله تعالى ﴿سبح اسم ربك﴾ والتسبيح إنما هو للذات دون اللفظ وقوله تعالى ﴿وما تعبدون من دونه إلا أسماء سميتموها﴾ وعبادتهم إنما هي للأصنام التي هي المسميات دون الأسامي، وأما التمسك بأن الاسم لو كان غير المسمى لما كان قولنا محمد رسول الله حكماً بثبوت الرسالة للنبي، بل لغيره فشبهة واهية. فإن الاسم وإن لم يكن نفس المسمى لكنه دال عليه، ووضع الكلام على أن تذكر الألفاظ ويرجع الأحكام إلى المدلولات، كقولنا زيد كاتب أي مدلول زيد متصف بمعنى الكتابة، وقد يرجع بمعونة القرينة إلى نفس اللفظ كما في قولنا: زيد مكتوب وثلاثي، ومعرب، ونحو ذلك.
The knower is something that has knowledge, not knowledge itself. And the Shaykh took the signification more general and considered in the names of attributes the intended meanings, so he claimed that the signification of 'al-Khaliq' (the Creator) is creation, which is other than the essence, and the signification of 'al-'Alim' (the Knower) is knowledge, which is neither an entity nor other. And they adhered in that to reason and transmitted revelation. As for reason, because if the names were other than the essence, they would be originated, and then the Exalted Creator would not have been in pre-eternity God, Knower, Powerful, and the like, which is impossible. Unlike creatorship (al-khaliqiyya), for its pre-eternity would necessitate the pre-eternity of the creature if what is meant is the Creator in act, like 'the cutter' in our saying 'the sword is cutter' at the moment of occurrence, contrary to our saying 'the sword is cutter in the scabbard', meaning that it is its property. For 'al-Khaliq' here means the ability for that. As for transmitted revelation, due to His statement 'Glorify the name of your Lord,' and glorification is only for the essence, not the word; and His statement 'And you do not worship besides Him except names which you have named,' and their worship is only for the idols, which are the named, not the names. As for the claim that if the name were other than the named, then our saying 'Muhammad is the Messenger of God' would not be a judgment of the establishment of the message for the Prophet, but for someone else, it is a weak doubt. For the name, even if it is not the named itself, indicates it, and the norm of speech is that words are mentioned and judgments refer to the significations (madlulat). Like our saying 'Zayd is a writer,' meaning the signification of Zayd is described with the meaning of writing. And sometimes by the aid of context it refers to the word itself, as in our saying 'Zayd is written, triliteral, declinable,' and the like.

Commentary
This passage presents the view that the names of God are distinct from His essence, and gives proofs. It begins by explaining the position of 'the Shaykh' (likely al-Ash'ari) that the signification (madlul) of names like 'al-Khaliq' is creation, which is other than the essence, while the signification of 'al-'Alim' is knowledge, which is neither an entity nor other (i.e., it is an attribute not identical to essence nor completely separate). The author then gives the rational proof: if names were other than the essence, they would be originated, and God would not have been called God, Knower, Powerful in pre-eternity—which is impossible. He contrasts this with the attribute of 'creatorship' (al-khaliqiyya): if taken as active creation (creator in act), pre-eternity would imply pre-eternity of the creature, which is avoided by taking 'al-Khaliq' as meaning the power to create, like calling a sword 'cutter' even when sheathed. Then he gives a transmission proof from two Quranic verses: 'Glorify the name of your Lord' and 'You do not worship besides Him except names you have named'—arguing that glorification and worship are directed to the essence or the named, not the words. Finally, he refutes an objection: if name were other than named, the statement 'Muhammad is the Messenger of God' would not attribute messengerhood to Muhammad but to the word; he replies that the name indicates the named, and judgments refer to the signification (madlul), not the word itself, except when context (qarina) specifies the word (as in grammatical descriptions).

Key Terms
العقل (al-'aql: intellect)
الأزل (al-azal: pre-eternity)
الاسم (al-ism: name)
الذات (al-dhat: essence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The rational proof shows that all names (including 'Creator') are identical to the essence in the same way.
Correction: While the proof shows names are not other than the essence, 'Creator' is understood as the power to create, not the act, to avoid implying pre-eternity of creation.

Checkpoint
According to the rational proof, what would happen if the names were other than the essence?
A) They would be eternal.
B) They would be originated.
C) They would be identical to the essence.
D) They would be neither.

Key Takeaway
The rational proof shows that God's names (attributes) cannot be separate from His essence, because that would make them originated, contradicting His pre-eternal perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's names are not separate from His essence, then when you say 'Ya Allah' or 'Ya Rahman', are you truly addressing His very being, or do you sometimes treat His names as mere labels? How does this truth reshape your conviction when you call upon Him?
?
The author's proof relies on the impossibility of God lacking perfection in pre‑eternity. But in your heart, have you ever felt that God's knowledge or power might have been added later? Today, are you willing to completely reject any thought that diminishes His eternal perfection, even in subtle ways?
?
This lesson uses a rational proof to defend a core belief. Are you someone who embraces such intellectual rigor in your faith, or do you find it uncomfortable? Will you commit to studying more proofs like this, so that your faith is built on both heart and mind?

The Transmission Proof That Names Are Other Than the Essence and Refutation of a Doubt
Author presents a transmission proof from the Quran and refutes an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the transmitted evidence from the Quran that names are distinct from the named, and how the author refutes the objection that this would break the reference of statements like 'Muhammad is the Messenger of God.'

Core Idea
The Quran shows worship and glorification are for the named, not the name.

The author continues by presenting two Quranic verses as evidence for the distinction between names and the named. The first, 'Glorify the name of your Lord,' shows glorification is for the essence, not the word. The second, 'You do not worship besides Him except names you have named,' indicates that worship is directed to the idols themselves (the named), not the names. Then he refutes a potential objection: if a name is different from the named, the statement 'Muhammad is the Messenger of God' would not refer to Muhammad. He explains that names indicate the named, and judgments refer to the signification, not the word.

Guided Path
1
Read the transmission proof passage
Identify the two Quranic verses and how they are used.
2
Understand the objection
The concern about 'Muhammad is the Messenger of God' if name and named are distinct.
3
Follow the author's response
Names indicate the named; speech conventions rely on context.
4
Consider the examples
Notice the difference between 'Zayd is a writer' and 'Zayd is written'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العالم شيء ما له العلم لا نفس العلم، والشيخ أخذ المدلول أعم، واعتبر في أسماء الصفات المعاني المقصودة، فزعم أن مدلول الخالق الخلق، وهو غير الذات ومدلول العالم العلم وهو لا عين ولا غير وتمسكوا في ذلك بالعقل والنقل، أما العقل، فلأنه لو كانت الأسماء غير الذات لكانت حادثة، فلم يكن الباري تعالى في الأزل إلهاً وعالماً وقادراً ونحو ذلك وهو محال بخلاف الخالقية فإنه يلزم من قدمها قدم المخلوق إذا أريد الخالق بالفعل، كالقاطع في قولنا السيف قاطع عند الوقوع بخلاف قولنا السيف قاطع في الغمد، بمعنى أن من شأنه ذلك، فإن الخالق ح معناه الاقتدار على ذلك، وأما النقل فلقوله تعالى ﴿سبح اسم ربك﴾ والتسبيح إنما هو للذات دون اللفظ وقوله تعالى ﴿وما تعبدون من دونه إلا أسماء سميتموها﴾ وعبادتهم إنما هي للأصنام التي هي المسميات دون الأسامي، وأما التمسك بأن الاسم لو كان غير المسمى لما كان قولنا محمد رسول الله حكماً بثبوت الرسالة للنبي، بل لغيره فشبهة واهية. فإن الاسم وإن لم يكن نفس المسمى لكنه دال عليه، ووضع الكلام على أن تذكر الألفاظ ويرجع الأحكام إلى المدلولات، كقولنا زيد كاتب أي مدلول زيد متصف بمعنى الكتابة، وقد يرجع بمعونة القرينة إلى نفس اللفظ كما في قولنا: زيد مكتوب وثلاثي، ومعرب، ونحو ذلك.
The knower is something that has knowledge, not knowledge itself. And the Shaykh took the signification more general and considered in the names of attributes the intended meanings, so he claimed that the signification of 'al-Khaliq' (the Creator) is creation, which is other than the essence, and the signification of 'al-'Alim' (the Knower) is knowledge, which is neither an entity nor other. And they adhered in that to reason and transmitted revelation. As for reason, because if the names were other than the essence, they would be originated, and then the Exalted Creator would not have been in pre-eternity God, Knower, Powerful, and the like, which is impossible. Unlike creatorship (al-khaliqiyya), for its pre-eternity would necessitate the pre-eternity of the creature if what is meant is the Creator in act, like 'the cutter' in our saying 'the sword is cutter' at the moment of occurrence, contrary to our saying 'the sword is cutter in the scabbard', meaning that it is its property. For 'al-Khaliq' here means the ability for that. As for transmitted revelation, due to His statement 'Glorify the name of your Lord,' and glorification is only for the essence, not the word; and His statement 'And you do not worship besides Him except names which you have named,' and their worship is only for the idols, which are the named, not the names. As for the claim that if the name were other than the named, then our saying 'Muhammad is the Messenger of God' would not be a judgment of the establishment of the message for the Prophet, but for someone else, it is a weak doubt. For the name, even if it is not the named itself, indicates it, and the norm of speech is that words are mentioned and judgments refer to the significations (madlulat). Like our saying 'Zayd is a writer,' meaning the signification of Zayd is described with the meaning of writing. And sometimes by the aid of context it refers to the word itself, as in our saying 'Zayd is written, triliteral, declinable,' and the like.

Commentary
This passage presents the view that the names of God are distinct from His essence, and gives proofs. Response to the First Argument: Eternity of Meanings
Author gives the first answer about eternity of meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the eternity of divine meanings despite the temporality of names and words.

Core Idea
The meaning is eternal even if the word is not.

The opponent argued that if names are not identical to the named, then God could not be eternally described as 'God' or 'Knower'. The author responds by distinguishing between the eternal meaning and the temporal word.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the concise response.
2
Identify the key distinction
The author distinguishes between 'ma'na' (meaning) and 'lafz' (word). Understand that the meaning is eternal, the word is temporal.
This distinction is central to the answer.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: how does this preserve God's eternal attributes? The objection fails because it conflates the word with the meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجيب عن الأول: بأن الثابت في الأزل معنى الإلهية، والعلم، ولا يلزم من انتفاء الاسم بمعنى اللفظ انتفاء ذلك المعنى. وعن الثاني: بأن معنى تسبيح الاسم، تقديسه، وتنزيهه، عن أن يسمى به الغير أو عن يفسر بما لا يليق، أو عن يذكر على غير وجه التعظيم، أو هو كناية عن تسبيح الذات كما في قولهم: سلام على المجلس الشريف، والجناب المنيف وفيه من التعظيم والإجلال ما لا يخفى، أو لفظ الاسم مقحم كما في قول الشاعر: ثم اسم السلام عليكما. ومعنى عبادة الأسماء أنهم يعبدون الأصنام التي ليس فيها من الإلهية إلا مجرد الاسم. كمن سمى نفسه بالسلطان، وليست عنده آلات السلطنة وأسبابها، فيقال إنه فرح من السلطنة بالاسم على أن في تقرير الاستدلال اعترافاً بالمغايرة حيث يقال التسبيح لذات الرب دون اسمه. والعبادة لذوات الأصنام دون أساميها، بل ربما يدعي أن في الآيتين دلالة على المغايرة حيث أضيف الاسم إلى الرب، وجعل الأسماء بتسميتهم، وجعلهم مع القطع بأن أشخاص الأصنام ليست كذلك.
And it was answered concerning the first: that what is established in pre-eternity is the meaning of divinity and knowledge, and it does not follow from the absence of the name in the sense of the word the absence of that meaning. And concerning the second: that the meaning of glorifying the name is sanctifying it and declaring it transcendent from being named with other than Him, or from being interpreted in an improper way, or from being mentioned without reverence; or it is a metonymy for glorifying the essence, as in their saying: 'Peace be upon the noble assembly and the lofty court,' and in it there is reverence and awe that is not hidden; or the word 'name' is superfluous as in the poet's saying: 'Then the name of peace be upon you both.' And the meaning of worshipping the names is that they worship the idols which possess from divinity nothing but mere name. Like one who calls himself 'the sultan' while he has none of the instruments and means of sultanate, so it is said he is delighted with sultanate through the name. And in the formulation of the proof there is an admission of difference when it is said: glorification is for the essence of the Lord, not His name; and worship is for the essences of the idols, not their names. Rather, perhaps one claims that in these two verses there is evidence of difference, because the name is added to the Lord, and the names are made by their naming them, and they are made with the certainty that the individuals of the idols are not like that.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage provides the author's responses to the first two objections that were raised. The first objection was: if the name is not identical to the named, then how can God be eternally described as 'God' and 'Knower'? The answer: what is established in pre-eternity is the very meaning (of divinity, knowledge), not the word; the absence of the word does not imply absence of the meaning. The second objection concerned the verse 'Glorify the name of your Lord' – if glorification is for the essence, why mention the name? The author gives several interpretations: (1) 'glorifying the name' means sanctifying and exalting it from being applied to others, from being interpreted improperly, or from being mentioned without reverence. (2) It is a metonymy (kinaya) for glorifying the essence itself, analogous to saying 'Peace be upon the noble assembly' meaning those present. (3) The word 'name' is redundant (muqham), like in the poet's line 'Then the name of peace be upon you both.' Then he explains the verse about worshipping idols: they worship idols that have only the name of divinity, like someone who calls himself sultan without any real authority. The author notes that the very phrasing of the proof (the opponent's own argument) contains an admission of difference: it says 'glorification is for the essence of the Lord, not His name' and 'worship is for the essences of the idols, not their names' – this shows that the name and the named are considered different in the argument itself. He concludes that one might even claim that the two verses actually support difference (maghayara), because the name is added to the Lord (showing it is not identical), and the names are said to be 'what you have named' – showing they are created, unlike the idols themselves.

Key Terms
المعنى (al-ma'na)
الإلهية (al-ilahiyya)
الاسم (al-ism)
اللفظ (al-lafz)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if the name is not eternal, the attribute is not eternal.
Correction: The author clarifies that the meaning (attribute) is eternal, only the linguistic name is temporal.

Checkpoint
What does the author establish as eternal?
A) The word 'Allah'
B) The meaning of divinity
C) The letters
D) The Quran

Key Takeaway
The meaning (ma'na) is eternal; only the verbal name is temporal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah using His names, do you ever feel that the words themselves are insufficient? How does knowing that the meanings are eternal and real, while words are temporal, change your relationship with those names?
?
If Allah’s knowledge of you is eternal and existed before you were born, how should that affect your awareness of being constantly seen and known by Him? What does that demand from you today?
?
The passage shows that the absence of a word does not mean the absence of truth. Where in your life have you focused too much on labels or expressions and missed the deeper reality of your faith? What cost have you paid for that confusion?

Response to the Second Argument: Interpreting 'Glorify the Name'
Author gives the second answer with multiple interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various interpretations of 'Glorify the name of your Lord' and how they refute the claim that names are identical to the named.

Core Idea
'Glorifying the name' is either metonymy for glorifying the essence or a redundant expression.

The opponent cited Quranic verses commanding glorification of the 'name' of God to assert that the name is identical to the essence. The author provides multiple tafsir-based responses showing that the 'name' here is not meant literally.

Guided Path
1
Read the second answer and list the interpretations
Read the passage and identify the three interpretations given: sanctification, metonymy, and redundancy.
2
Understand the concept of kinaya
Kinaya (metonymy) is a figure of speech where something is referred to indirectly. Here, glorifying the name stands for glorifying the essence.
Example: 'Peace be upon the noble assembly' means peace upon the people.
3
Notice the admission of difference
The author points out that the opponent's own phrasing ('glorification for the essence of the Lord, not His name') implies a distinction between name and named.
Also, the idafa (addition) of 'name' to 'Lord' indicates difference.

Two Objections to the Distinction View and Their Refutation
Two objections and their refutation regarding names and named.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two key objections raised against the view that names are other than the named, and how Al-Sanusi responds by distinguishing between the word, its signification, and the named essence.

Core Idea
Names are not identical to the essence, but their meanings are distinct while the essence is one.

Al-Sanusi now addresses two powerful objections to the position that names are other than the named. The first objection focuses on the physical and linguistic properties of words versus the nature of meanings. The second objection invokes scripture: if God has many names but is one, how can names be distinct from the named? The answers refine the distinction and affirm that the multiplicity lies in the concepts, not the essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage to grasp the flow
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: two objections followed by an answer.
2
Understand the first objection
The first objection argues that names are words (accidents) with linguistic properties, while the named are meanings that cannot have those properties. How could they be identical?
Consider the contrast between physical words and abstract meanings.
3
Understand the second objection
The second objection quotes the Quran and hadith: God has many names but is one. If names were identical to the named, multiplicity would imply multiplicity in the essence.
This objection uses revelation against the distinction view.
4
Follow the response
The answer clarifies: the dispute is about the signification (madlul) of the word, not the word itself. Moreover, the named concepts (e.g., Knower, Powerful) are many, while the essence described by them is one.
The key is distinguishing between the essence and the attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عورض الوجهان لوجهين: الأول: أن الاسم لفظ وهو عرض غير باق ولا قائم بنفسه، متصف بأنه مركب من الحروف، وبأنه عجمي أو عربي ثلاثي أو رباعي والمسمى معنى لا يتصف بذلك وربما يكون جسماً قائماً بنفسه متصفاً بالألوان متمكناً في المكان إلى غير ذلك من الخواص فكيف يتحدان. الثاني: قوله تعالى ﴿والله الأسماء الحسنى فادعوه بها﴾ وقوله عليه السلام «إن الله تعالى تسعاً وتسعين اسماً» مع القطع بأن المسمى واحد لا تعدد فيه. وأجيب بأن النزاع ليس في نفس اللفظ بل مدلوله، ونحن إنما نعبر عن اللفظ بالتسمية، وإن كانت في اللغة فعل الواضع أو الذاكر، ثم لا ننكر إطلاق الاسم على التسمية كما في الآية والحديث على أن الحق أن المسميات أيضاً كثيرة، للقطع بأن مفهوم العالم غير مفهوم القادر، وكذا البواقي، وإنما الواحد هو الذات المتصف بالمسميات.
Then the two aspects were confronted with two aspects: The first: that the name is a word, and it is an accident not enduring and not subsisting by itself, characterized by being composed of letters, and by being non-Arabic or Arabic, triliteral or quadriliteral, while the named is a meaning that is not characterized by that, and perhaps it is a body subsisting by itself, characterized by colors, occupying place, and other properties—so how could they be identical? The second: His statement the Most High: 'And to God belong the Most Beautiful Names, so call upon Him by them,' and his statement, peace be upon him: 'God has ninety-nine names,' with the certainty that the named is one without multiplicity in it. And it was answered: that the dispute is not about the word itself but about its signification (madlul). And we only express the word by 'tasmia' (naming), even though in language it is the act of the assignor or the mentioner. Then we do not deny the application of the term 'name' to 'tasmia' as in the verse and the hadith. However, the truth is that the named (musammayat) are also many, because it is certain that the concept of 'Alim' (Knower) is not the concept of 'Qadir' (Powerful), and likewise the rest. Only the one is the essence described by these named (attributes).
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage presents two objections against the view that names are other than the named, followed by a response. The first objection is based on the nature of names: a name is a word, an accident (arad) that is not self-subsistent, composed of letters, and has linguistic properties (e.g., Arabic, triliteral). The named (musamma) is a meaning (ma'na) that does not have these properties; it might be a self-subsistent body with colors and location. How then can they be identical? The second objection cites the Quran and hadith: 'To God belong the Most Beautiful Names, so call upon Him by them,' and the hadith that God has ninety-nine names. Since the named (God) is one, there cannot be multiple names if they are identical to the named; but there are many names, so they must be different. The answer: The dispute is not about the literal word (lafz) but about its signification (madlul). The author clarifies that they use 'tasmia' (naming) for the word, but technically 'name' can be applied to the naming act. More importantly, the truth is that the named (musammayat) are also many: the concepts of 'Knower' and 'Powerful' are distinct; only the essence (dhat) that is described by these named attributes is one. So the multiplicity of names corresponds to the multiplicity of meanings (attributes), while the essence is one.

Key Terms
اللفظ (al-lafz: word)
العرض (al-arad: accident)
التسمية (al-tasmia: naming)
المفهوم (al-mafhum: concept)
الذات (al-dhat: essence)
المسمى (al-musamma: named)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith of ninety-nine names means that the names are identical to the essence, so multiplicity of names implies multiplicity in God.
Correction: The names refer to distinct concepts (e.g., Knower, Powerful); the essence is one but has many attributes.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the second objection, why does the multiplicity of God's names not imply a multiplicity in the essence?
A) Because the names are identical to the essence.
B) Because the names are the same in meaning.
C) Because the concepts of the names are distinct but they all refer to the one essence.
D) Because the hadith is not authentic.

Key Takeaway
God's essence is one, but His names signify distinct attributes and concepts, so multiplicity of names does not imply multiplicity in the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Ya Alim, Ya Qadir,' do you truly feel that you are addressing distinct aspects of Allah, or does your mind reduce them to a vague 'all-powerful'? How does this lesson challenge you to give each name its due reverence?
?
The objection about the hadith of ninety-nine names might have subtly planted doubt about God's oneness in your heart. After studying this passage, will you now approach those names with a clearer understanding, and if so, how will you change your dhikr or dua?
?
Your own concept of God: do you treat His attributes as real distinctions in your worship, or do you unconsciously merge them into a single notion? What practical step can you take today to honor the multiplicity of His names while affirming His unity?

Two Interpretations of 'سبح اسم ربك'
Two interpretations of a Qur'anic verse about glorification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two scholarly interpretations of the verse 'Glorify the name of your Lord' and their implications for the relationship between name and named.

Core Idea
The verse can mean glorifying the essence or treat 'name' as interpolated.

This lesson explores a key linguistic and theological discussion among Muslim scholars: how to understand the command to glorify the 'name' of Allah. The passage presents two distinct interpretations, each bearing on whether the name is identical to the named or distinct. By examining these views, you will grasp a foundational issue in divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully, noting the two interpretations introduced by 'ما في قولهم' and 'أو لفظ الاسم مقحم'.
The footnote (6) is part of the original text.
2
Identify the first interpretation
The first interpretation is that 'سبح اسم ربك' means glorification of the essence itself, not a separate name. The example of 'على المجلس الشريف' shows veneration of the entity directly.
This view treats 'اسم' as referring to the essence.
3
Identify the second interpretation
The second interpretation is that the word 'اسم' is extra or interpolated (مقحم), as in the poetic line 'ثم اسم السلام عليكما'. Here the word for peace is what is meant, not the word 'name'.
This view suggests 'اسم' is not meant to be taken literally.
4
Reflect on the implications
Both interpretations assume that the name and the named are not identical. One emphasizes glorification of the essence, the other treats 'name' as redundant. Consider how this supports the kalam distinction between the two.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تسبيح الذات كما في قولهم : سلام على المجلس الشريف، والجناب المنيف وفيه من التعظيم والإجلال ما لا يخفى (٦) ، أو لفظ الاسم مقحم كما في قول الشاعر : ثم اسم السلام عليكما . ومعنى عبادة الأسماء أنهم يعبدون الأصنام التي ليس فيها من الإلهية إلا مجرد الاسم. كمن سمى نفسه بالسلطان، وليست عنده آلات السلطنة وأسبابها ، فيقال إنه فرح من السلطنة بالاسم على أن في تقرير الاستدلال اعترافاً بالمغايرة حيث يقال التسبيح لذات الرب دون اسمه. والعبادة لذوات الأصنام دون أساميها، بل ربما يدعي أن في الآيتين دلالة على المغايرة حيث أضيف الاسم إلى الرب، وجعل الأسماء بتسميتهم، وجعلهم مع القطع بأن أشخاص الأصنام ليست كذلك
Glorification of the essence, as in their saying: 'Peace be upon the noble assembly and the lofty presence,' and in it is of veneration and exaltation what is not hidden (6), or the word 'ism' is interpolated as in the poet's saying: 'Then the name of peace be upon you both.' And the meaning of worshipping names is that they worship idols which have nothing of divinity except the mere name. Like one who names himself sultan but does not have the instruments and means of sultanate, so it is said he rejoiced from sultanate by the name. Moreover, in the formulation of the proof there is an admission of difference when it is said: glorification is for the essence of the Lord, not His name; and worship is for the essences of the idols, not their names. Rather, it may be claimed that in the two verses there is an indication of difference because the name is added to the Lord, and they made the names by their naming, and they made them, with the certainty that the individuals of the idols are not like that.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage presents evidence that the name (ism) is distinct from the named (musamma). The author begins by giving two interpretations of the verse 'سبح اسم ربك' (Glorify the name of your Lord). The first interpretation is that it means glorify the essence itself, using the phrase 'على المجلس الشريف' as an example of glorifying a noble entity. The second interpretation is that the word 'اسم' is extra, as in the poetic line where 'اسم السلام' is used. Then, the author explains the concept of 'عبادة الأسماء' (worship of names): idolaters worship idols that have nothing divine except the name, similar to someone who is called a sultan without having any actual power. This shows that the name and the named are different. Furthermore, the proof itself admits difference when it says glorification is for the essence of the Lord, not His name, and worship is for the essences of idols, not their names. Finally, it is claimed that the two verses indicate difference because the name is added to the Lord, and the idols are named by their namers, with the certainty that the idols themselves are not divine.

Key Terms
تسبيح
اسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the second interpretation of the verse 'سبح اسم ربك' mentioned in the passage?
A) It means to glorify the essence of the Lord.
B) The word 'ism' is interpolated and not meant literally.
C) It refers to the physical act of glorifying.
D) It means to glorify the name only.

Key Takeaway
Scholars differ on whether 'سبح اسم ربك' commands glorification of the essence or treats 'name' as an extra word, both views affirming a distinction between name and named.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'سبحان ربي العظيم' in prayer, do you consciously remember that you are glorifying Allah's essence, or has it become a mere repetition? How can you make this dhikr more present in your heart starting today?
?
The verse says 'سبح اسم ربك' and scholars differ on its meaning. Does this ambiguity trouble you, or does it deepen your reliance on scholarly interpretation? Are you willing to submit to the understanding of the Salaf even when you don't fully grasp it?
?
If the word 'اسم' in the verse could be considered extra, how careful must you be when using words to describe Allah? Have you ever spoken about Allah's attributes without realizing you were speaking about the created concept in your mind rather than the reality of Allah? Repent and learn.

Worshipping Names and the Name-Named Distinction
Explains worship of names and proves name-named distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of worshipping names and how it proves the distinction between the name and the named, as well as the evidence from the Qur'anic verses.

Core Idea
Worshipping idols by name alone shows names are not the same as the named.

This lesson delves into the theological concept of 'عبادة الأسماء' (worship of names), where idolaters worship entities that have only the name of divinity without actual divinity. The passage uses this example, along with a logical proof from the earlier verse, to demonstrate that the name and the named are distinct. It also points to a deeper Qur'anic indication of this difference.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully, noting the definition of worshipping names and the analogy of the false sultan.
2
Understand the concept of name-worship
The passage says idolaters worship idols that have nothing divine except the name. Like someone called sultan without power, they only have the name.
This highlights that the name is separate from the reality.
3
Analyze the proof of difference
The proof states: glorification is for the essence of the Lord, not His name; worship is for the essences of idols, not their names. This admission of difference is built into the very formulation.
The proof uses the same logic to separate name from named.
4
Examine the Qur'anic indication
The passage claims the two verses (about glorifying the Lord's name and the idols' names) indicate difference because 'اسم' is added to the Lord, while idols are named by their worshippers, showing the names are not identical to the entities.
This is a more subtle linguistic argument.

Two Objections Against Name-Named Identity and the Reply
Presents two objections against name-named identity and the reply.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two key objections against identifying the name with the named, and how the reply resolves them by distinguishing between the word, its meaning, and the divine essence.

Core Idea
Names are multiple, but the named essence is one.

This passage addresses a classic theological objection about Allah's names. Some argue that since words are accidents and names are many, they cannot be identical with the one divine essence. Sanusi presents these objections and their reply, clarifying the relationship between names, named attributes, and the single essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: two objections followed by a reply.
The passage is concise; pay attention to the connectors 'الأول' and 'الثاني'.
2
Identify the two objections
Find the first objection (about the word being an accident) and the second objection (multiplicity of names). Understand how each challenges the identity of name and named.
3
Understand the first objection
The first objection argues that the word (lafẓ) is an accident with linguistic properties, while the named (musammā) is often a substance with different properties, so they cannot be identical.
Consider why an accident and a substance cannot be the same thing.
4
Understand the second objection
The second objection quotes Quran and hadith mentioning many names for Allah, while the named (God) is one. How can multiple names be identical with one named?
5
Follow the reply and conclusion
The reply distinguishes between the word and its meaning, and clarifies that 'name' can refer to the act of naming. The real point: the named concepts (attributes) are many, but the essence is one. This preserves both multiplicity and unity.
The key is that the dispute is about meaning, not the word itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عورض الوجهان لوجهين : الأول : أن الاسم لفظ وهو عرض غير باق ولا قائم بنفسه، متصف بأنه مركب من الحروف، وبأنه عجمي أو عربي ثلاثي أو رباعي والمسمى معنى لا يتصف بذلك وربما يكون جسماً قائماً بنفسه متصفاً بالألوان متمكناً في المكان إلى غير ذلك من الخواص فكيف يتحدان . الثاني : قوله تعالى والله الأسماء الحسنى فادعوه بها (۲) وقوله عليه السلام إن الله تعالى تسعاً وتسعين اسماً» (۳) مع القطع بأن المسمى واحد لا تعدد فيه . وأجيب بأن النزاع ليس في نفس اللفظ بل مدلوله، ونحن إنما نعبر عن اللفظ بالتسمية، وإن كانت في اللغة فعل الواضع أو الذاكر، ثم لا ننكر إطلاق الاسم على التسمية كما في الآية ،والحديث على أن الحق أن المسميات أيضاً كثيرة، للقطع بأن مفهوم العالم غير مفهوم القادر ، وكذا البواقي، وإنما الواحد هو الذات المتصف بالمسميات .
Then the two aspects were objected to with two objections: The first: that the word is an accident, not enduring, not subsisting by itself, described as composed of letters, and as being non-Arabic or Arabic, triliteral or quadrilateral, while the named is a meaning not described by that, and perhaps it is a body subsisting by itself, described by colors, occupying space, and other properties, so how can they be identical? The second: His statement, exalted be He, 'And to Allah belong the best names, so call Him by them' (2) and his statement, peace be upon him, 'Allah has ninety-nine names' (3), with the certainty that the named is one, not multiple. And it was answered: that the dispute is not about the word itself but about its meaning. We only express the word by the naming, even if in language it is the act of the coiner or the rememberer. Then we do not deny applying the name to the naming as in the verse and hadith. In fact, the truth is that the named entities are also many, because the concept of 'knower' is not the concept of 'powerful,' and similarly the rest. The one is only the essence qualified by the named attributes.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage contains two objections against the view that the name and the named are identical. The first objection argues from the nature of words (alfāẓ) as accidents (a'rāḍ) that are composite, while the named entity (musammā) is often a substance (jawhar) with different properties; thus they cannot be united. The second objection points to the Quran and hadith that mention many names for Allah, while the named (God) is one. The answer explains that the dispute is about the meaning (madlūl) of the word, not the word itself. The word 'name' (ism) is used both for the word and for the act of naming (tasmiya), and in the Quran and hadith, 'name' sometimes refers to the act of naming. Moreover, the named concepts (e.g., knower, powerful) are indeed multiple, but they all qualify a single essence (dhāt). Hence, multiplicity of names does not mean multiplicity of the named essence.

Key Terms
اسم
مسمى
لفظ
تسمية
عرض
ذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that because Allah has many names, His essence is multiple.
Correction: The reply clarifies that the named concepts (attributes) are many, but the essence qualified by them is one.

Checkpoint

A) Because the name is a substance and the named is an accident.
B) Because the name is an accident with specific linguistic properties while the named is a meaning or body with different properties.
C) Because the name is eternal and the named is temporal.
D) Because there are multiple names but one named.

Key Takeaway
The multiplicity of Allah's names reflects multiple attributes, not multiple essences; the essence remains one.

Clarification of Proof Use by Both Parties in the Name-Named Debate
Responds to a critique that both parties misuse scriptural proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that both parties misuse scriptural proofs is answered, and how each group interprets key verses to support their position.

Core Idea
The debate is about referents of names, not the word 'name' itself.

This passage responds to a critique that both sides in the name-named debate are misusing Quranic verses. The author shows that the dispute is not about the word 'name' but about the individuals it signifies. He then clarifies how each group interprets specific texts to support their view—one taking 'name' to mean the divine essence, the other taking it to mean multiple attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Notice the structure: an objection, a refutation, then two interpretations.
The passage includes footnotes referencing verses and al-Razi.
2
Identify the objection
The passage begins with 'فإن قيل' stating that both parties' use of proofs is invalid because the dispute is about referents, not the word. Understand why this challenges both sides.
The objection argues that if the debate were about the word 'name' itself, the discussions about multiplicity would be meaningless.
3
See how al-Razi's view is refuted
The author claims that al-Razi's argument that the word 'name' is named by 'name' collapses, because here name and named are considered one. Note the dismissal of the need for a distinction between 'name' and 'named' as two individuals.
This refutation is tied to the nature of the dispute.
4
Compare the two interpretations
The first group interprets 'Glorify the name of your Lord' as intending the divine essence via the name. The second group interprets 'the best names' as multiple attributes distinct from the essence. Compare how each uses the same verse category.
Notice the first group faces the problem of the possessive construction.
5
Reflect on the possessive construction issue
The author notes that the first group's interpretation leads to an 'إشكال الإضافة' (problem of the possessive). Consider why adding 'of your Lord' to 'name' is problematic if 'name' means essence.
This is a subtle point that highlights the interpretive challenge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : تمسك الفريقين بالآيات والحديث مما لا يكاد يصح ، لأن النزاع ليس في اسم بل في إفراد مدلوله من مثل السماء والأرض والعالم والقادر والاسم والفعل وغير ذلك على ما يشهد به كلامهم ألا يرى أنه لو أريد الأول لما كان للقول بتعدد أسماء الله تعالى وانقسامها إلى ما هو عين أو غير أو لاعين ولا غير معنى وبهذا يسقط ما ذكره الإمام الرازي من أن لفظ الام أن لفظ الاسم مسمى بالاسم لا الفعل والحرف . فههنا الاسم والمسمى واحد ولا يحتاج إلى الجواب بأن الاسم هو لفظ الاسم من حيث إنه دال وموضوع ، والمسمى هو من حيث إنه مدلول وموضوع له بل فرد من أفراد الموضوع له فتغايرا(۱) قلنا : نعم إلا أن وجه تمسك الأولين أن في مثل سبح اسم ربك (٢) أريد بلفظ الاسم الذي هو (۳) من جملة الأسماء مسماه الذي هو اسم من أسماء الله تعالى، ثم أريد به مسماه الذي الذات الإلهية ) ، إلا أنه يرد إشكال الإضافة، ووجه تمسك الآخرين أن في قوله تعالى والله الأسماء الحسنى (ه) أريد بلفظ الأسماء مثل لفظ الرحمن والرحيم، والعليم، والقدير وغير ذلك مما هو غير لفظ الأسماء ، ثم إنها متعددة فيكون غير المسمى الذي هو ذات الواحد الحقيقي، الذي لا تعدد فيه أصلاً.
For it is said: The adherence of the two parties to the verses and hadith is hardly valid, because the dispute is not about a name but about the individuals of its meaning such as heaven, earth, world, powerful, name, verb, etc., as their statements witness. Do you not see that if the first were intended, there would be no meaning to the saying that the names of Allah are multiple and their division into what is identical, other, or neither identical nor other? And by this, what al-Rāzī mentioned falls, that the word 'name' is named by 'name,' not by the verb or particle. Here the name and the named are one, and there is no need for the answer that the name is the word 'name' in that it is indicative and coined, and the named is in that it is the meaning and what it is coined for; rather, they are two individuals of the coined-for, so they differ (1). We say: Yes, but the way the first group adheres [to the proof] is that in such as 'Glorify the name of your Lord' (2), the word 'name,' which is (3) one of the names, intends its named, which is a name from the names of Allah, and then intends by it its named, which is the divine essence ( ), except that the problem of the possessive construction arises. And the way the second group adheres is that in His statement 'And to Allah belong the best names' (5), the word 'names' intends such as 'the Merciful,' 'the Compassionate,' 'the Knower,' 'the Powerful,' etc., which are other than the word 'names.' Then they are multiple, so they are other than the named, which is the essence of the One, the Real, in which there is no multiplicity at all.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage addresses a further objection: the use of Quranic verses and hadith by both sides in the debate is problematic because the dispute is not about a particular name but about the referents (afrād madlūl) of the term. For example, if they were only discussing the word 'name' itself, the entire discussion about multiplicity and division would be meaningless. This also refutes al-Rāzī's claim that the word 'name' is itself named by 'name', and thus name and named are identical. The answer (by the author, likely al-Taftazānī) clarifies that each side interprets the texts differently. The first group (who hold name=essence) interpret 'Glorify the name of your Lord' as intending by 'name' the actual divine essence, though they admit the possessive construction is problematic. The second group (who hold name≠named) interpret 'the best names' as actual words like 'al-Raḥmān', which are multiple, and these are other than the one essence.

Correction: The text shows it is about the referents of specific names like 'the Knower', not just the word 'name'.

A) They take 'name' to mean the act of naming.
B) They take 'name' to mean the divine essence.
C) They take 'name' to mean any of Allah's attributes.

Key Takeaway
The debate over whether Allah's names are identical to His essence hinges on how each party interprets the referents of the names in scriptural texts.
