Objection and Response on Proof-Texts for 'Name' and 'Named'
Objection and response on proof-texts for name-named debate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that proof-texts are misapplied in the name-named debate and how the response reinterprets them to support both sides.

Core Idea
The dispute is about referents, not words, so proof-texts must be read accordingly.

This passage presents a key objection in the classical debate over whether God's names are identical with His essence or distinct. The objector argues that both sides misapply scripture because the core issue is not the word 'name' but what it refers to. The answer clarifies how each side legitimately uses the same verses to support their positions.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector claims that the dispute is about the referent (the thing named), not the name itself. Notice how he criticizes both groups for misusing verses.
He also dismisses al-Razi's point about the word 'name' being named by 'name'.
2
Identify the objector's conclusion
The objector says that if the dispute were about the word, the entire discussion about multiplicity and identity would be meaningless. He then rejects the common answer that separates the word from its referent.
3
Read the response
The response begins with 'Yes' (conceding the opponent's point that the dispute is not about the word), but then reinterprets the proof-texts. It shows how the first group uses 'Glorify the name of your Lord' to point to the divine essence, and the second group uses 'To God belong the most beautiful names' to show multiplicity.
4
Compare the two interpretations
Notice that both groups read the same verses but focus on different aspects: the first group sees unity, the second sees multiplicity. The response resolves the objection by shifting the debate to the level of intended referents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : تمسك الفريقين بالآيات والحديث مما لا يكاد يصح، لأن النزاع ليس في اسم بل في إفراد مدلوله من مثل السماء والأرض والعالم والقادر والاسم والفعل وغير ذلك على ما يشهد به كلامهم ألا يرى أنه لو أريد الأول لما كان للقول بتعدد أسماء الله تعالى وانقسامها إلى ما هو عين أو غير أو لاعين ولا غير معنى وبهذا يسقط ما ذكره الإمام الرازي من أن لفظ الاسم مسمى بالاسم لا الفعل والحرف. فههنا الاسم والمسمى واحد ولا يحتاج إلى الجواب بأن الاسم هو لفظ الاسم من حيث إنه دال وموضوع، والمسمى هو من حيث إنه مدلول وموضوع له بل فرد من أفراد الموضوع له فتغايرا.
قلنا : نعم إلا أن وجه تمسك الأولين أن في مثل سبح اسم ربك أريد بلفظ الاسم الذي هو من جملة الأسماء مسماه الذي هو اسم من أسماء الله تعالى، ثم أريد به مسماه الذي الذات الإلهية، إلا أنه يرد إشكال الإضافة، ووجه تمسك الآخرين أن في قوله تعالى والله الأسماء الحسنى أريد بلفظ الأسماء مثل لفظ الرحمن والرحيم، والعليم، والقدير وغير ذلك مما هو غير لفظ الأسماء، ثم إنها متعددة فيكون غير المسمى الذي هو ذات الواحد الحقيقي، الذي لا تعدد فيه أصلاً.
If it is said: The two groups' reliance on verses and hadith is hardly sound, because the dispute is not about a name but about the particularization of its referent from such things as sky, earth, world, powerful, name, action, and other things, as their speech testifies. Do you not see that if the former were intended, there would be no meaning to the statement about the multiplicity of God's names and their division into what is identical, or other, or neither identical nor other? And by this falls what Imam al-Razi mentioned, that the word 'name' is named by 'name', not by action or particle. So here the name and the named are one, and there is no need for the answer that the name is the word 'name' insofar as it is indicative and posited, and the named insofar as it is signified and posited for, but rather it is an individual of the individuals of the posited-for, so they are distinct.
We say: Yes, but the first group's reliance is that in such as 'Glorify the name of your Lord' (Q. 87:1), by the word 'name'—which is among the names—its named was intended, which is a name of God's names, then by it its named was intended, which is the divine essence, except that the problem of the construct phrase arises. And the other group's reliance is that in His saying 'And to God belong the most beautiful names' (Q. 7:180), by the word 'names' was intended such as the words 'the Most Merciful', 'the Merciful', 'the All-Knowing', 'the All-Powerful' and others, which are other than the word 'names'; then they are multiple, so they are other than the named which is the essence of the truly One, in whom there is no multiplicity at all.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair. The objector argues that neither side's proof-texts are valid because the dispute is not about the word 'name' but about how the referent (e.g., sky, earth, God's names, the divine essence) is related to the term. The objector claims that if the dispute were merely about the word, then discussions about God's names being identical or other would be meaningless. He then dismisses al-Razi's point about the word 'name' being a name for itself. He concludes that, taken in one sense, name and named are identical, and the common answer that distinguishes between the word and its referent is unnecessary because the named is just an instance of the posited-for, so they are distinct. The answer concedes that the dispute is not about the word, but explains the actual reliance: the first group (those who affirm identity) use verses like 'Glorify the name of your Lord' to mean the divine essence via the name, though the construct phrase creates a problem; the second group (those who distinguish) use verses like 'To God belong the most beautiful names' to show that the names (e.g., al-Rahman) are other than the unique divine essence.

Divine Names Are Based on Revelation
The author states the second investigation on divine names being tawfiqiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'ari school holds that divine names are based on revelation (tawfiqiyya) and how this differs from describing attributes.

Core Idea
Naming Allah is an act of authority; describing Him is an act of truthfulness.

This passage tackles a central debate in Islamic theology: can we invent names for God based on reason alone? The author, Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani, clarifies that naming (tasmia) is a practical matter requiring divine permission, unlike describing attributes, which is permissible as truthful report. The lesson unpacks the arguments and examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice the opposing views mentioned at the start.
Pay attention to the names of scholars and groups.
2
Identify the core distinction
Find the key distinction between naming (tasmia) and describing attributes (sifat). The passage says naming is a practical act, while attributes are reports.
This distinction is central to the argument.
3
Understand the argument
The passage argues that naming requires authority because it is an act of disposal in the named. Therefore, it is restricted to revelation. Contrast this with attributes, which are permissible as truthful statements.
4
Consider the examples
Reflect on why scholars like al-`Arif and al-Fatn hesitated due to fear of irreverence, and why it cannot be analogized to al-Harith and al-Zari`.
These examples illustrate the principle of reverence.
5
Reflect on the implications
Think about why even ordinary people would reject certain names for the Prophet, and what this teaches about the seriousness of naming in Islam.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني أسماء الله تعالى توفيقية خلافا للمعتزلة، والقاضي مطلقاً والغزالي في الصفات، وتوقف إمام الحرمين ومحل النزاع ما اتصف الباري بمعناه، ولم يرد إذن ولا منع به، ولا بمرادفه، وكان مشعراً بإجلال من غير وهم إخلال لنا أنه لا يجوز في حق النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ، بل لا يرتضيه آحاد الناس. قالوا شاع في سائر اللغات . قلنا غير محل النزاع . قال الإمام الحل والحرمة من أحكام الشرع فيتوقف على دليل شرعي وعلى عبرة بالقياس في الأسماء والصفات. :قلنا التسمية من العمليات. وقال الغزالي أجزاء الصفات إخبار بصفات مدلولاتها فيجوز بدلائل إباحة الصدق، بل استحبابه إلا لمانع بخلاف التسمية، فإنه تصرف في المسمى فلا يصلح إلا لمن له الولاية، وإنما لم يجزم مثل العارف والفطن لما فيه من وهم الإجلال، ولا يمثل الحارث والزارع بعدم الإجلال.
The Second Investigation: The names of Allah Most High are tawfiqiyya (based on revelation), contrary to the Mu'tazila, and Qadi absolutely, and al-Ghazali concerning attributes, and Imam al-Haramayn was hesitant. The point of dispute is what the Creator is described by in terms of meaning, and no permission or prohibition has come concerning it, nor its synonym, and it is indicative of reverence without implying deficiency in our view that it is not permissible with respect to the Prophet, peace be upon him, and indeed even ordinary people would not approve it. They said: It is common in all languages. We said: This is not the point of dispute. The Imam said: Permissibility and prohibition are among the rulings of the Law, so they depend on a legal proof and on considering analogy in names and attributes. We said: Naming is among practical matters. And al-Ghazali said: The parts of attributes are reports about the attributes of their objects, so they are permissible by proofs of permitting truthfulness, even recommended, except if there is an impediment, unlike naming, for it is an act of disposal concerning the named, so it is not suitable except for one who has authority; and those like al-`Arif and al-Fatn did not decide due to the illusion of reverence it contains, and it cannot be analogized to al-Harith and al-Zari` due to lack of reverence.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage defines the second investigation: whether divine names are tawfiqiyya (based on revelation) or can be rationally derived. It lists opposing views (Mu'tazila, Qadi, Ghazali on attributes, Imam al-Haramayn's hesitation). The point of dispute is clarified: names that describe God by meaning without revealed permission or prohibition. The author argues that naming is a practical act (من العمليات) requiring divine authority, whereas attributes are reports (إخبار) about qualities, so they are permissible as truthful statements. He also explains why scholars like al-`Arif and al-Fatn did not decide on certain names due to fear of implying irreverence, unlike examples like al-Harith and al-Zari`.

The Ash'ari Position on Divine Names
Al-Taftazani states the Ash'ari position and defines the point of dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the core Ash'ari position that divine names are tawqifiyyah (divinely instituted) and the precise scope of the disagreement with other schools.

Core Idea
Divine names are not invented by reason; they are exclusively revealed by Allah.

Imam al-Taftazani opens this chapter by stating the Ash'ari position that the names of Allah are tawqifiyyah—meaning they must be derived from revelation, not human reasoning. He immediately clarifies that this is not a blanket rule: the dispute concerns only those names that have a valid meaning, convey veneration, and have neither been explicitly permitted nor prohibited in the Quran and Sunnah. In this lesson, we will examine his precise formulation and the initial objection from linguistic usage.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and its translation. Notice how al-Taftazani begins by stating the position and then narrows down the point of dispute.
Pay attention to the phrase 'محل النزاع' (point of dispute) – it defines the exact scope of the disagreement.
2
Identify the three conditions for the dispute
The passage lists three conditions for a name to be in dispute: it must describe Allah by its meaning, no permission or prohibition has come for it, and it conveys veneration without implying deficiency. Find these in the text.
These conditions are crucial for understanding why some names are uncontroversial.
3
Understand the first objection and response
The Mu'tazila and others object that such names are widespread in all languages. Note al-Taftazani's concise response: 'That is not the point of dispute.' This shows the Ash'ari focus on the legal authority of revelation.
The objection assumes consensus, but the Ash'ari response dismisses it as irrelevant to the core issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني
أسماء الله تعالى توفيقية
قال (المبحث الثاني) المبحث الثاني أسماء الله تعالى توفيقية خلافا للمعتزلة، والقاضي مطلقاً والغزالي في الصفات، وتوقف إمام الحرمين ومحل النزاع ما اتصف الباري بمعناه، ولم يرد إذن ولا منع به، ولا بمرادفه، وكان مشعراً بإجلال من غير وهم إخلال لنا أنه لا يجوز في حق النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ، بل لا يرتضيه آحاد الناس. قالوا شاع في سائر اللغات . قلنا غير محل النزاع .
قال الإمام الحل والحرمة من أحكام الشرع فيتوقف على دليل شرعي وعلى عبرة بالقياس في الأسماء والصفات.
:قلنا التسمية من العمليات. وقال الغزالي أجزاء الصفات إخبار بصفات مدلولاتها فيجوز بدلائل إباحة الصدق، بل استحبابه إلا لمانع بخلاف التسمية، فإنه تصرف في المسمى فلا يصلح إلا لمن له الولاية، وإنما لم يجزم مثل العارف والفطن لما فيه من وهم الإجلال، ولا يمثل الحارث والزارع بعدم الإجلال.

لا خلاف في جواز إطلاق الأسماء والصفات على الباري تعالى إذا ورد إذن الشرع وعدم جوازه إذا ورد منعه وإنما الخلاف فيما لم يرد به إذن ولا منع ، وكان هو موصوفاً بمعناه ، ولم يكن إطلاقه عليه مما يستحيل في حقه، فعندنا لا يجوز، وعند المعتزلة يجوز ، وإليه مال القاضي أبو بكر منا ، وتوقف إمام الحرمين ، وفصل الإمام الغزالي رحمه الله فقال بجواز الصفة، وهو ما يدل على معنى زائد على الذات دون الاسم، وهو ما يدل علي نفس الذات، ويشكل هذا بمثل الإله اسماً للمعبود ، والكتاب اسماً للمكتوب، والرسم اسماً لما من العظام أي بلي، وب سماء الزمان والمكان والآلة ، ولعل المتكلم يلتزم كونها صفات، وإن كانت اسماً عند النحاه، وقد أورد تمام تحقيق الفرق في فوائد شرح الأصول لنا. أنه لا يجوز أن يسمى النبي بما ليس من أسمائه، بل لوسمي أسمائه ، بل لوسمي واحد من أفراد الناس بما لم يسمه به أبواه لما ارتضاه . فالباري تعالى وتقدس أولى. قالوا أهل كل لغة يسمونه باسم مختص بلغتهم كقولهم خذامي وتنكري ، وشاع ذلك وذاع من غير نكير وكان إجماعاً.
قلنا : كفى بالإجماع دليلاً على الاذن الشرعي، وهذا ما يقال انه لا خلاف فيما يرادف الأسماء الواردة في الشرع .
قال إمام الحرمين : معنى الجواز وعدمه الحل والحرمة، وكل منهما حكم شرعي لا يثبت إلا بدليل شرعي والقياس إنما يعتبر في العمليات دون الأسماء
The Second Chapter: The Names of Allah Most High Are Tawqifiyyah (Divinely Instituted). He said: (The Second Chapter) The second chapter: The names of Allah Most High are tawqifiyyah, contrary to the Mu'tazila, and to al-Qadi [Abu Bakr] absolutely, and to al-Ghazali concerning the attributes, and Imam al-Haramayn hesitated. The point of dispute is that which the Creator is described by its meaning, and no permission or prohibition has come concerning it, nor concerning its synonym, and it conveys veneration without the illusion of deficiency—according to us it is not permissible with respect to the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace; indeed, ordinary people would not approve of it. They said: It is widespread in all languages. We said: That is not the point of dispute. Imam [al-Haramayn] said: Permissibility and prohibition are rulings of the Law, so they depend on a legal proof, and analogical reasoning is not applicable in names and attributes. We said: Naming is among practical matters. And al-Ghazali said: The expression of attributes is informative about the attributes of their referents, so it is permissible—indeed recommended—by evidence of the permissibility of telling the truth, unless there is an impediment, unlike naming, for it is an act of disposing over the named, so it is not valid except for one who has authority. And he did not affirm such as 'al-'Arif' (the knowledgeable) and 'al-Fatin' (the intelligent) due to the illusion of veneration, nor does he cite 'al-Harith' (the plower) and 'al-Zari' (the sower) as examples lacking veneration. There is no disagreement concerning the permissibility of applying names and attributes to the Creator Most High if a legal permission has come, and concerning its impermissibility if a prohibition has come. The disagreement is only concerning that for which neither permission nor prohibition has come, and He is described by its meaning, and its application to Him is not impossible with respect to Him. According to us, it is not permissible; according to the Mu'tazila, it is permissible; al-Qadi Abu Bakr from our school inclined to that view; Imam al-Haramayn hesitated; and Imam al-Ghazali, may Allah have mercy on him, made a distinction, saying that the attribute (which indicates a meaning additional to the essence) is permissible, unlike the name (which indicates the essence itself). This is problematic regarding such as 'Ilah' (God) as a name for the worshipped, and 'Kitab' (Book) as a name for the written, and 'Rasm' (drawing/skeleton) as a name for what remains of bones—i.e., it is worn away—and the naming of time, place, and instrument. Perhaps the theologian commits to these being attributes, even though they are names according to grammarians. He has presented the complete verification of the difference in the benefits of our commentary on al-Usul. It is not permissible to name the Prophet by something not among his names; indeed, if a single individual among people were named by what his parents did not name him, it would not be approved. So the Creator, Most High and Exalted, is more deserving. They said: The people of every language name Him with a name specific to their language, like their saying 'Khodami' and 'Tankari', and this spread and became known without disapproval, and it was [a matter of] consensus. We said: Consensus suffices as evidence for legal permission. And this is what is said: There is no disagreement concerning that which is synonymous with the names that occur in the Law. Imam al-Haramayn said: The meaning of permissibility and impermissibility is lawful and unlawful, and both are legal rulings that are not established except by a legal proof, and analogical reasoning is considered only in practical matters, not in names.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage defines and defends the Ash'ari position that the names of Allah are tawqifiyyah—divinely instituted, meaning they cannot be invented by human reasoning but must be derived from revelation. The author begins by stating the chapter heading and then immediately identifies the opposing schools: the Mu'tazila, al-Qadi Abu Bakr (al-Baqillani) unconditionally, and al-Ghazali with respect to attributes, while Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni) suspended judgment. The point of dispute (mahall al-niza') is then clarified: it concerns a name whose meaning is truly applicable to Allah, for which no explicit permission or prohibition has come in revelation, nor has a synonym been revealed, and which conveys veneration without implying deficiency. The Ash'ari ruling is that such a name is not permissible—indeed, even ordinary humans would not allow being called by a name their parents did not give them, so how much more for Allah? An objection is raised: such names are widespread in all languages (e.g., the Persian words 'Khodami' and 'Tankari' for God). The response is that this is not the point of dispute, because such names were never used by early Muslims, and if there were consensus, it could be taken as evidence of implicit legal permission—but that would already place it outside the disputed category. The passage then reports that Imam al-Haramayn ruled that permissibility and prohibition are legal rulings requiring a legal proof, and that analogical reasoning (qiyas) is not valid in names and attributes. The author adds: 'We said: naming is among practical matters'—perhaps a rejoinder that qiyas does apply to practical rulings, but naming is not a practical ruling? Al-Ghazali's distinction is then presented: attributes (sifāt) are statements reporting a meaning additional to the essence, so their application is permissible (even recommended as truthful speech) unless there is a specific impediment; but names (asmā') are acts of designation over the named, so they require authority—only one with authority (i.e., Allah or His Messenger) can assign a name. Al-Ghazali refrained from applying names like al-'Arif and al-Fatin to Allah because they might create an illusion of veneration (wahm al-ijlāl) that is inappropriate. On page 344, the author reiterates that there is no disagreement on names explicitly permitted or prohibited; the disagreement is only over names in the middle category. He reports al-Ghazali's further distinction: a name indicates the essence itself, while an attribute indicates a meaning additional to the essence; thus attributes are permissible but names are not. He notes a difficulty: some words like 'Ilāh' (God) are names according to grammarians but might be considered attributes in this theological context. The author then draws an analogy: just as it is not permissible to give the Prophet a name not among his names, nor to call an ordinary person by a name his parents did not give him, so it is even more inappropriate for Allah. The objection from language usage is revived: people in every language use specific names for God; this was widespread and met with no disapproval, suggesting consensus. The response is that consensus itself provides evidence of legal permission—so if there truly is consensus, then those names have implicit permission and are thus not part of the disputed category. The passage concludes with Imam al-Haramayn's statement that permissibility and impermissibility are legal rulings that require a legal proof, and that qiyas applies only to practical matters, not to names.

Key Terms
أسماء الله تعالى
توفيقية
المعتزلة
محل النزاع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Ash'ari position applies to all names of Allah, even those in the Quran.
Correction: The passage explicitly limits the dispute to names not mentioned in revelation; names like 'al-Rahman' are agreed upon.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Ash'ari ruling on applying to Allah a name that has a valid meaning, conveys veneration, and has not been explicitly permitted or prohibited in revelation?
A) It is permissible.
B) It is not permissible.
C) It is recommended.
D) It is obligatory.

Key Takeaway
Divine names are tawqifiyyah: for names not revealed, the Ash'ari ruling is impermissibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah in your du'a, do you ever use words or descriptions that you have chosen yourself because they feel respectful? After reading this passage, how does the analogy with the Prophet (ﷺ) make you reconsider the importance of using only revealed names? Will you commit to verifying every name you use with the Quran and Sunnah?
?
The passage states that even ordinary people would not accept being called by a name their parents did not give them. How does this simple example expose the arrogance of inventing names for the Creator? What does your heart say about the necessity of divine authorization in how we address Allah?
?
If you have ever assumed that any respectful name for Allah is permissible as long as it is not degrading, how does the precise definition of the 'point of dispute' challenge that assumption? What specific step will you take today to ensure you are not guilty of assigning names to Allah without His permission?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
10 min
Legal Rulings and the Nature of Naming
Al-Taftazani presents Imam al-Haramayn's and al-Ghazali's arguments on the legal basis of naming.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the arguments that permissibility and prohibition are legal rulings requiring a legal proof, and al-Ghazali's distinction between names and attributes.

Core Idea
Naming is an act of authority, not mere description; only Allah or His Messenger can assign names.

After establishing the basic position, al-Taftazani reports two key arguments from Imam al-Haramayn and al-Ghazali. Imam al-Haramayn argues that permissibility and prohibition are legal rulings that can only be established by a legal proof, and analogical reasoning (qiyas) does not apply to names and attributes. Al-Ghazali, on the other hand, distinguishes between attributes (which are informative and thus permissible) and names (which are acts of designation requiring authority). This lesson unpacks these nuanced positions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on Imam al-Haramayn's argument
Find where Imam al-Haramayn says that permissibility and impermissibility are legal rulings that depend on a legal proof, and that qiyas does not apply to names and attributes.
This argument shifts the debate from rational permissibility to legal authority.
2
Analyze al-Ghazali's distinction
Al-Ghazali says that attributes (sifat) are reports about the essence and are permissible as truthful statements, while names (asma) are acts of disposing over the named and thus require authority. Notice how he uses the examples of 'al-'Arif' and 'al-Fatin' to illustrate the danger of illusion of veneration.
This distinction is subtle: grammatically, 'al-'Arif' is an attribute, but theologically, it might imply deficiency.
3
Understand the broader implication
The Ash'ari position is that only one with authority (Allah and His Messenger) can assign names to Allah. This reinforces the principle of tawqifiyyah.
This connects back to the core idea of the lesson.

المبحث الثاني
أسماء الله تعالى توفيقية
قال (المبحث الثاني) المبحث الثاني أسماء الله تعالى توفيقية خلافا للمعتزلة، والقاضي مطلقاً والغزالي في الصفات، وتوقف إمام الحرمين ومحل النزاع ما اتصف الباري بمعناه، ولم يرد إذن ولا منع به، ولا بمرادفه، وكان مشعراً بإجلال من غير وهم إخلال لنا أنه لا يجوز في حق النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ، بل لا يرتضيه آحاد الناس. قالوا شاع في سائر اللغات . قلنا غير محل النزاع .
قال الإمام الحل والحرمة من أحكام الشرع فيتوقف على دليل شرعي وعلى عبرة بالقياس في الأسماء والصفات.
:قلنا التسمية من العمليات. وقال الغزالي أجزاء الصفات إخبار بصفات مدلولاتها فيجوز بدلائل إباحة الصدق، بل استحبابه إلا لمانع بخلاف التسمية، فإنه تصرف في المسمى فلا يصلح إلا لمن له الولاية، وإنما لم يجزم مثل العارف والفطن لما فيه من وهم الإجلال، ولا يمثل الحارث والزارع بعدم الإجلال.

لا خلاف في جواز إطلاق الأسماء والصفات على الباري تعالى إذا ورد إذن الشرع وعدم جوازه إذا ورد منعه وإنما الخلاف فيما لم يرد به إذن ولا منع ، وكان هو موصوفاً بمعناه ، ولم يكن إطلاقه عليه مما يستحيل في حقه، فعندنا لا يجوز، وعند المعتزلة يجوز ، وإليه مال القاضي أبو بكر منا ، وتوقف إمام الحرمين ، وفصل الإمام الغزالي رحمه الله فقال بجواز الصفة، وهو ما يدل على معنى زائد على الذات دون الاسم، وهو ما يدل علي نفس الذات، ويشكل هذا بمثل الإله اسماً للمعبود ، والكتاب اسماً للمكتوب، والرسم اسماً لما من العظام أي بلي، وب سماء الزمان والمكان والآلة ، ولعل المتكلم يلتزم كونها صفات، وإن كانت اسماً عند النحاه، وقد أورد تمام تحقيق الفرق في فوائد شرح الأصول لنا. أنه لا يجوز أن يسمى النبي بما ليس من أسمائه، بل لوسمي أسمائه ، بل لوسمي واحد من أفراد الناس بما لم يسمه به أبواه لما ارتضاه . فالباري تعالى وتقدس أولى. قالوا أهل كل لغة يسمونه باسم مختص بلغتهم كقولهم خذامي وتنكري ، وشاع ذلك وذاع من غير نكير وكان إجماعاً.
قلنا : كفى بالإجماع دليلاً على الاذن الشرعي، وهذا ما يقال انه لا خلاف فيما يرادف الأسماء الواردة في الشرع .
قال إمام الحرمين : معنى الجواز وعدمه الحل والحرمة، وكل منهما حكم شرعي لا يثبت إلا بدليل شرعي والقياس إنما يعتبر في العمليات دون الأسماء
The Second Chapter: The Names of Allah Most High Are Tawqifiyyah (Divinely Instituted). According to al-Ghazali's argument in the passage, why is naming (tasmīyah) different from describing by attributes?

B) Attributes are more specific than names.

?

Divine Attributes

Refining the Dispute and Final Arguments
Al-Taftazani clarifies the dispute, presents the analogy with the Prophet, and addresses the consensus objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the final clarification of the disputed category, the analogy with naming the Prophet, and the role of consensus in providing legal permission.

Core Idea
The real disagreement is only about names not in revelation; consensus can serve as proof of divine permission.

In this concluding lesson, al-Taftazani reiterates the precise scope of the disagreement: there is no dispute over names explicitly permitted or prohibited. The controversy concerns the middle category. He then reinforces the Ash'ari position with an analogy: just as one cannot name the Prophet without authorization, so too with Allah. The objection from widespread language usage is met with a powerful response: if there is genuine consensus among Muslims, that itself constitutes evidence of legal permission. The passage ends with Imam al-Haramayn's restatement that permissibility and impermissibility are legal rulings requiring a legal proof, and qiyas is only for practical matters.

Guided Path
1
Review the clarification of the dispute
Read the part that says 'لا خلاف في جواز إطلاق الأسماء والصفات على الباري تعالى إذا ورد إذن الشرع وعدم جوازه إذا ورد منعه'. This shows that the dispute is only over the middle ground.
This clarifies that the Ash'ari position is not as extreme as sometimes portrayed.
2
Analyze the analogy with the Prophet
Al-Taftazani argues that just as it is impermissible to name the Prophet with a name not given by his parents, it is even more so for Allah. This analogy highlights the principle of divine authority.
This analogy is based on the idea that naming is an act of assignation, not description.
3
Understand the consensus argument
When objectors cite widespread usage in languages, the Ash'ari response is that if there is consensus, it serves as evidence of implicit legal permission—thus removing it from the disputed category.
This is a clever move: consensus is a valid source of law in Sunni Islam.
4
Final note on qiyas
Imam al-Haramayn's final statement reiterates that legal rulings require a legal proof and that qiyas is restricted to practical matters, not names.
This closes the argument by reaffirming the methodological principle.

:قلنا التسمية من العمليات. وقال الغزالي أجزاء الصفات إخبار بصفات مدلولاتها فيجوز بدلائل إباحة الصدق، بل استحبابه إلا لمانع بخلاف التسمية، فإنه تصرف في المسمى فلا يصلح إلا لمن له الولاية، وإنما لم يجزم مثل العارف والفطن لما فيه من وهم الإجلال، ولا يمثل الحارث والزارع بعدم الإجلال.

لا خلاف في جواز إطلاق الأسماء والصفات على الباري تعالى إذا ورد إذن الشرع وعدم جوازه إذا ورد منعه وإنما الخلاف فيما لم يرد به إذن ولا منع ، وكان هو موصوفاً بمعناه ، ولم يكن إطلاقه عليه مما يستحيل في حقه، فعندنا لا يجوز، وعند المعتزلة يجوز ، وإليه مال القاضي أبو بكر منا ، وتوقف إمام الحرمين ، وفصل الإمام الغزالي رحمه الله فقال بجواز الصفة، وهو ما يدل على معنى زائد على الذات دون الاسم، وهو ما يدل علي نفس الذات، ويشكل هذا بمثل الإله اسماً للمعبود ، والكتاب اسماً للمكتوب، والرسم اسماً لما من العظام أي بلي، وب سماء الزمان والمكان والآلة ، ولعل المتكلم يلتزم كونها صفات، وإن كانت اسماً عند النحاه، وقد أورد تمام تحقيق الفرق في فوائد شرح الأصول لنا. أنه لا يجوز أن يسمى النبي بما ليس من أسمائه، بل لوسمي أسمائه ، بل لوسمي واحد من أفراد الناس بما لم يسمه به أبواه لما ارتضاه . فالباري تعالى وتقدس أولى. قالوا أهل كل لغة يسمونه باسم مختص بلغتهم كقولهم خذامي وتنكري ، وشاع ذلك وذاع من غير نكير وكان إجماعاً.
قلنا : كفى بالإجماع دليلاً على الاذن الشرعي، وهذا ما يقال انه لا خلاف فيما يرادف الأسماء الواردة في الشرع .
قال إمام الحرمين : معنى الجواز وعدمه الحل والحرمة، وكل منهما حكم شرعي لا يثبت إلا بدليل شرعي والقياس إنما يعتبر في العمليات دون الأسماء
The Second Chapter: The Names of Allah Most High Are Tawqifiyyah (Divinely Instituted). He said: (The Second Chapter) The second chapter: The names of Allah Most High are tawqifiyyah, contrary to the Mu'tazila, and to al-Qadi [Abu Bakr] absolutely, and to al-Ghazali concerning the attributes, and Imam al-Haramayn hesitated. The point of dispute is that which the Creator is described by its meaning, and no permission or prohibition has come concerning it, nor concerning its synonym, and it conveys veneration without the illusion of deficiency—according to us it is not permissible with respect to the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace; indeed, ordinary people would not approve of it. They said: It is widespread in all languages. We said: That is not the point of dispute. Imam [al-Haramayn] said: Permissibility and prohibition are rulings of the Law, so they depend on a legal proof, and analogical reasoning is not applicable in names and attributes. We said: Naming is among practical matters. And al-Ghazali said: The expression of attributes is informative about the attributes of their referents, so it is permissible—indeed recommended—by evidence of the permissibility of telling the truth, unless there is an impediment, unlike naming, for it is an act of disposing over the named, so it is not valid except for one who has authority. And he did not affirm such as 'al-'Arif' (the knowledgeable) and 'al-Fatin' (the intelligent) due to the illusion of veneration, nor does he cite 'al-Harith' (the plower) and 'al-Zari' (the sower) as examples lacking veneration. There is no disagreement concerning the permissibility of applying names and attributes to the Creator Most High if a legal permission has come, and concerning its impermissibility if a prohibition has come. The disagreement is only concerning that for which neither permission nor prohibition has come, and He is described by its meaning, and its application to Him is not impossible with respect to Him. According to us, it is not permissible; according to the Mu'tazila, it is permissible; al-Qadi Abu Bakr from our school inclined to that view; Imam al-Haramayn hesitated; and Imam al-Ghazali, may Allah have mercy on him, made a distinction, saying that the attribute (which indicates a meaning additional to the essence) is permissible, unlike the name (which indicates the essence itself). This is problematic regarding such as 'Ilah' (God) as a name for the worshipped, and 'Kitab' (Book) as a name for the written, and 'Rasm' (drawing/skeleton) as a name for what remains of bones—i.e., it is worn away—and the naming of time, place, and instrument. Perhaps the theologian commits to these being attributes, even though they are names according to grammarians. He has presented the complete verification of the difference in the benefits of our commentary on al-Usul. It is not permissible to name the Prophet by something not among his names; indeed, if a single individual among people were named by what his parents did not name him, it would not be approved. So the Creator, Most High and Exalted, is more deserving. They said: The people of every language name Him with a name specific to their language, like their saying 'Khodami' and 'Tankari', and this spread and became known without disapproval, and it was [a matter of] consensus. We said: Consensus suffices as evidence for legal permission. And this is what is said: There is no disagreement concerning that which is synonymous with the names that occur in the Law. Imam al-Haramayn said: The meaning of permissibility and impermissibility is lawful and unlawful, and both are legal rulings that are not established except by a legal proof, and analogical reasoning is considered only in practical matters, not in names.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage defines and defends the Ash'ari position that the names of Allah are tawqifiyyah—divinely instituted, meaning they cannot be invented by human reasoning but must be derived from revelation. The author begins by stating the chapter heading and then immediately identifies the opposing schools: the Mu'tazila, al-Qadi Abu Bakr (al-Baqillani) unconditionally, and al-Ghazali with respect to attributes, while Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni) suspended judgment. The point of dispute (mahall al-niza') is then clarified: it concerns a name whose meaning is truly applicable to Allah, for which no explicit permission or prohibition has come in revelation, nor has a synonym been revealed, and which conveys veneration without implying deficiency. The Ash'ari ruling is that such a name is not permissible—indeed, even ordinary humans would not allow being called by a name their parents did not give them, so how much more for Allah? An objection is raised: such names are widespread in all languages (e.g., the Persian words 'Khodami' and 'Tankari' for God). The response is that this is not the point of dispute, because such names were never used by early Muslims, and if there were consensus, it could be taken as evidence of implicit legal permission—but that would already place it outside the disputed category. The passage then reports that Imam al-Haramayn ruled that permissibility and prohibition are legal rulings requiring a legal proof, and that analogical reasoning (qiyas) is not valid in names and attributes. The author adds: 'We said: naming is among practical matters'—perhaps a rejoinder that qiyas does apply to practical rulings, but naming is not a practical ruling? Al-Ghazali's distinction is then presented: attributes (sifāt) are statements reporting a meaning additional to the essence, so their application is permissible (even recommended as truthful speech) unless there is a specific impediment; but names (asmā') are acts of designation over the named, so they require authority—only one with authority (i.e., Allah or His Messenger) can assign a name. Al-Ghazali refrained from applying names like al-'Arif and al-Fatin to Allah because they might create an illusion of veneration (wahm al-ijlāl) that is inappropriate. On page 344, the author reiterates that there is no disagreement on names explicitly permitted or prohibited; the disagreement is only over names in the middle category. He reports al-Ghazali's further distinction: a name indicates the essence itself, while an attribute indicates a meaning additional to the essence; thus attributes are permissible but names are not. He notes a difficulty: some words like 'Ilāh' (God) are names according to grammarians but might be considered attributes in this theological context. The author then draws an analogy: just as it is not permissible to give the Prophet a name not among his names, nor to call an ordinary person by a name his parents did not give him, so it is even more inappropriate for Allah. The objection from language usage is revived: people in every language use specific names for God; this was widespread and met with no disapproval, suggesting consensus. The response is that consensus itself provides evidence of legal permission—so if there truly is consensus, then those names have implicit permission and are thus not part of the disputed category. The passage concludes with Imam al-Haramayn's statement that permissibility and impermissibility are legal rulings that require a legal proof, and that qiyas applies only to practical matters, not to names.

Key Terms
الإذن الشرعي
المنع
الإجماع
القياس
العمليات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any name used for Allah in any language is permissible without restriction.
Correction: The Ash'ari position holds that such names are only permissible if there is evidence of consensus (which implies divine permission); otherwise, they are not permissible.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does the Ash'ari respond to the objection that people in all languages have used specific names for God?
A) They deny that such usage exists.
B) They say it is irrelevant because the dispute is only about names not in revelation.
C) They claim that such usage is an innovation.
D) They argue that consensus itself constitutes evidence of legal permission.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position is that divine names require revelation or consensus; analogical reasoning cannot be used.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah with a name like 'The Powerful' or 'The Provider', do you ever pause to ask whether that name is explicitly taught in revelation, or do you assume it is fine as long as it seems appropriate? How can you verify?
?
The analogy with naming the Prophet shows that even a beloved figure cannot be called by a name he was not given. How does this shape your sense of adab (etiquette) toward Allah? Are you willing to abandon a name or description of Allah that you like because it lacks divine authorization?
?
In your daily life, do you rely on consensus of the community (e.g., common Islamic phrases) as sufficient proof for how you speak about Allah? What would you do if you discovered a common practice had no basis in revelation? Would you change your habit?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
8 min
The General Ruling on Naming God
The author states the ruling on using names and attributes for God and the scholarly disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the criteria for permissible and impermissible names and attributes for God and the scholarly disagreement over terms not mentioned in revelation.

Core Idea
Naming God requires a divine warrant, not human reasoning.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid addresses a critical question: May we call God by any name that conveys a true meaning, or must we restrict ourselves to revealed names? The author lays out the agreed-upon rule for names mentioned in revelation, then turns to the disputed category where reason and language seem to permit something but revelation is silent. The discussion reveals deep methodological commitments about the role of revelation versus reason in theology.

Guided Path
1
Understand the three categories
Read the passage and identify the three cases: (1) permissible with divine permission, (2) impermissible with divine prohibition, (3) disputed when there is no explicit ruling.
The author first establishes what is uncontested before entering the disagreement.
2
Map the scholarly positions
Notice the different views: Ash'ari (impermissible), Mu'tazila (permissible), al-Bāqillānī (inclined to permissibility), al-Juwaynī (suspended), and al-Ghazālī (distinguished between names and attributes).
Al-Ghazālī's distinction is nuanced: attributes may be permissible, but names are restricted to revelation.
3
Examine the supporting arguments
The author gives an analogy from naming the Prophet and common people, and addresses the counterexample of translations of God's names into other languages.
The response to the translation objection is that consensus itself provides the required legal permission.
4
Reflect on the methodological principle
Al-Juwaynī emphasizes that permissibility and prohibition are legal rulings, requiring a legal proof; analogical reasoning (qiyās) applies only to practical matters, not to names.
This highlights the priority of revelation over reason in matters of divine names.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا خلاف في جواز إطلاق الأسماء والصفات على الباري تعالى إذا ورد إذن الشرع وعدم جوازه إذا ورد منعه وإنما الخلاف فيما لم يرد به إذن ولا منع ، وكان هو موصوفاً بمعناه ، ولم يكن إطلاقه عليه مما يستحيل في حقه، فعندنا لا يجوز، وعند المعتزلة يجوز ، وإليه مال القاضي أبو بكر منا ، وتوقف إمام الحرمين ، وفصل الإمام الغزالي رحمه الله فقال بجواز الصفة، وهو ما يدل على معنى زائد على الذات دون الاسم، وهو ما يدل علي نفس الذات، ويشكل هذا بمثل الإله اسماً للمعبود ، والكتاب اسماً للمكتوب، والرسم اسماً لما من العظام أي بلي، وب سماء الزمان والمكان والآلة ، ولعل المتكلم يلتزم كونها صفات، وإن كانت اسماً عند النحاه، وقد أورد تمام تحقيق الفرق في فوائد شرح الأصول لنا. أنه لا يجوز أن يسمى النبي بما ليس من أسمائه، بل لوسمي أسمائه ، بل لوسمي واحد من أفراد الناس بما لم يسمه به أبواه لما ارتضاه . فالباري تعالى وتقدس أولى. قالوا أهل كل لغة يسمونه باسم مختص بلغتهم كقولهم خذامي وتنكري ، وشاع ذلك وذاع من غير نكير وكان إجماعاً. قلنا : كفى بالإجماع دليلاً على الاذن الشرعي، وهذا ما يقال انه لا خلاف فيما يرادف الأسماء الواردة في الشرع . قال إمام الحرمين : معنى الجواز وعدمه الحل والحرمة، وكل منهما حكم شرعي لا يثبت إلا بدليل شرعي والقياس إنما يعتبر في العمليات دون الأسماء
There is no disagreement regarding the permissibility of applying names and attributes to God the Most High when the permission of the Law (sharʿ) has come, and its impermissibility when its prohibition has come. Rather, the disagreement concerns what has not been permitted nor prohibited, while He is described by its meaning, and its application to Him is not impossible in His right. According to us (Ashʿarīs), it is not permissible. According to the Muʿtazila, it is permissible. Al-Qāḍī Abū Bakr (al-Bāqillānī) inclined to it from among us. Imām al-Ḥaramayn (al-Juwaynī) suspended judgment. Imām al-Ghazālī (may Allah have mercy on him) made a distinction, saying that the attribute (ṣifa) is permissible, which indicates an additional meaning beyond the essence, unlike the name (ism), which indicates the essence itself. This is problematic with examples like 'al-Ilāh' as a name for the worshipped, 'al-Kitāb' as a name for the written, 'al-Rasm' as a name for what is of bones, i.e., 'bala', and 'b-samā'' of time, place, and instrument. Perhaps the theologian (mutakallim) commits to them being attributes (ṣifāt), even if they are names according to grammarians. He has fully addressed the investigation of the difference in the benefits of our commentary on al-Uṣūl. That it is not permissible to name the Prophet with what is not among his names, nay, if he were named with his names, nay, if one individual among people were named with what his parents did not name him, they would not approve. So God the Most High and Exalted is more worthy. They said: The people of every language name Him with a name specific to their language, like their saying 'Khuḍāmī' and 'Tankirī', and this became widespread and common without denial, and it was a consensus. We said: Consensus suffices as evidence for legal permission. This is what is said: there is no disagreement regarding what is synonymous with the names occurring in the Law. Imām al-Ḥaramayn said: The meaning of permissibility and impermissibility is lawful (ḥalāl) and forbidden (ḥarām). Each of them is a legal ruling (ḥukm sharʿī) that is only established by a legal proof, and analogical reasoning (qiyās) is only considered in practical matters (ʿamaliyyāt), not in names.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage defines the rules for applying names and attributes to God. It begins by stating there is no disagreement that names and attributes mentioned in revelation are permissible, while those prohibited are not. The dispute concerns terms not mentioned in revelation but whose meaning is true of God and not impossible. The Ashʿarī position is that such terms are impermissible, while the Muʿtazila permit them. Al-Bāqillānī inclined to the Muʿtazila view, al-Juwaynī suspended judgment, and al-Ghazālī distinguished between attributes (which indicate an additional meaning) and names (which indicate the essence), permitting attributes but not names. The author notes difficulty with examples where terms like 'al-Ilāh' are considered names by grammarians but might be considered attributes. He then argues that just as one cannot name the Prophet with a name not given by revelation, nor name a common person with a name not given by his parents, so even more so for God. The objection that people of every language use names for God in their own language is answered by saying that consensus itself is evidence of legal permission. Finally, al-Juwaynī clarifies that permissibility and impermissibility are legal rulings that require a legal proof; analogical reasoning applies only to practical matters, not to names.

Key Terms
أسماء
صفات
الإذن الشرعي
الإجماع
القياس
الحل والحرمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The permissibility of using a name for God can be determined by reason alone, without reference to revelation or consensus.
Correction: The passage emphasizes that permissibility and impermissibility are legal rulings that require a legal proof; even when the meaning is true of God, the name is not automatically allowed unless there is divine warrant or consensus.

Checkpoint
According to the Ashʿarī position in this passage, what is the ruling on using a name or attribute for God that is not mentioned in revelation but whose meaning is true of Him?
A) It is permissible.
B) It is impermissible.
C) It depends on consensus.
D) It is obligatory.

Key Takeaway
Names and attributes for God require a divine warrant; where revelation is silent, Ashʿarīs prohibit such names, and analogical reasoning cannot be used to permit them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you describe Allah using words that sound good to you but are not from the Quran or Sunnah? Will you commit to only using His revealed names and attributes, starting today by learning one new name each day?
?
The analogy of naming a common person without parental permission is used. When you speak about Allah, do you guard your tongue as carefully as you would when speaking about a respected elder? What will you do this week to ensure your speech about Allah is based on revelation?
?
If you have ever assumed that a certain description of Allah is acceptable because it 'makes sense' to you, how will you now verify that assumption? Write down one description you use and check if it has a basis in revelation.
